THE RED PAGE .
HER GIFTS.
High grace, the dower of queens; and therewithal
Some wood-born wonder’s sweet simplicity ;
A glance like water brimming with the sky
Or byacintb-light where forest shadows fall;
Such thrilling pallor of cheek as doth enthral
The heart; a mouth whose passionate forms imply
All music and all silence held thereby ;
Deep golden locks, her sovereign coronal ;
A round reared neck, meet column of Love’s shrine
To cling to when the heart takes sanctuary :
Hands which for ever at Love’s bidding be,
And soft-stirred feet still answering to his sign:
These are her gifts, as tongue may tell them o’er.
Breathe low her name, my soul ; for that means more,
Dante Gabriel Rossetti.
4
When the steamship Austral left our shores for
England at the end of last month, she carried as
passengers a tired old man of 68 and a bright
young woman not long out of girlhood, yet
already fading in a feverish struggle with disease.
They were William Michael Rossetti and his
daughter Helen, vainly travelling to nullify the
results of developing the brain at the cost of the
body.
Some day, no doubt, intellectual humanity will
not need to bother about its sluggish liver or the
imperfect oxygenation of its blood. The race-
organism will gradually adapt itself to civilised
environment at a considerable expense of pangs
to weaker vessels. Meantime, it should never be
forgotten that the natural man is still little re-
moved from the savage man, who hunts his food
before he eats it, sleeps in the woods, drinks from
the streams, and stimulates his cortex to no ex-
tent worth mentioning. To live in cities, to wear
modern elothes, to lessen body-exercise, to in-
crease brain-work, to drink muxed liquors and
attend Masonic banquets—this is to ensure physi-
cal ruin, if not for one’s-self then for one’s
descendants. The inside of our platters remains
mostly barbarian, however admirably the outside
be barbered. So culture costs.
The family history of strenuous brain-workers
follows usually a regular course. In the first
notable generation some hand-laborer of excep-
tional physical energy lifts himself a little above
his fellows. His children—the second generation
—are educated to be “ something better ” than
their father. They inherit much of his energy,
yet are physically less vigorous, mentally more
complex, than he was—often men and women of
decided talent. A genius in the third generation,
with still less bodily strength, is occasionally to
be expected. There may be a fourth generation
of hootio weeds. There is never any fifth genera-
tion.
• * • *
The paternal grandfather of the Rossettis —
Maria, Dante Gabriel, William Michael, Chris-
tina—was a lusty Abruzzese Italian. Their
father, Gabriele, after fighting the Austrians with
pen and sword, became a professor of Italian
literature in England. He was clever, eccentrio,
and died at 71.
The maternal grandfather of the Rossettis was
an Italian man of letters who settled in England.
Their mother had literary tastes, and died at 85.
Maria Rossetti, born to a father 43 and. mother
25, was a brilliant, ‘ peculiar ’ woman who wrote
an introduction to Dante, and died at 49, un-
married.
Dante Gabriel, one year younger, remarkable
both as poet and painter, married at 32 a delicate,
imaginative milliner’s assistant, daughter of a
Sheffield cutler, was father next year of a still-
born child (his wife dying shortly after), and died
at 54.
William Michael, one year younger, in his
prime a very sound (not brilliant) critic, deficient
in the imaginative power of the others, reproduc-
ing the traits rather of his mother than of his
father, married in 1874, when 45, Lucy Madox
Brown, daughter of painter Ford Madox Brown
and author of a dull life of Mrs. Shelley.
Christina, one year younger, remarkable as a
poet, died at 64, unmarried.
In several aspects the third Rossetti generation
thus shows unmistakeable physical decadence,
accompanied by superior mental achievement.
• * . * •
Judged by their completed work, Dante Gabriel
is most notable of the Rossettis. His poems
would suffice to make him famous in the mental
history of the century, and as painter he almost
or quite equalled his results as poe*. Yet the
poet was greater than his poems, the painter than
his pictures. Nothing of his work, splendid as it
is in both his spheres of Art, so impressed his
friends as he himself impressed them. His most
brilliant performance seemed inadequate to his
more brilliant promise. And his brother, in edit-
ing his poems after his death, significantly placed
as conclusion and climax this couplet—
Who shall say what is said in me,
With all that I might have been dead in me f
» * * »
Dante Gabriel Rossetti had the temperament of
genius, but not its intensity : he was a furnace
lacking fire. In his youth, eye and voice and
contagious enthusiasm made his words alive : his
writings are mainly dead matter. There flashed
continually before him ‘ the light that never was
on sea or land,’and hardly could he succeed in
imaging one pale reflection upon paper. For want
of a few score red corpuscles, a volt or two of
nerve-force, a dozen quick throbs of vitality, this
first-rate man has left only a second-rate repu-
tation.
From two brain-fagged parents, as from two
fresh-brained parents, genius cannot spring. Had
Frances Polidori, the mother of the Rossettis,
been a buxom, uncultured country lass, or even
had their father been fifteen years younger, Dante
Gabriel might have rivalled Shakespeare. As it
is, he has fallen below Swinburne. But Swin-
burne’s father was a sailor. (Pity his mother was
bo healthy.)
[lf you would be a genius, choose a sailor for
your father, or a clergyman. Genius in the last
resort is simply vis vitas, animal heat, applied to
the right sort of brain : and sailors and clergy-
men, with comparatively torpid brains, have
bodies strong in vitality. It is static vitality,
which you can transmute into dynamic with
Nature’s approval, for Nature sleeps through the
night of one generation to wake up fresh and
lively in the morning of the next. That is why
(.ergymen’s sons—from Horatio Nelson to Stephen
Crane—are so full of energy : their brains have
just woke up.]
Besides the indirect evidence of his poems,
there is direot evidence of this lack of vitality in
Dante Rossetti. He himself said that at school
he was deficient in youth and physical courage,
and disinclined to take part in boyish games or
quarrels— ‘ not without generous impulse, but in
the main selfish of nature and reclusive in habit of
life.’ So Joan of Are, in her pathetic defence,
said that as a young girl, when her companions
were amusing themselves, ‘ I have neither sung
nor danced, but have made nosegays for the Holy
Virgin of Domremy.’ Later, Rossetti knew him-
self for a brooding, lonely, phantasy-swayed soul.
He is, he says in a sonnet addressed to his father,
of those that haunt
The vale of magical dark mysteries.
• * * »
Though they were separated by four centuries,
Joan of Arc was hardly more of a fleshly mystic
than Dante Rossetti. He was a curious throw-
back to medisevalism— ‘ by religious bias of
nature a Middle-Ages monk,’ says Hall Caine,
who knew him well; ‘superstitious in grain,’
says bis brother, ‘and anti-scientific to the
marrow.’ To steep one’s-self in Rossetti gives
precisely the feeling of going to church—not
the crude, ugly, Protestant church with the
serried pews and the bare walls, and the
business expectancy of the collection ; but a sen-
suous Catholic church, with twilight and tapers,
and old women kneeling in alcoves, and continual
faint music, and a slow mass intoning. Or at
Holyrood there are rooms in which an imaginative
person can linger behind the ruck of tourists, and
smell the musty-sweet odors of the tapestry, and
people the tarnished armor, and start at the
shadowed blood-stain from the orimson win-
dow, and shiver as Rizzio’s awful shriek
seems echoed along the corridors, and be called
to order by a crusty cicerone impatient of strag-
glers ; yet get far less value for his time and money
than if he had invested them in a copy of Ros-
setti’s poems—which, by the way, were not pur-
chaseable from any Sydney bookseller when his
brother was here—so little demand is there for
them, or so much, or so unmediseval are the book-
sellers.
* * * *
* Fleshly mystic ’ is, of course, a pleonastic
phrase, since all mystics are fleshly—as witness
the pains it costs them to subdue the flesh. St.
Antony may triumph over a score of naked
temptations, but only St. Antony and his
kindred are ever bothered with a score of naked
temptations. If religious ecstasy be the flower,
suppressed sex is the root.
Yet both ‘fleshly ’ and ‘ mystic’ are needed to
describe Rossetti; for in his youth he was more
mystio, in elder life more fleshly. Mother and
father blended almost equally in bin ; but, as he
lived, the more sober and English character of his
mother seemed to supplant, to some extent, the
strange Italian moods of his father. His earliest
work is his most mystical work : in many of the
‘ House of Life’ sonnets /which, on the whole,
represented his maturity, as ‘ The Blessed
Damozel ’ represented his youth) there is no trace
of the medisevalism attributed to him.
The fleshly side of Rossetti is little spoken of,
but it dominated all his days and ways. That
is why Robert Buchanan’s attack hit him so
hard : he felt it true in essence, though not in ex-
pression or inference. In‘Jenny’ he hints that
he sowed sex-oats—to reap, as he did reap, sore
eyes and melancholy.
• • • •
It seems decided nowadays that ‘The Quar-
terly ’ did not kill Keats, so D. G. Rossetti
remains the single authentic example of an
author slain by a review in four acts —insomnia,
chloral, paralysis, coffin. When Rossetti was on
his deathbed it apparently repented Buchanan of
what he had done, and he apologised and recanted
in the preface to ‘ God and the Man.’ Too late :
Rossetti died with half his music in him.
Robert Buchanan has labored much and credit-
ably in literature, yet all that he has done doubt-
fully suffices to blot out his crime of poet-murder.
For he killed Rossetti’s spirit as well as his body.
Rossetti was pre-eminently fitted to proclaim the
gospel of sensuous beauty refined to the pitch of
sublimity, and after his motives and character had
been so grossly and ignorantly vilified in ‘The
Fleshly School of Poetry ’ he never dared to give
his inspiration play. He smothered his sexuality
instead of putting it into his work—which was bad
both for toe man and the work.
Robert Buchanan passes for enlightened—but
can a Scotchman ever eradicate the hereditary
germ of Calvinism ? In literature and art he is
himself the strongest supporter of the theory that
he always makes his bride wait while he asks a
blessing : he has eternally the air of gazing at the
nude through his fingers, with half-averted eyes.
Anyhow, Buchanan used to be a jealous, ill-
conditioned fellow. When he quarrelled with
Labouchere, and was crushed by the might
of that terrible alliteration —‘He came to me a
hungry and scrofulous Scotchman. I gave him
food for his 6elly, and sulphur for his 6aek ’ —he
was an Ishmael in the community of authors.
Not purity, but envy, seemed the motive of his
attack on Rossetti. Whatever the motive, the
effeot was cruel.
* • * •
Rossetti should have been true to himself. He
should have accepted exile as Byron and Shelley
accepted exile, because they would not tether art
and life to hypocritical conventions. He chose to
remain and let the canker eat into his soul, to
brood over thwarted aims, and to conform his
passionate artistic impulses to the moral standard
of the middle-classes of England—of England,
where you find Bibles in hotel bedrooms and fifty
thousand women nightly tramping London pave-
ments. 5
It was partly, of course, lack of energy and lack
of commissions which made Rossetti a painter of
women’s beads, and left ‘ Dante’s Dream ’ his only
large completed picture. But if he had dared he
would have painted women in ‘ the altogether.’
Having sacrificed the undraped full-length in
deference to Galvinistic cant and Puritan preju-
dice, he laboured continually at faces hardly sur-
passed for character and colouring. He was par-
ticularly amorous of a long, slender throat —the
‘bird’s strained throat,’ or ‘neck’s inclining
flower-stem,’ as he phrases it. Considering his
temperament and his spoilt career, Rossetti has
left a wonderful quantity of good work in both
painting and poetry. And yet, as compared with
himself, all his and pictures were like im-
pressions in a copying-book—some strong, some
faint, but none so vivid as the original.
• • * •
NordfU says Rossetti was a degenerate, and
Nordau is right. To degeneracy we owe all that
is best in every field of artistic accomplishment.
From degeneracy—that is, from a departure
from the plane of sound physical health most
advantageous to the perpetuity of the race
—springs everything that most beautifully em-
broiders the coarse stuff of human existence.
Just as women carry children with nervous ten-
sion and trouble (so that ‘ every child means a
lost tooth,’ in the popular phrase), men and
women carry within them books and paintings
and statues and music at the cost of neurosis
corresponding to the difficulty of birth. ‘ When
I was working on the telegraph-line in Maori-
land,’ says Henry Lawson, ‘ I ate well, and slept
well, and felt well—but I could write nothing.
When my health goes, I get ideas.’ Leslie
Stephen was quioe wrong when he said the
highest poetry is the product of a thoroughly
healthy mind. The highest poetry is only
produced at a high cost of nervous energy—
that is to say, at the cost of degeneracy, tempo-
rary or permanent. Rossetti himself, to whom
(at all events in later years) a fine poem meant
utter physical prostration, has finely phrased the
truth—
By thine own tears thy song must tears beget,
O Singer ! Magic mirror thou hast none
Except thy manifest heart; and save thine own
Anguish or ardour, else no amulet.
Cisterned in Pride, verse is the feathery jet
Of soulless air-flung fountains ; nay, more dry
Than the Dead Sea for throats that thirst and sigh,
That song o’er which no singer’s lids grew wet.
The Song-god—He the Sun-god—is no slave
Of thine: thy Hunter he, who for thy soul
Fledges his shaft: to no august control
Of thy skilled hand his quivered store he gave :
But if thy lips’ loud cry leap to his smart,
The inspir’d recoil shall pierce thy brother’s heart.
And when Sydney Smith satirised Rogers’ slow
composition—‘ When Rogers produces a couplet
he goes to bed, and straw is laid down, and the
answer to enquiries is that Mr. Rogers is doing as
well as can be expected ’—he might really have
been paying Rogers a very high compliment.
• * * »
Rossetti’s medisevalism, in putting him out of
the modern sphere, puts him also below it. From
the complexity of modern aims and ideas we look
down to his fantastic but simple weavings and
embodiments as to some child’s play with dolls
and colored wools. It is interesting, even fascin-
ating : we dimly comprehend how real it is to the
child ; but it can never be real for us. It is
always a little quaint and childish and non-
existent.
Thus Rossetti’s was essentially a narrow field.
He took interest in few things keenly, and lacked,
as a rule, the power of inspired expression—not
brilliant expression, that is, but forcible-with-
thought expression. However clearly he imaged
—and conceivably his world of marvels was to
him very vivid —he rarely attained to express
anything like the picture he saw. That is, he
pictured externals admirably, but he failed to
communicate the spirit, the message of the scene
as he felt it.
There is consequently a certain aloofness, even
deadness, in nearly all Rossetti’s work. To re-
vert to a previous metaphor, a stranger in
Paris goes O-how-gratefully from the glare and
clamor of the Halles into the obscurity and quiet
of the church of St. Eustache. So soothing is it,
so refreshing, to sit while the strained nerves re-
lax and the thoughts recline on the sensuous
adjuncts of the place as on a mental couch.
But after a while the gloom oppresses, the
heavy atmosphere is breathed with difficulty.
With as great relief as you entered you pass out
again into the light and bustle. How sweet it is
to breathe fresh air ! how good to see the sun !
And, gazing at the turmoil of the street, you
reflect that after all, however crude, however
sordid, this is Life : the peace within the church
is the peace of mental death.
• » * *
Rossetti needed the woman-stimulus to wake
him to his best. He adored Danta largely because
Dante adored Beatrice. Like many others, he
gloated on Beatrice at second - hand. In his
brother’s edition o." his poems ‘Dante at Verona’
—without Beatrice—is followed by ‘ A Last Con-
fession ’ —a tale of murder for love cf a woman.
The first is one of Rossetti’s poorest poems: the
second one of his finest.
Take it cerebrally : so many nerve-cells, so
much electricity. Talk of Dante (without
Beatrice) and you put so many Rossetti cells in
connection, and release so much thought-lightning.
Bring in the woman, and your flash grows the
more vivid as fresh cells, highly-charged, add
their strength to the battery.
Rossetti’s most alive poetry is in his sonnets,
and the most alive of his sonnets had for sub-
ject or inspiration a woman—his wife. What
he wrote of her—or, to a leaser extent, of
women generally—he felt, and it shows the
feeling: much of the rest of his work reads
literary-exercisisb, as if the writer only half be-
lieved it. Generally speaking, Rossetti is grace-
ful without being tender, interesting without being
forcible, clever without being impressive. All
requisites for a poet he has in greater or less
degree—except the Breath of Life.
So throughout his work Rossetti reflected more
than he originated—was mirror rather than
source. Genius creates ideas as well as form :
talent generally stops at the form. There is sur-
prisingly little illumination in Rossetti’s work,
considering its volume : nearly always he is con-
tent to shuffle the old cards with a new and mar-
vellous dexterity.
• • • •
Rossetti’s phrasing counts for a great deal,
but the phrase is not the highest fruit of poetry.
He labored his words so much that he often
missed a possible message—polished caskets when
he should have been cutting gems. He used to go
and read romances of chivalry at the British
Museum, in order to find ‘stunning words for
poetry.’ The greatest poems could hardly be
written by a man so self-conscious, so deliberate.
Every writer strives to find a suitable phrase-
mate for his thought; but the French system does
not bring the best results, nor should the lady
take the initiative. ‘ Will you marry me ? ’ should
be said, not by phrase to thought, but by thought
to phrase.
Rossetti knew this well enough. ' Funda-
mental brain-work,’ he said, was the main
quality in a sonnet. Yet fundamental brain-
work was the very quality Rossetti laoked.
For pomp of sound and luxury of language his
sonnets are unrivalled ; but many are weak in
thought, and some weak in style and tawdry
with artifice. W. M. Rossetti has always shirked
criticising his brother’s work on the ground
that kin-comments are bound to be unsatisfactory.
As be had better taste, if less rhetoric, than Dante
Gabriel, one suspects another reason was that he
would have needed to show his love in Lordly
fashion, by chastening his brother vigorously.
• • • *
For, after all, measured by the highest standard,
Dante Rossetti’s conquests, glories, triumphs,
spoils of color and verbiage—positively mag-
nificent—shrink to comparatively little poetio
measure. Many of his poems are but corpses,
decked with flowers and swathed in odorous
cerements, yet white and chill upon their carven
bier. And with even the best of them comes
always a smell of the lamp—a censer-smell, if
you will—but not the smell of morning aiid
sweet earth. Rossetti was a literary Titan,
who piled phrase-Ossa upon Pelion, yet never
quite attained to scale Olympus. His poems
are a vast mausoleum glittering with gold
and gorgeous with fantastic decoration; and at
the centre . . . past all the silken corridors and
ivoried chambers ... a little ashes.
SONNET FOR A VENETIAN PASTORAL.
BY GIORGIONE. (IN THE ICUVRE.)
Water, for anguish of the solstice :—nay,
But dip the vessel slowly,—nay, but lean
And hark how at its verge the wave sighs in
Reluctant. Hush ! beyond all depth away
The heat lies silent at the brink of day:
Now the hand trails upon the viol-string
That sobs, and the brown faces cease to sing,
Sad with the whole of pleasure. Whither stray
Her eyes now, from whose mouth the slim pipes creep
And leave it pouting, while the shadowed grass
Is cool against her naked side ? Let be
Say nothing now unto her lest she weep,
Nor name this ever. Be it as it was,—
Life touching lips with Immortality.
Dante Gabriel Rosutli

UTILITIES, one who eschews everything but
what is strictly necessary. You’ve a penchant

see the occasion for bothering about PLUSH and
VELVET. Simple and good is your motto ;
you’re almost a Quaker, though not quite, and
you’d rather see your money in the bank than on
your back, and dividends are better, say you, than
Ribbons and Laces. Well, have your own way.
We provide for all sorts and sizes.
Or perhaps you are of another class. A world-
ling, if you will, floating down the stream of life,
with “youth at the prow and pleasure at the
helm,” you want to dress well and to look well.
The eyes of the fair are upon you, and you would
win their smiles. You want Clothes and Hats
and Boots, Gloves you must have, and a thousand
other things you crave for. Rings, Lockets,
Gems, exquisite Neckties, Shirts galore, good
CIGARS, and TOBACCO of the best. You’d
like them all, wouldn’t you ? You can have them,

what other stores are asking, a mere song.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN ACADEME.
My life is housed within grey walls of gloom
That I built high around it in past days ;
A spider-web is woven in a maze
About my heart, as round a rose in bloom.
Yet through the curtained windows of this room
Falls a soft flood of twilight-tinted rays ;
Through the closed door a dream of music strays
And night is languid with a bland perfume.
’Tis in my heart to mingle with the throng
Of dancers, swaying to their trodden rhyme ;
To drink of wine ; to join in jest or song
And the delicious riot of all time—
The flush of kisses, and delirious sighs
Of women, and the passion of their eyes.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.
SUNT LACHRYMAE RERUM.
Her hair wa's golden, curling wilfully ;
Her form was modelled most deliciously,
Well-poised and delicately garmented ;
Dainty from russet toe to saucy head —
Subtly alluring. Thus it seemed to me.
The smiling autumn sun sank brilliantly
In gleaming grandeur, exquisite to see.
I did not watch the cloud-flecks overhead —
Her hair was golden.
And after sunset came the moon, and she
(The moon) shone duly on our Arcady ;
And Pan had chuckled—only Pan is dead.
Ah, Love ! the cups we quaffed, the things we
said !
But now —I sit in ashes, wearily :
Her hair was golden !
E. MORTON.
BIRTH AND DEATH
Sadly wailing and crying,
Each soul of man is born ;
Shall we also, at our dying,
Go out with a cry forlorn ?
Nay ! weeping ’mid others’ mirth, we shall laugh
when others mourn.
Weep, O child, foreseeing
The struggle and stress of life ;
Sing, O man, when fleeing
From all the clamor and strife ;
Welcome Death as a friend, truer than brother
or wife.
Why should we yearn for Heaven
When weary of this sad Earth ?
Go we, with souls unshriven,
Where neither is grief nor mirth,
Excess of gloom or glory, nor second death or
birth.
Seek we no realm of darkness,
Nor land surpassing bright,
To stretch in staring starkness
Or soar in wild delight:
But a place of easeful silence, that knows nor day
nor night.
Sleeping and never waking
To toil of hand or brain ;
Never a new dawn breaking
To usher trouble or pain :
Never a priest to tell us —“ Ye must be born
again ! ”
Boon of loving and living,
Joy of action and thought,
Earning, spending and giving—
What are they? Less than nought.
What is the bliss of being to the bliss of being not?
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
TO-DAY.
Hear me now ! For Time is flying,
And the beating of his wings
Drowns the vows of love undying,
Dims the light where Memory clings ;
All the saddest songs of sorrow
Are the dirges of delay,
And our hearts may lose to-morrow
What our hands may hold to-day !
When a green grave by the river
Claims the last of love and you,
And Death’s hand has dried for ever
All our wreaths of rose and rue ;
When above your silent sleeping
Pitying pine-boughs moan and toss.
And the moonbeams, pale with weeping,
Fling their snow-white arms across ;
When the one star that was nearest
Dims and dies a world away,
How am I to tell you, dearest,
What you will not hear to-day ?
GLENROWAN.
HORSE-BELLS.
I love to lie and listen to the horse-bells’ merry
sound,
When the cattle are in camp, and we out-stretched
upon the ground ;
There is music in the horse-bells, and I love to
hear their song,
As they join in happy chorus—
Tinkle 1 Tonkle ! Tankle ! Tong !
They tonkle through the brigalow ; they tankle
near the swamp;
They tinkle on the ridges, and they wrangle round
the camp;
There’s little Fairy’s thimble-bell, and Billy s bull-
frog strong,
And big and little blend in
Tinkle 1 Tonkle! Tankle! Tong!
The great moon glares above ; in camp the cattle
rest content;
It is a pleasure just to live, as with the wattle
scent • ,
The soft breeze brings the music of the horse-bells
merry song,
Soothing ever and repeating
Tinkle ! Tonkle I Tankle ! Tong !
W.A.

\he publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
zrticle, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
my other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
' Correspondence " column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 24, 1897.
Humiliation and Prayer.
The drought has settled down heavily on
New South Wales in this autumn of 1897,
and, as usual, the Gon-fearing Ministry has
prescribed a day of humiliation and prayer
for rain. And the country—or, rather, a
very small part of it—has prayed in a small,
dispirited fashion, as one who has lost faith,
and never had any faith worth speaking of to
lose. Also, it has humiliated in moderation,
with its best clothes and its bell-topper on,
and its spotless silk umbrella in its hand.
In the old days, when men were profoundly
ignorant, and therefore full of faith and not
ashamed of their creed, they did such things
openly, so that they might induce others to
follow their example. If they made a special
appeal to heaven for deliverance from
drought or pestilence, or from an over-
whelming enemy, they walked in procession
in the streets, barefooted, scant of clothing,
with ashes and hair shirts, and tears and
banners and hysterics; and prostrated them-
selves in the dust, and publicly acknowledged
the God whom they believed in so profoundly
that they could wallow before Him without
feeling absolutely ridiculous. If humiliation
could bring rain, it probably came in those
days. Now, the rain, as a rule, doesn’t
come—possibly because the humiliation is
such a pathetic fraud and failure. The modern
Christian—-most of all the modern Protestant
Christian, for the Roman Catholic still dares
to kneel in public and kiss the Cardinal’s
ring is inwardly ashamed of the faith
which he alleges is in him. He professes
to have the same belief as people had
in olden times, but though he says that he
humiliates for rain he dare not do it openly,
lest the small boy in the street should tell
him to go and get his hair cut. His hope of
a heaven which is eternal is not strong
enough to run the gauntlet of the transient
hoodlum on earth. And he dare not even
humiliate to any extent indoors with his
fellow-believers, lest the matter should get
into the papers, or lest his fellow-believers
should laugh at him. or lest he should laugh
at himself. The Bulletin isn’t here argu-
ing the question whether faith, if there is
enough of it, will bring down rain or not.
The matter at issue for the moment is that
the alleged Christian, except- in a few pro-
foundly illiterate countries, hasn’t got the
faith, and is never likely to have it again.
* 9 * *
The theory of modern religion is that the
whole duration of this life—of everybody’s
life all combined—is so infinitesimal a speck
against the vastness of the life to come, that
nothing in this earth matters except faith.
The practice of modern life is that faith,
unless carefully concealed, is semi-lunacy and
hysteria. Therefore, if the shiny, bell-
toppered magnate who runs a wealthy bank
was found humiliating for rain, he would as-
suredly be sacked by the board. Also if the
boss of any well-established insurance office
in a large way of business became divinely
endowed with powers of prophecy, he would
certainly lose his appointment, unless he con-
cealed his gift very carefully indeed. Pray-
ing for rain is a survival of the old belief in
miracles. It implies that Providence runs
the world by a series of sudden jerky inter-
ferences with the customary order of things,
and the theory that an intangible influence
above sends rain by request when it isn’t
otherwise due is merely a piece of the old
belief that the said influence sends portents,
the gift of tongues, and prophetic powers,
and bestows the power of healing the sick
and raising the dead. The decently-edu-
cated Christian still professes to believe, in a
shadowy sort of fashion, that Providence
works miracles in the matter of rain ; but
when the man who heals the sick by the
laying on of hands turns up, which he does
frequently, the same believer generally de-
nounces him as an impostor. And it is worth
noting that he always proves to be an impos-
tor. As for the very occasional person who
raises the dead, he invariably proves to be
either a very bad impostor or a very shriek-
ful lunatic ; and the prophet is usually fined
55., on the information of Constable Mul-
holland, as a cheap and vulgar charlatan,
and every intelligent Christian endorses that
view of him.
* • * *
The limits of modern faith in the matter of
miracles, therefore, require a good deal of
definition. Practically they are somewhat
as follows : The Deity, who is in the habit of
governing this world by small acts of direct
interference, can bestow on anyone the
power of healing the incurable, or raising the
dead, or the gift of tongues, or of prophecy,
and used to do these things as matters of
daily occurrence. But it is so dead sure
that He does not do them now, even
in answer to the most earnest prayer,
that the subject is not worth talking
about. He can abolish sin, or put an end to
war, or bring about another deluge, or
visibly blast unbelievers with tire and brim-
stone, or make some specially chosen person
proof against death for countless centuries,
but He doesn’t. Also, He can send a
miraculous supply of rain—and does. The
sending of rain is the only miracle that
remains ; all the rest are dead and gone and
out of date and dropped into disuse. And
the trouble is that this is the one particular
miracle which cannot be proved or disproved.
Rain always comes sooner or later, with
prayer or without prayer, with much or
little faith or no faith at all. And when it
comes it is consequently impossible to prove
that it is or is not miraculous, or that this
particular rain wouldn’t have come in any
case. All the miracles which could be proved
have been abandoned as hopeless, and he
who asks for them is called either a fanatic
or a blasphemer, and he who professes to
work them is rightly designated a sham.
The raising of the dead would be a provable
miracle, for the dead don’t rise promiscu-
ously in any case, but though it is no more
difficult for Omnipotence to raise the dead
when they wouldn’t otherwise have risen
than to bring down rain when it wouldn’t
otherwise have fallen, this exhibition of
divine authority is never expected or
attempted. The striking of rocks to pro-
duce miraculous streams of water has also
gone out of fashion. So have the sacred
rivers, like Jordan on special occasions,
which cured the lame and leprous ; in fact,
every miracle that stood out clearly and
definitely and beyond mistake as such has
slumped beyond repair. Even the constant
prayer that the life of the Sovereign may be
prolonged isn’t expected to bring any answer
that can be demonstrated to be an answer ;
the most enthusiastic believer of the crowd
doesn’t profess to imagine that all the prayers
of all the churches in the British Empire
will keep Victoria alive even for another
century, brief and insignificant as a century
is. The rain miracle is absolutely the one
survival of the old faith, and why it has been
specially selected as a remnant, except by
reason of its haziness and uncertainty, no
one knows.
» « * «
Although the special interference in the
matter of rain is the one poor, weak wonder
which is alleged to have straggled down to
the present day, it is maintained, as forcibly
as ever, that all the other kinds used to
happen such a very long time ago that no
one can prove anything either for or against
their credibility. They stopped short about
the period when authentic history com-
menced, and just when there began to be
an independent record to check the state-
ments of the legendary priesthood. There
is a sharp line drawn somewhere across his-
tory ; on the far side of it everything is
believable deluges, strange fish whieb
swallowed prophets, giants who tore up lions
with their naked hands, people who walked
in furnaces and were not consumed, lepers
who were healed, corpses which came hack
to life, loaves which were constantly eaten
but were never finished, signs and wonders
and portents innumerable. On this side of
it, with one exception, lies nothing but the
ordinary grind of existence —the dull, grey
monotony of the explainable and the com-
monplace. Everything else, as soon as the
cold breath of incredulity blew upon it,
went out like the burnt candle that gutters
and vanishes into darkness. The improv-
able rain miracle has somehow straggled
across the border line and maintains its in-
congruous presence in this painfully modern
world like an old-time crusader in a recon-
structed bank, but it is the last remnant of
a faith in the supernatural which otherwise
petered out about the year 400. The church
has no belief in anything beyond it. Even
when, the other day, a cablegram announced
that an alleged Messiah had appeared in
Brazil, though the aggregate parsondom of
nearly half the earth professes to believe in
the second coming of the Messiah and His
possible arrival at any moment, not one
cleric of them all thought it worth while to
inquire if this might possibly be He. They
all believe in the second coming as a matter
of theory. They all disbelieve so utterly as
a matter of practice, that any one who calls
himself a Messiah is dismissed as an im-
postor and an impossibility simply because
he does so call himself.
* * ♦ *
The Bulletin is waiting to see parsondom
in general manifest some real faith in its own
creed. If it did manifest faith this paper
would probably disbelieve as utterly as it
does now in the clumsy theory that the In-
finite takes advice from the trivial, babel-
tongued tribes of man as to the best w-ay to
run the universe, but it would at least have
a shade more respect for the great aggregate
parson than it has at present. It is waiting
till the Church —if it really believes
that rain conies through praying-
gives up asking that one drought shall
break up, seeing that every drought breaks
up sooner or later, prayer or no prayer, and
that the effect of prayer is consequently un-
provable ; and asks instead that there be no
more drought in this arid land, and that the
desert shall become well-watered, and the
dry creeks shall run all the year round, and
the grass shall be perennially green out
where the dead men lie. It is quite as easy
for the Almighty to grant the one boon as
the other, and no boon—so the Scripture
alleges—is too large to ask, if there is only
faith enough behind it. The latter-day
materialist might not believe any the more
in the connection between rain and prayer
if the Church did this, but, if the Church
would stake its faith and its dogma on one
provable miracle, he would begin to have at
least a glimmer of belief that it had a faith
of some kind. Also, the Church professes
a belief in the virtue of humiliation, and the
world is waiting to see it humiliate. It pro-
fesses to imagine that abasement will move
the elements and alleviate a national calamity,
and the people who look on are waiting to
see a procession of pious business men do
the abasement by proceshing barefoot and in
sackcloth and ashes down the main street of
Melbourne or Sydney—or any other suitable
exhibition of the kind. The humiliation
that is expressed by wearing better clothes
and a newer and more gorgeous hat than
usual is not worth mentioning, and is wholly
bogus. As the Church doesn’t humiliate,
the only possible inference is that it has no
real faith in that nostrum. As it doesn’t
pray for any definite and convincing miracle,
the inference is that it has no belief worth
mentioning in the efficacy of prayer. And,
if it doesn’t believe in its own creed, what
does it believe in, or expect the world to
believe in 1

Mr. Ashton, M.P., Discourses on Equal State Repre-
sentation in the Federal Senate.
Dear Bulletin,— The question as to whether the small
state of Tasmania is to possess the same powers in the
Australasian Federation as the ten-times-more-populous
states of N.S. Wales and Victoria is one that will very-
soon bulk more largely in the eye of the people of Aus-
tralia than it did, for various reasons, during the recent
campaign. And as public attention becomes focussed
upon it there will probably be at least some mild surprise
occasioned by the striking affirmative unanimity which
prevails upon the point—not amongst the representatives
of the thinly-populated States, for there is no cause for
surprise in that but amongst the representatives of the
larger States, to whom the adoption of the Commonwealth
Bill’s proposals means a large sacrifice of independence. In
the U.S. Convention the delegates from the larger states
contended for proportional representation ; those from the
smaller states for equal representation. Equal represen-
tation is in the U.S. safeguarded and modified by a power
of veto being vested in the President, who stands almost
supreme as the embodiment of the popular will But
despite that safeguard, the concession asked for'by the
smaller States was made only after negotiation had'been
strained almost to breaking point. In Australia to dav
the position is so different as to suggest that w e are
much less insistent as to the rights of majorities
than the Americans were a century ago. I,?S of
having the concession forced from them as a necessary
sacrifice to expediency, nearly all the delegates of the
the States-N.S.W.-have agreed to give the
little States, even before they asked for it, all they could
possibly think of demanding. Moreover, some of the
most eminent of the delegates have elevated to the
dignity of a sacred principle a thing which history show s
clearly to have been a mere dernier restart, grudgingly
t 0 aS a l l uestlona ble expedient by people who
stood in momentary fear of hostile invas'ioi so long as
they remained disunited. And, to make matters stfll
worse, some of the delegates profess to treat the question
as a perfectly non-arguable one, and actually do treat the
people who dare to question the soundness’of the “ prin
ciple as persons whose lack of political culture prevents
their recognising that “equal representation » P Vw
tion. eßSential 0f the federal B >’ Btem as opposed to unifies*
* • »
But >r ,» „ f«W*l os.enti.l Thor* » rot „,„ „. %yt nf
consummating Australian union. One is by federating
the other by unifying the provinces. “Unification”
shortly put, means the merging of all the Australian par-
liaments into one big Central Parliament, and the conver-
sion of the various provinces into so many mere
municipalities, whose powers of self-go\ eminent would
be prescribed for them by the central parliament in the
same way as the N.S.W. municipalities to-day have their
“constitutions” made for them by the Parliament of
N.S.W. The powers and privileges of unified provinces
could be limited or destroyed by methods precisely
similar to those employed by N.S.W. Parliament when it
amends or repeals a Municipalities Act, and independent
provincial power would become in every detail a thing of
the past. “Federation' which is generally conceded to
be more suited to Australian requirements—means the
transference to the Federal Parliament of only those
matters in respect of which the people of Australia be-
lieve themselves to possess an absolute identity of in-
terest, irrespective of their place of residence, and the
retention by the “local legislatures” of absolutely
supreme and sovereign powers to deal with all questions
of legislation and administration not so transfered.
• » * * •
In this establishment of independent sovereignties—
one to deal with national matters and the other with
State or local concerns—is to be found the essential
difference between Federation and unification. An ideal
Federation, in short, may be defined as a form of union
which secures unity in national matters and perfect inde-
pendence in local matters. The more eminent of our
Federation advocates contend, however, that if a schtme
of union is to conform to true federal principles, the line
between it and unification must be much broader than
the one here drawn. Not only must the powers of the
Federal Parliament he strictiy defined and limited, but
according to them, even in the federal domain itxelf
dominant power must rent in the hands of a “ Majority
of States,” and not in those of a Majority of the People
In other words, they demand that those elements of
localism which the professed object of Federationists is
to get rid of, are to be kept alive in a form even more
dangerous to the interests of the nation than that in
which they at present exist !
• » * *
The temper of the present Convention seems to be in
favor of a substantial acceptance of the Commonwealth
Bill proposals. These are that the Lower House should
be elected on a population basis in a ratio of one member
to, say, 30,000 people (with a minimum of four represen-
tatives), while the Upper House shall consist of eight mem-
bers from each state, irrespective of its population. On
the above basis, the Lower House, on present popula-
tions, would he composed of 42 New South Welshmen,
40 Victorians, 17 South Australians, 4 Tasmanians, and 4
Westralians. The Senate will possess full powers of veto,
and, probably, full powers of amendment. Comparing
the position of the smaller Stati t in the Senate with t heir
position under a system of pro ortional representation,
as in the Lower House, it will be t?en that in the Upper
House Tasmania and West Aus: alia would have ten
times more power than their popu tion entitled them to,
and South Australia 2J times.
* * * *
The desire for “equal representation” springs, of
course, from a fear among the smaller States (according to
their prominent politicians) to trust themselves to the
tender mercies of the bigger ones. “ We comprise such
a small minority of the population,” they say, “ that we
cannot come into any scheme of union in which we might
be overwhelmed by the big majorities of the larger States.”
And the way suggested, not onlv by the small, but also by
the large, States, to get over this difficulty, is that the
majorities in the big States should allow themselves to be
“ overwhelmed ” by the minorities in the small States.
Certainly, nothing could well be simpler than this solu-
tion ; and, from the standpoint of the little States,
nothing more satisfactory. As Mr. Isaacs phrases it, the
small States “ asked for a shield, and are to be given a
sword. ”
* * * ft
The Federal Parliament, we will imagine, has been
established, and proceeds to deal with the fiscal question.
The Constitution will assuredly provide, as the Common-
wealth Bill provided, for the maintenance of pre-federa-
tion tariffs until the Federal Parliament has framed a
uniform tariff. It may be that, after Federation, the
smaller States become infected with a fear that the
uniform tariff adopted will detrimentally affect their
finances, and decide upon taking joint action against it.
The two larger States, on the other hand, remain true to
the federal spirit, and vote solid for a federal tariff. The
division in the House of Representatives show-s : For the
tariff, 82 : against, 25; majority in favor, 57. Then the
Bill goes to the Senate, and, the voting still following the
lines of State divisions, the figures are : For the tariff
Bill, lti ; against, 24 ; and the Bill which has been carried
in the Low er House by the representatives of 2£ millions
of people is thrown out in the Upper House by the repre-
sentatives of less than a quarter of the population of the
Federation ! Or, take a Colored Races Restriction Bill.
4 lctoria and N.S. Wales might vote solidly in favor of
stringent restriction, and Tasmania, W.A., and S.A.
against. In the Lower House the voting would be 82 to
25 in favor of restriction ; in the Upper House, 24 to 16
against. And the policy of Australia in relation to the
colored races of the world would be determined by the
voice of an insignificant fraction of the people, made
potent by the fact that they lived in a weak little state
and not di a hi# and powerful state —a prize political
paradox . Divorce laws, banking laws, and currency laws,
&c., all stand in the same beautiful position—liable to be
made for a whole nation by an insignificant fraction
thereof, or not made at all.
* * * *
But not only might there he combinations of small
States to effect general vetoes. The same combinations
might take place on matters of detail. Hobart, Perth,
and Adelaide might wish to be better fortified than their
resources up to date have permitted, and the representa-
tives of the provinces of which f hey are the capitals
might agree amongst themselves to secure the lion’s share
of defence expenditure. Their course is a plain one. By
24 votes to 16 in the Semite they ean veto any proposal
which has only a majority of people behind it, and keep
on vetoing and amending until the opposing majority, in
its utter helplessness, conies to its senses and concedes
w-hat the minority asks. There are those who contend
that the power r,o veto is not tantamount to a power to
legislate. The answer to that eontention may be fur-
nished by a simple illustration. Under our Constitution —
and the British no member of Parliament has a right to
propose taxation, unless his proposal be covered by a
“ message from the Crown.” The Crown is never ad-
vised by its responsible advisers to give that approval to
the proposal of a private member. The result in practice
is that all taxation proposals are made by Governments,
and the most a Legislative Assembly can do is to veto,
and keep on vetoing, until a proposal is brought forward
that meets with its approval. And yet it would hardly
be denied that the Legislative Assembly- “ legislates ” on
questions of taxation.
* » * «
Perhaps no better illustration of the dangers ahead
could he furnished than that provided by a cablegram
during the last few days, which informs us that owing to
the l .8. “ Jingo” senators being “ implacable and jealous
of the Kxecutive” they have killed the Arbitration Treaty
between the U.S. and Great Britain. The head of the
Kxecutive is the representative of the nation, and favors
peaceful arbitration taking the place of war. A majority
of the Senate, probably- representative of merely- a fraction
of the L.S. people, through “dislike” of Great Britain
and jealousy- of the Executive, prefers a continuation of
the system of arbitrament by Maxim gun. And the
minority “has it”—to use a future Federal Parliamentary
forniula. W hat would he thought of the work of the
framers of the Australian Constitution if some time in the
future a. majority of people expressed themselves in favor
of certain relations between Great Britain or some other
outside power, and they were frustrated in their object
by the representatives of a minority of people, resident
in & majority of States; If, under our Federation, there
be always perfect accord amongst the people of all the
States everything w-ill run smoothly. But if there be a
difference of opinion, then the minority of people who
live on the yellow, blue and grey portions of the map will
prevail over those w hose bad luck it is to constitute the
majority who reside on the green and white portions.
Surely-a color-blind constitution-builder would be worth
his weight in gold at the present moment.
* ♦ « »
Not only is there danger in a provision for giving a
lasmanian ten times the political power of a Cornstalk,
but unless the object lie to degrade the system of Austra-
lian union to the level of a mere League of States—with
no power, by the way, for any of them to secede —there
appears to he absolutely no necessity for it. The powers
°* « e e^era ' Parliament, it has been shown, will be
confined to questions of common interest to all Austra-
lians—questions with which it is agreed a Central Parlia-
ment may deal more effectively and satisfactorily than
could be the case if the questions were dealt with separ-
ately by distinct and independent legislatures A Federal


Supreme Court—as “ guardian of the Constitution ’’—will
prevent the Federal Parliament from infringing upon a
domain clearly defined by the Constitution Act; and
before the area of this domain can be extended the assent
of each and every state to the necessary amendment of
the Constitution will be requisite. Possessing sovereign
rights in respect of and under their own constitutions,
and parting only with powers which they themselves
affirm can be more advantageously exercised jointly than
severally, what valid reason for fear is there amongst the
people of any state who truly favor the Federation of the
Australias ?
There can be no true national unity except it be based
upon the principle of government by a majority of the
people governed, irrespective of the residence-places of
those who constitute that majority. To determine ques-
tions of national concern, after Federation, in the same
way as the question of the Convention’s meeting-place
was determined, viz., by treating the State and not the
voter as the supreme entity, is to keep alive those
elements of disunion which federalists say Federation is
to annihilate. At present a few people in any of the
Australias can do what they please, be their actions
bitterly' antagonistic to the wishes of a large majority
resident in the other provinces. Given “ equal represen-
tation of States ” in our Federal system, and a few Aus-
tralian people will still be able to act in a manner opposed
to the majority—with this important difference : They
can compel the majority to do the same thing.
* » * •
One N.S.W. delegate has said, both upon the platform
and in Convention, that the man who favors “ one man
one vote” cannot logically oppose “ one State one vote.”
A curious illustration, surely, of how even the utt.erer of
a common phrase may fail to comprehend its true mean-
ing ! In the old pre-reform days of England, there were
boroughs consisting of one elector which had the same
representation in the Parliament as large manufacturing
cities containing many thousands of voters ; and there is
a case on record where the elector of a single-vote con-
stituency, replying to an objection as to the class of
representatives he chose to select, observed that if people
didn’t hold their tongues he’d “ send a couple of sweeps
into the Commons.” This is the parallel of equal State
representation ; “ one man one vote” is its antithesis. It
is only necessary now for the States to be allowed to fix
their own franchise, and to allow senators to be chosen by
the Parliaments instead of by the people, and there will
be planted in the Constitution at its birth seeds the in-
evitable fruit of which is disruption and death. For
there will be no provision, be it remembered, for the peace-
ful secession of even a majority !
* * * •
The only true unionist is the man who approaches the
federal question from the standpoint of Australian nation-
hood, and the “ true inwardness ” of “equal representa-
tion ”isto be discovered only from that standpoint. No
one has any objection to the 100 residents of Tinpofc
Creek making their own roads and regulating their own
street traffic, nor to the thousands of city of Sydney
residents exercising the same powers. But—in the
interests of unity—there is an irresistibly obvious objec-
tion to the hundred Tinpot Creekers having an equal
power with the hundreds of thousands of Sydneyites in
the passing of a tariff for the whole province—or in the
determination of a divorce law—or in the apportionment
of defence expenditure—or in the exclusion of colored
races.
• • • •
And if, by any extraordinary process of political con-
juring, the four hundred thousand people of Sydney
could be induced to enter into such a form of “union,”
how long might it be expected to last ?
Sydney, April 2.
JAS. ASHYTON.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Border-Customs Trouble.
One particularly quaint result which the Con-
vention’s financial committee arrived at, in its
desire to divide the Federal surplus among the
states in a spirit of ghastly conscientiousness,
deserves a mention all to itself. The most
notable advantage of Federation, it has always
been understood, will be the abolition of border
tariffs, and border customs-houses and customs-
officers, but the trouble about the surplus seems
in a fair way to make this illusory. For the
first five years after the Federal tariff comes
into use, and the provincial tariffs go out, and
the border-duties are done away with, it is pro-
vided that each province is to be credited with
its own customs revenue, and debited with its
own separate expenditure, and get back its own
surplus. The difficulty is to find out what is
its own customs revenue. Goods for the
Riverina district of N.S.W. will often pay duty
at Melbourne, goods for the Barrier and Wim-
mera districts at Adelaide, and so on, and if the
revenue was credited to the state in which it
was originally collected, a lot of the N.S.W.
surplus would be handed back to Victoria and
S.A., and a lot of the Victorian surplus to S.A.
also. So, for the five years during which the
surplus arrangement lasts, it is proposed
to keep the customs - houses and the cus-
toms-officers on the borders, as at present.
They will collect no duties in cash, but
they will overhaul things in the old labori-
ous way, and carefully note how much
the duty would be if they did collect it, and
credit the province which receives the goods,
and debit the province which sends them, and
go through all the old formalities and be to a
great extent the same old trouble as heretofore.
Thus if S.A. imports a ton of foreign sugar,
duty £5, it will be credited with the £5 in the
first place with a view to the ultimate distribu-
tion of the surplus ; but if it sends half-a-ton to
Broken Hill and the other half to Dimboola,
then it will be carefully debited with the £5
again, and Vic. and N.S.W. will be credited
with £2 10s. each. There will be all the ex-
pense of collecting the border customs without
any border customs revenue. This expense and
worry and foolishness are the inevitable results
of the original clumsy idea of having any
Federal surplus at all. If the Federal Govern-
ment had taken over the customs, excise, public
debts, railways, and, if necessary to balance
matters, the education system, its revenue and
expenditure would have just about equalled each
other. There would have then been no surplus
to return—consequently, there would have been
no need to keep a vast array of absolutely unpro-
fitable customs officials in the border towns.
Also, there would have been no problem of
differential railway-rates, no Inter-State Rail-
way Commission, and no unfathomable difficulty
about how to divide the cost of establishing a
uniform railway gauge. When the financial
committee decided to shirk the unification of
the debts and the railways in order to save
trouble, it brought down on itself a dozen
worse troubles as a consequence —the surplus
difficulty and the border customs difficulty
which grows out of it, and the break of gauge
problem and the differential rate worry being a
few of them. The border customs difficulty,
by the way, will be even worse under Federa-
tion than it was under provincialism. Suppos-
ing, for instance, a Melbourne man imports
some American leaf tobacco and mixes it
with some Victorian ditto to make his pecu-
liar brand of plug, and he exports some
of the plug to N.S.W. Under the old
system the duty was so much per lb. on
the whole plug. Under the new one, N.S.W.
is to be credited with the duty on that part of
the plug which was imported, but it isn’t
credited with anything on the locally-grown
portion of the plug. Therefore a complete
history of the plug has to go along with it.
And in the case of every separate article which
is made partly out of local and partly out of
dutiable foreign produce, the same wildly-im-
possible analysis must be made if the surplus
is to be conscientiously divided. Also the
border customs people must be able to decide
whether, say, a pair of pants whose original
history is lost wore made of local cloth, or of
imported cloth locally made up, or were im-
ported in a made-up state. The compromise
effected by the finance committee looked reason-
able enough till it got down to details. Now it
looks about as unpromising as the search after
the fourth dimension.
Tlie Elephant and the Pin.
Sydney Telegraph is against the cheap and
simple idea of settling deadlocks in the Federal
Parliament by the two Houses sitting and vot-
ing as one—
Because a substantial minority of the House, added to a
majority of the Senate, might be strong enough to outvote
the House majority and its Senate friends. There does
not, therefore, seem to be any more suitable means of
finality than the Referendum.
A truly remarkable reason. Of course, if dead-
locks were settled by the two Houses sitting
and voting as one, the Senate would have a
chance of winning occasionally. If it is pro-
posed to fix up some form of settlement by
which the Senate will never have a chance of
winning, and the Lower House will carry its
point every time, then there is no need for any
mode of settlement at all; the same result
will be arrived at by enacting that the Lower
House shall always carry its point over the
Senate without discussion. Also, if the Senate
is never to have its way unless when it goes
exactly the same way as the Lower House,
there is no use for any Senate. The Bulletin
is a sincere advocate of the Referendum, yet it
believes that, as the sole means of settling dis-
agreements between the two Houses, the
Referendum will never answer. A popular
vote of all Australia will mean a month or two
of hard labor and the expenditure of a good
many thousands. If it is the only means of
settling deadlocks—if the popular Chamber
must either submit to the State Chamber all
the time or else go to a national vote on every
point of difference—then it will have to submit
as a rule for the simple reason that the popular
Chamber is also the one which has to find the
money, and the staggering bill for all these
appeals to the people will be its bill. Suppos-
ing a serious feud arises between the two
Houses some beautiful results may be expected
from the system advocated by the Telegraph.
The Referendum will be the weapon of the
People’s House against the House of States.
The People’s House has to pay for it.
Therefore, the House of States could
deliberately man down the popular Chamber
by creating deadlocks on every possible
point, and forcing one appeal to the
people after another until the ruinous cost of
the process, to say nothing of the interminable
delay, would make the People’s House prefer
to knock under in all save the most extreme
cases. What is wanted is a prompt and abso-
lutely inexpensive process of settling small
differences of opinion, leaving the Referendum
as a final appeal on really important matters,
and the Norwegian system whereby the two
Houses sit and vote together to decide points of
difference is good enough in ordinary cases.
The Referendum, it is true, can settle large
matters and small ones equally well, just as the
elephant can pick up a pin if need be. But the
practical aspect of the case is that it doesn’t
pay to keep an elephant merely for the purpose
of picking up pins one at a time. All the pins
he can pick up won’t pay for the keep of the
elephant.
Two Men in a Fix.
Premier Reid’s gentle crawl-down from his un-
alterable resolve to close N. S. W. Parliament dur-
ing his absence on all-fours in front of the blessed
Throne gets him, to some extent, out of a very
tight place. Also, it should be the signal for
Opposition-leader Lyne, if he knows when to
come in out of the wet, to do the most graceful
crawl-down that he is capable of from his own
rather awkward situation. The position as
regards N.S.WVs famous back-down Premier
divides itself naturally into three parts : (1) If
Reid goes to England at all, leaving Brunker
or somebody else as Acting-Premier, and
especially if he goes at the public expense as
anticipated, he makes a ghastly fool of himself.
He laid down the law in Dibbs’s case that no
politician should go to England taking the
Premiership with him ; that no Premier should
go away whooping loosely over the face of the
globe at the public expense ; and that there
couldn’t be any such person as an Acting-Pre-
mier, and he can’t go without thereby implying
either that he is doing a grievous wrong, or
that he was lying when he said that Dibbs did
a great wrong a few years ago. (2) If Reid
goes, and lets Parliament sit in his absence
with the aspiring Carruthers, and the feeble
Brunker and Garrard and the weak,
bloodless Gould in charge of things, he may
be interrupted on the way by an intima-
tion that he is Premier no longer, and then he
will lose his salary and his position and cut a
supremely ridiculous figure into the bargain.
Reid’s own arguments against the things he
now proposes to do were so powerful that if
Lyne throws them forcibly at the Government
in his absence it is just possible that it will
crumble straight oft. (3) If Reid shuts up
Parliament in his absence, then, in addition to
his other iniquities, he will keep back the
Federal movement for probably three months,
and probably wreck it altogether by the delay,
and he will do this solely for his own selfish
ends, and he will be known thereafter as the
man who sold the welfare of Australia merely
to ensure a few more months’ continuance of his
salary. Supposing Reid came back with an
order of knighthood glowing on his stomach, to
face this obloquy in addition to all the rest, his
billet, if there is any public spirit at all in
N.S.W., wouldn’t be worth a day’s purchase.
This is the situation as regards Reid, and it is
decidedly bad all round ; yet Lyne’s position
doesn’t amount to very much either. If Reid
leaves Parliament to get on with the Federa-
tion Bill in his absence, and Lyne interposes
with a motion of censure, and says all the hard
things about Reid which that absurd person
deserves, he must stop the most important
business which Australia ever had on hand in
order to do it. Supposing he could throw out
Reid, he must block Federation while the
motion of censure is fought over, and
afterwards while he forms a new Minis-
try and makes other necessary arrange-
ments, and thereby throw everything out
of gear for the sake of his own selfish
grab after office. Then Reid would
return to point a chubby finger of derision at
Premier Lyne as the man who undermined an
opponent in his absence, and hazarded and per-
haps wrecked the making of a nation merely
that he might hoist his own lengthy person into
office, and sold his country rather than lose a
chance to satisfy his own private grudge and
his private desire for a salary. Therefore, if
Lyne is well - advised he will express, in a
few concise words, his utter loathing of Reid’s
inconsistency and time-serving, but will pro-
mise, all the same, to raise no party disturb-
ances at all in his absence, provided Parliament
sticks to the business of pushing the Federa-
tion Bill through ; and to postpone what he has
to say until the Convention has got through
with its second meeting and Federation is either
assured or gone under for the time being. After
that Lyne, as the disinterested patriot who
sacrificed a great party opportunity for the sake
of his country, can rise and say a lot of things
with great effect.
Swindling* the Income-Tax-payer.
A little while ago The Bulletin pointed out
how N.S.W. Income Tax Commissioners were
wrongfully demanding money from people in re-
ceipt of an income from trustees which was under
the taxation limit. And in many cases the Com-
missioners take no notice of small deductions
allowed by law, appearing to deliberately cal-
culate on the improbability that any person,
however justly aggrieved, will take the
bother of appealing for the matter of a few
shillings. Now the Commissioners claim, from
many persons who paid tax on their incomes for
1895, a fresh amount on the ground that their
incomes for 1896 were larger than their incomes
for 1895. At the same time they claim this fresh
amount on the incomes for 1896 also, so that a
man who had a small income in the one year,
and a large income in the next, is taxed as if he
had a large income in both years. This is un-
just, and—in spirit, at all events—illegal.
Further, it happened that many persons in the
receipt of weekly incomes received only 51
weeks’ wages in 1895, because there were only
51 pay-days, and 53 weeks’wages in 1896, because
there were 53 pay-days. These persons proposed
to pay tax on the actual incomes received, but
the Commissioners demand that they shall pay as
if they had received 53 weeks’ wages in each
year. The methods of the Commissioners are
not the methods of business men or of honor-
able men. They are the methods of Polish
pawnbrokers or racecourse welshers. The
system of direct taxation is a good system, but
bad administration will damn the best system.
Doubtless many persons attempt to cheat the
Commissioners, but that is no reason why the
Commissioners should attempt to cheat any per-
son. IftheCommissionersare allowed tocontinue
as they are doing, the policy of direct taxation
will so stink in the nostrils of the people that
it will have to be repealed. The wealthy, who
are for the first time forced to pay in direct
proportion to their incomes, wish for nothing
better. Parliament must interfere to prevent
further mal-administration.
“Nil” (who is duly licensed to kill) to The
Bulletin, on the patent-inedicine business :
Re the Bunko-Bolus man. You say : “ The code agitates
itself to protect the public against its own weakness in
trusting to confidence-men . . . and the like, but the
patent-medicine man is free of all supervision.” (1).
When a man fails to pick the little three-spot, or keeps an
appointment with a gentleman who does not return with
his watch, &c., he freely admits that he was spoofed, and
wants the law to interfere. When the same man spends
£lO on a sure and certain remedy for those “symptoms,”
he is never swindled. There is only one thing no man
will admit he was a big enough fool to do, viz., to
spend money on bogus medicine and derive no benefit.
(2). Nature cures about 9999 - 236 of the ills that beer
and various things are responsible for, if left alone : also,
a large percentage of ditto if not too viciously interfered
with. Hence lots of people get well in spite of patent
medicines. But who is going to admit that he was suffi-
cient of an idiot to spend £lO on Dr. Peter Pip's patent
pimple pills and get worse on their account, or get better
without their aid ? (3) We are all about as much imbued
with a love of and a hankering after the marvellous and
the mysterious as the buck Kaffir from the wilds of Bang-
wan-boko or thereabouts. The average man’s ideas of his
works (he calls them his “ system ”) are about as clear as
his conception of bi-metallism. Hence ignorance—mys-
tery —faith—and cure (in a big majority of cases), and, of
course, it was Dr. Peter P.’s p.p.p.’s that worked it. (4)
The overwhelming mass of people believe in at least one
patent medicine, and their belief is no less should
it fail in their particular case. One of the best-
known members (N.S.W.) of the Federal Convention told
me many years ago, when he was in the Ministry,
that any such law as you suggest would be a gross in-
justice and an infringement of the liberty of the subject,
and that, personally, he believed quack doctors were no
more frauds than I was. Tub Bulletin (referring to
testimonials) : “Most of these letters are lies . . .. the
majority—were bought and paid for.” You may be right,
but I doubt it. I know dozens of men who are as keen as
brither Scots in business, and form the matrix frohr which
juries are drawn, who have each a pet patent medicine,
of the capabilities of which they would take their dying
oath. They would sign conscientiously a score of such
testimonials as you refer to. To wind up. The public
believe in Dr. Peter Pip’s preparations, and kill or cure
they are satisfied with the result, and thus a legislator,
who introduced a law such as you sketch, would be asked
to wait until someone oivnecl, let alone complained, that
there was any patent medicine swindle.
The latest impracticable philanthropist is one
Samuel Andrews, of Dunedin, who died leav-
ing property worth £3OO a year, the income
from which, as a token of good feeling towards
a bleak and soggy town, is to be spent on guns
and cartridges and giddy uniforms and flags
and frill for the Dunedin volunteers. The
deceased philanthropist almost always goes on
like this. If the lamented Samuel had left his
little income to the State as a sinking-fund to
ultimately pay off the public debt, it would not
have been much in itself, bur still it would
probably have led other philanthropists to
follow in his footsteps, and the result might be
something really valuable. Even if no one fol-
lowed his example, the deceased, having all
eternity before him, could afford to stand by
and watch his little £3OO a year starting out in
pursuit of a £45,000,000 debt, and gradually
overtaking it. That is the kind of private
philanthropy which these overburdened pro-
vinces are most in need of, and it is also the
kind which the deceased man of affluence never
goes in for. His general ambition is to found a
Chair of Music, or to endow some church which
believes in the damnation of unbaptised infants.
♦
G. H. Reid, having ascertained what the feel-
ing of the Convention was with regard to the
railways, said he would consent to a provision
prohibiting a province charging two rates for
the one class of goods over the same line—that,
and no more. It would effectively prevent pre-
ferential treatment, he said. But it would do
nothing of the kind ; and an instance is at
hand. South Australia, naturally anxious to
have the smelting of Broken Hill ores on her
seaboard, charges more than twice as much for
carrying bullion as for carrying crude ore, not-
withstanding that bullion is the more easily
handled. So, too, although hundreds of
trucks are run up from the ports to Broken
Hill empty, an almost prohibitive rate
is put upon coke. Mr. Reid’s proposal
would not in the least overcome this develop-
’ment of provincial railway management; yet
the effects of it were so serious to Broken Hill,
that at one time then-Premier Dibbs was
appealed to to impose an export duty upon
crude ore, in order to make the fight a fair one.
Later on, when smelting fumes began to kill
people, the agitation for the export duty ceased,
and S.A. was allowed to have the smelting
industry, fostered by these rates (differential,
preferential, or whatever you like to call them),
along with the victims which it makes.

How history is made ! The Montreal (Canada)
Weekly Witness tells its version of the recent
utter defeat of the Maoriland Cold Tea party,
when its Parliamentary force came down from
23 to 12, and its high priest Stout, gob in a bad
second behind Hutcheson, the ship-rigger :
Thirty-seven Prohibitory Alliance candidates were
elected to the New Zealand Legislature as a result of the
prohibition battle there in December on the same day
that the no-license plebiscite was taken. As we recently
noted, the temperance vote recorded had increased in
two and a half years from 49,000 to 96,000. This, how-
ever, was not the most significant outcome, for out of a
total of 70 seats only 29 candidates of the so-called
Liberty League were elected, as against 37 Alliance candi-
dates and four v;ho are not claimed by either side. Had
it not been for the over-confidence of a section of the
prohibitionists in one constituency (Wellington), who ran
an independent candidate and thus divided the temper-
ance vote, the Liberty League would have had another
defeat. The remarkable success of the Prohibitory
Alliance in the election of prohibition candidates to the
New Zealand Legislature is further emphasized by the
triumphant return of Sir Robert Stout, president of the
Alliance. The prohibitionists set out to elect a
majority to the new legislature, and at the same
time to outlaw the liquor traffic. They based their
hopes of success upon the phenomenal enthusi-
asm of the temperance meetings throughout the
colony, and upon the supplementary fact, which they
considered made complete triumph assured, that in New
Zealand women have the right of suffrage. The election
of thirty-seven of their candidates fully justified the ex-
pectations of the Alliance leaders and safeguarded the
temperance legislation of the past, which provides for a
plebiscite on the temperance question at every general
election until the liquor traffic shall have been voted out
of existence by a three-fifths majority or the law shall
have been changed. To Canadian prohibitionists such an
object lesson abounds with interest and instruction.
Now what Maoriland Cold Tea-ite was it who
stuffed the Canadian editor with all that
gorgeous Ananias ? And what a beautiful liar
he must be !
“ F. A. Hank ” to The Bulletin :
Frequently we read of city and country bakers being
mulet in heavy damages for selling light-weight bread.
To the reader the question often suggests itself —Why are
bakers always chosen by the police, or inspectors, or who-
ever the prosecutors may be, as the delinquents ? What
about the storekeepers ? Are they all honest ? Do they
never sell a pound of tea an ounce or two light-weight, or
containing an ounce or more of adulteration ? The
publican also: does he always tender the standard
nobbier of the whisky whose brand is on his bottle ?
Other petty tradesmen—butchers, ironmongers, etc.—
are they all honest ? One never hears of a butcher selling
a pound of steak weighing 140 z., or an ironmonger whose
71b. packets of nails weigh just 6|lb. But these are what
might be termed retail thieves. What about those big
concerns which buy of the producer by weight? Are
their weights never tested and found wanting? Is there
no inspector to see the farmer’s corn weighed by agents ?
Invariably an agent returns to the grower an average of
four bushels and a fraction, eitffer over or under, to the
bag —no matter what sized bag, or what weight
corn. The meat co’s who buy by the 1001 b. dead weight
—are their weights always correct? One neyer hears
of them being challenged. Sugar, butter, and other com-
panies who buy by weight or measure - are their weights
and measures always correct ? What if an inspector hap-
pened along one fine day with correct standard weights,
and weighed 100 bullocks hanging on the balks at some
meat-works, and found, on comparing the works’ weight
with his own, a shortage of perhaps 501 b. a carcase?
Would the co. be allowed to adjust its weights, or be
compelled to suffer the penalty at the same ratio-as that
charged the baker for his little bit of dishonesty ? In the
latter event a very big reserve-fund would be required to
meet the exigency. If the officials who are appointed to
enforce this Act were to carry it out in its entirety, and
not make fish of one and flesh of another, a great deal of
the widespread dissatisfaction which now exists would be
abolished, and the dishonest baker and butcher and the
wholesale as well as the retail thief would share and share
alike.
A Hymn in Time of Jubilee.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To the kings of rags and tatters shall we crook
a craven knee ?
To the calves of Gold and Silver shall our
slavish homage be ?
Shall we kiss the clay-foot idol,
Meekly brook the bit and bridle,
Bow the head in House of Rimmon as the
nidd’rings bowed before
For a gaud the birthright barter won by hero-
blood of yore ?
Nay ! to Regal bands of Rapine may no free-
man honor bring,
And our knees know naught of rev’rence for the
Baubles on the Thing,
But, where Man is truly royal,
There, we bend us duly loyal.
To the Noble of God’s making, lo ! our plaudits
rise and ring,
And we greet him when we meet him with a
joyous “ Live the King ! ”
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.
