Her Majesty’s (Sydney) re-opened on
Saturday, after a week’s silence, with the
“Prisoner of Zenda.” When this regal drama
made its first Australian appearance, in Melbourne,
a few weeks ago, The Bulletin offered up a pro-
found remark to the effect that Anthony Hope’s
brilliant novel is not a production which drama-
tises easily, and renewed observation confirms this
opinion. Quite a lot of picturesque incidents have
had to be dropped out of the original story, as not
adapted for stage treatment. Rassendyll and his
double, the dnnky king, don’t meet, because
they are both represen the same individual,
therefore everything exdSpt the last act of that
disastrous royal drunk has to be left to the imagi-
nation. The gorgeous procession which escorts
the sham king into Strelsau—the real king being
locked up in a cellar at the time—when the royal
understudy enters his capital in state, half full of
glqry and the other half of dread lest he be bowled
out*and have bricks thrown at him by his loyal sub-
jeets,isalso omitted, processions being slow and un-
manageable and taking up too much room. The
coronation, as a ceremony which occupies much
time and affords but little thrill, is likewise
omitted. Rupert Hentzau doesn’t lay out the
bogus monarch; the play has to move rapidly and
can’t three weeks for any shattered poten-
tate to recover in. The midnight adventure in
the park is dropped as a thrilling scramble which
leads to nothing. And the storming of the Castle
of Zenda can’t be staged as Anthony Hope de-
picted it, inasmuch as it was a generous, whole-
souled shitfdy which raged simultaneously all over
the vast building, and was also carried on in the
moat outside, and on the bridge, and about the
adjacent forest, and there was a gory struggle on
the roof, and another in the hall, and a chewing-
ma|ch in the cellar all in progress at once. That
wholesale cat-fight couldn’t be adequately placed
on any stage. When all these necessary omissions
are made, there is more reposeful drama and
delineation of character and peaceful conversa-
tion, and much less whoop and blood and battle-
axe than might be expected.
» • • •
Sfcill there is a certain amount of very
commendable pageant in the play; the gilded
halls of monarchy have a lot to recommend them,
and the nobility of Ruritania, with all its best
clothes on, is really gorgeous. The feminine part
of the nobility is specially recommended as
being young and lovely and calculated to be
kissed at first sight, and the local military
imposing spectacle. The main features
of the plot are all there. That dissipated
reveller, Ihe Crown Prince of Ruritania, gets
drunk and drugged and incapable on the eve
of his coronation, and, his throne being shaky at
the best, his English cousin duly personates him
in order to keep brother Michael from rushing the
crown. And then, the monarch having disap-
peared altogether out of the cellar where he was
deposited during the coronation, the English
eousin has to keep the throne till further orders,
and to hug the king’s best girl to avert suspicion.
Finally the monarch turns up again, and the
cousin gives up the throne and the best girl and
the palace, and goes away, with a straw hat on in
place @f a crown, back to his original obscurity.
It is a pathetic*~essay on the foolishness of not
hanging on to a throne when you have got one,
and ofmaking yourself a sacrifice for any beery
monarch that you never saw before and are never
likely to see again.
• » * *
The new co. at Her Majesty’s has no very
strong points and only one or two weak ones. It
is a good, respectable, all-round combination.
Gaston Mervale, as Black Michael, leaves a good-
sized impression behind him as a villain of impos-
ing mien. Julius Knight’s king is eminently
respectable. He wears the gorgeous togs of
monarchy as one born to purple and giddy mil-
linery, and makes love to the real king’s best girl
in a fashion which ought to be kingly if it isn’t,
and when he creates Mario Majeronia K.G.M.G.,
or something of the sort, on the -first day of his
sovereignty he does it well. Also, he utters in a
convincing fashion all the noble sentiments which
the sham king gives way to while the real king is
away drunk; and withal he leaves behind
him an impression that he might be rather
more convincing than he is. The one man
who is an absolutely unqualified success is
Hawtrey as Colonel Sapt, the masterful old
veteran who runs the conspiracy and puts his
grizzled head in the lion’s mouth for an ungrate-
ful master. Carvill also makes a dashing, bri-
gandish Rupert Heotzau, and the ladies of the co.
are, on the whole, satisfactory. Miss Ada Ferrar
wears the red hair of the Elphbergs over a pretty
face and a warm heart. Miss Efljott Page fills
satisfactorily an awkward part which has suffered
a great deal in the process of dramatisation ; and
Miss Nelly Mortyne gets a show to be a sinful
wife in the prologue, and sins very pleasantly in a
eostume which can be highly recommended.

“Squattocracy,” with which the new
season opened at Sydney Lyceum, is another of
those weird' productions which William Cosgrove
seems to get hold of velocity before
any other manager can rush after them. It con-
tains internal evidence of having been perpe-
trated in an exercise-book by a small youth in an
inky collar when he was “kept in,” and purports
to deal with the troubles of a Riverina squatter
who is about to be sold up for the mortgage. The
squatter-man is a small person painted in blobs of
bright red, as if he had had tomatoes thrown at
him. He employs a station-hand who is the dis-
guised eldest son of an English earl, and the earl’s
second son comes out to look for this lost scion,
and tell him that the fatted calf is ageing rapidly
in the paternal back yard. Whether the calf is a
third son or a complete stranger doesn’t appear.
The second son’s method consists in sending his
aristocratic wife to be housemaid on the station
and entering himself there as a species of amateur
jackaroo, and then for acts they carefully avoid
explaining things to the man they are looking for.
Now and then, however, they have a hurried
interview in a corner and say that if they are dis-
covered all will be lost, and suspicion will ruin
everything, and the lady is urged to bear up and
be a housemaid a little longer because they are on
the verge of suecess. In between times the
squatter rushes in weeping and says he owes
.£BB,OOO Bs. Bfd., and he orders the flunkey in
livery to lay a man-trap for the eldest son, the
result being that the beery tutor is caught in it.
He also gives a gorgeous garden party in Act 11.,
the guests including nine ladies and three males,
one of them without a waistcoat and with no
visible collar. By way of further variety the
tutor gets drunk, and the vulgar heir dresses him
up as the cook —a lady who has fits and sometimes
insists on sitting on the stove, under the im-
pression that she is a teapot. After a while the
eldest son takes to tearing around with revolvers,
and the family arms itself, but just then the
squatter is made a K.C.M.G., and his jackaroo
gives him £200,000 to pay off the mortgage with.
What happens after that the present writer doesn’t
remember very distinctly, but he has a vague idea
that an insolvent
bank is seen in
the distance
spearing dead
cows and eels, or
that the squatter
backs himself to
race a large iron
safe downstairs
with a yard’s
start.

A brief revival of “ Charley’s Aunt ” is in
progress at Sydney Criterion. Thornton’s yearn-
ing to play in skirts has to explode at frequent
intervals, else the quaint, crinkly little humorist
would split down the back with suppressed enthu-
siasm, and the present is as good an occasion as
any for the Brazilian female to break out. The
co. which plays the “ Aunt ” this time is stronger
in almost every point than the one which pro-
duced her a year or two ago in the days of the
Thornton-Arnold partnership, and whereas the
weak parts of the original show have been im-
proved upon a good deal, the strong ones
haven’t deteriorated in any way. The wild-look-
ing cap and streamers of the bogus widow
are as convincing as ever, and Time hasn’t
laid its effacing fingers on her respectable
black frock to any extent worth mentioning;
while the mouldy, dropsical umbrella of
Stephen Spettigue exhales the same odour of
damp and fungus and aggressive middle-class
seriousness as before. Spettigue’s umbrella always
appears to The Bulletin as the outward and
visible symbol of the Nonconformist conscience,
and the embodiment of the small Baptist chapel
up the back street, and the emblem of the heavy
meat-tea with which the serious Hinglish family
regales itself. “Charley's Aunt” revives. this
time for a short period only, though her exact
duration is not yet decided, and it w vaguely
rumoured that “Mamma” will follow. Mamma
is the only present-day comedy in which a British
gentleman frankly and undisguisedly spanks
a British lady on the ear for the good of her soul.
M? Z * * * *
Ovide Musin’s concert co. is doing well, so
far, at Sydney Palace, and that small building is
considerably bulged out at the sides by the pres-
sure of business. The Belgian melodist is a man
of might on the violin, and he is well supported ;
and though it has never yet been decided whether
Sydney is a musical city, or merely a city full of
people who want to look as if they were musical,
such an entertainment as his is practically certain
to do well. The Palace makes a good setting for
Musin’s artistic little show. It seems incongruous
to put the common or scrub species of variety
co. amid such costly and gorgeous surroundings,
and the general heavenliness of things seems
wasted on the soullessness of the earthy song-and-
dance person.

The Potter - Bellew reappearance in
“Romeo and Juliet,” at Melbourne Princess’s,
deserves grateful thanks from everybody who saw
those soulful exponents of the tender passion take
poison in a silent tomb seven years ago. It is a
rejuvenating spectacle. Mrs. Potters stage beauty
has not grown visibly older during the long inter-
val ; Bellew’s Romeo seems rather younger than
he used to be. Shakespeare, in constructing* his
boy - and - girl love-story, took care to set most
of the scenes in a dim religious light, for the
sake of all slim artists who might wish to
bring the experience of a lifetime to bear
upon one or other of the name-parts
Probably the Bard had Mrs. Potter and
Bellew in his prophetic eye at the time, in which
case eternal William regrets, no doubt, that the
lad}- hasn't turned her experience to better account.
Comparing her present performance with the pre-
vious effort, one finds no improvement in Juliet’s
elocution. W hat it has lost in spasmodic eccen-
tricity it has gained in unnatural monotone. The
actress administers her dose of charm and
annoyance in about the same proportions as before
Bellew now reels off his lines more easily'
without any sacrifice of poetic effect. The Bclle-
ao “P ts trallant gurgling boy as an ideal
lovemaker, and pleads guilty to a growing admira-
tion for the legs whereon he stands alone in this
character. Romeo and Juliet ” was to conclude
last night (Wednesday), consequently the good
points of Cecil Ward (as Mercutio), Cathcart and
one or two other members of the eo. must divide a
hastv blessing among them. “ School for Scan-
f®U?WS ancient tragedyffor two nights
only, and on Saturday a second change of pro-
gramme will occur if necessary.

The members of the Federal Convention
having taken a Sunday drive, the following reso-
lution was passed at a meeting of the Gawler
(S.A.) Wesleyan Sunday-school teachers :
That this meeting expresses its regret at the general
desecration of the Sabbath by the members of the Federal
Convention and those associated with them, and consider
that it is a violation of a direct law of God and is against
the best interests of the community.
This is but a small example of the goodness of
Gawler.

The following is the programme of the ceremonial
which is to be observed on 22nd June, on the occasien
when Sir George Turner is to be the guest of the British
nation. The Queen will come to London on Monday. 21st
June, from Windsor, and about 11 a.m. on the 22nd Her
Majesty, with a brilliant cortfege, representing even'
power in the world and all her colonies, will set out from
Buckingham Palace for St. Panl’s Cathedral.
What a fuss to make “on the occasion when
George Turner is to be the guest of the British
nation ! ”
The Corbett - Fitzsimmons battle will
shortly be reproduced with faithful details at
the Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-st., Sydney.
It was cabled that the great fight was recorded
all through by the Kinetoscope, and the colonial
branch of the Edison Laboratory before the fight
took place made its arrangements for securing the
record. The Edison Vitaseope, Kinetoscope, and
Kinetophone, and the wonderful X-rays are still
on show in the Parlor, and are attracting great
public interest.*
“ In Sight of St. Paul’s ” is a show that recalls
Old memories melodramatic—
A mug goes astray in the commonplace way,
And his brother heaps curses emphatic
On the evil brown head of a courtesan, wed
To a “ lag ” who pops up unexpected.
The muggins gets shot by another bad lot,
And Baker s unjustly suspected
(He’s alwayt unjustly suspected).
The lag who returned, being callously spurned
By the wife of his bosom, poor fellar !
Gets jealous and jibs, and is stabbed in the ribs,
And left to go mad in a cellar.
Miss Watson, the wife, leads a dissolute life
At a “ Den ’’ furnished up to the knocker,
Where a simple Aileen is dragged into the seen a
And told to drink fizz (it’s a mocker —
The Bible says fizz is a mocker).
But a “ tart,” with a heart, plays a chivalrous
part ;
Of her errors we freely absolve her
Since she comes to the aid of the innocent maid,
And shoets, with a shiny revolver,
Miss Watson, whose fate (we are happy to state)
Is hot as they make ’em, or hotter ;
For she doesn't expire till the house is on fire—
She’s cooked ere the devil has got her —
Done brown, ere the devil has got her.
She lies there a-croaking, whilst flaming and
smoking.
The Den is reduced into cinders ;
A sight to amaze is the stage all ablaze,
And the roof dropping out of the winders.
There are engines at work, back to Rue Petite
Bourke,
And firemen right cheerfully hosing
A tottering wall; in the midst of it all
Stands Baker, the rescuer, posing.
This fine conflagration compels admiration
(The play altogether impresses)
Wheu the last curtain falls on “In Sight of St
Paul’s,”
'Tis the greatest of Royal successes—
Of blood-spattered scenic successes.
The plot may be doosid far-fetched and illucid
(Though others are often absurder),
Yet the characters act as thouch fiction were fact
When their game is committing a murder.
Some dramas go slow, being heavy with woe,
But this is a Megsouic sprinter
That will certainly hum for a long time to come
And warm up the house for the winter.
One needs something warm for the winter.

said production at Melbourne Royal are held over
for further consideration.)

The WILD CAL
COLUMN
The Federal Convention having decided that it
can’t think out an equitable plan of taking over all the
provincial public debts, Tiik Bulletin desires to suggest
a compromise which is wholly equitable and as simple as
falling off a log—or even two logs. It is that the Federal
Government should take over so much per head of each
province’s liabilities. For instance, if Westralia comes in
the debts of the five federating provinces will be, on a
rough estimate : Westralia, £39 per head ; Victoria, £4O ;
N.S.W., £4B; Tasmania, £52; S.A., £67. Therefore, if
the Federal Government took over the whole Westralian
debt, and £39 per head of all the other debts, it would be
giving exactly the same measure of relief all round, and it
could then begin the work of making them into a uniform
Federal 3 per cent, stock. If the consolidation was a
great success and a large saving of interest was effected
there would probably be some serious attempt to devise
an equitable plan for taking over the rest of the
provincial liabilities. If nothing at all is done
in this direction all the advantages to be gained by the
superior credit of the Federal Government are wasted.
Even supposing there is a discrepancy in the rates of in-
terest until the consolidation is effected, that could easily
be got over by charging the amount of the discrepancy to
the separate states. The plan would answer equally well if
Westralia did not come in. Then Victoria would repre-
sent the minimum debt; the whole of it could he taken
over and £4O per head of the debts of the other provinces.
The result, roughly speaking, would be that £123,000,000
of debt would be consolidated, and if the Federal Govern-
ment’s superior credit enabled it to reduce interest even
ss. per cent, the saving would be over £300,000 a year.
And this transaction is free from every one of the difficul-
ties which stand in the way of a consolidation of the
debts as a whole.
» * * *
The-N.S.W. Government has decided to collect
two whole years’ income-tax in the 12 months, so, if there
isn’t a surplus at the end of June, there certainly should
be. Whether it will also try to collect two years’ land-
tax inside one financial year remains to he seen. What
The Bulletin now wants to know is : What particular
swindle is left for next financial year—the one ending
June, 1898? By the appearance of things, all the possible
swindles have been used up, but still there may he other
unsuspected possibilities ahead. Premier Reid produced
his first financial statement at December, ’94, but he had
then been only a month or two in office, and hadn’t
developed a policy of his own. That year, for which
Dibbs was mainly responsible, was the only one with a
surplus that could stand inspection. The first statement
for which Reid was wholly responsible appeared in June,
1895, when he took some £411,000 out of loan money
and put it into current revenue—a proceeding which
was promptly squashed by the Auditor-General, and by
this squash the surplus was turned into a huge deficit.
His next budget was that dated June, ’96, when, under
pretence of starting the “ cash basis ” he charged
£967,000 of ordinary expenditure to the accumulated
deficit, and added it to the public debt, in consequence of
which he claimed to have a surplus of £333,000, whereas
the Government Statistician shows that he had a deficit
of £634,000. Another financial statement is due two
months hence, and the “ fake ” this time is collecting two
years’ income-tax in one year. And w T hat the public
wonders now is this: Is there any “fake ” left for 1898 ?
So far G. Reid hasn’t produced one financial statement
which wasn’t glaringly dishonest, and in open, una-
bashed cookrng of the public accounts he has had no
equal in Australasia up to date. But the capacity for
inventing “fakes” must give out, and when the last one
is used up G. Reid will probably ride for a fall, and let
someone else face the trouble.
* * * »
The N.S.W. Income-tax Commissioners have
this year invented a new and grotesque three-card trick
of their own. Every tax-payer is charged on last year’s
income, for the sufficiently obvious reason that he doesn’t
know yet what this year’s income will amount to. Thus,
if a man had £2OO of taxable income in ’95 he paid £5 tax
on it in ’96. But if in ’96 he had £l5OO of taxable income
through drawing a £l3OO prize in Tattersall’s sweep, the
official sharks demand that he shall pay £37 10s. for 1897
and £32 10s. arrears for last year—in other words, that he
shall pay on the £l5OO for both years. To put it another
way, the two years’ taxable income was £l7OO, and the
Commissioners demand that he shall pay on an income of
£3OOO. If his taxable income for ’97 is only £2OO again,
his share of taxation is only £9, whereas he has paid £37
10s., so that the Treasury really owes him a refund of £32
10s. What his chance of getting it amounts to The Bul-
letin doesn’t know, but it doesn’t seem as if it was up to
much. The department doesn’t even give any promise
that it will refund overcharges.
So far as The Bulletin can make out from
a careful reading of the Act, this proceeding is an abso-
lutely unlawful bluff. The Act says that the tax-
able income for the year preceding the assessment
shall be the basis of taxation ; that is to say, the 1896 tax
shall be payable on the-1895 income. Nothing is said, so
far as this paper can discover, about any right to after-
wards alter the 1896 tax whenever it suits the Commis-
sioners to do so, so as to make itpayableon the 1896 income.
No doubt most people will pay rather than fight the
Government in the Supreme Court and the Privy Council
ert a cost of perhaps £lOOO, but the apparent utter
illegality of the proceeding remains all the same.
Maoriland reckons that its railways have paid
£3 2s. 6d. per cent, on cost for the financial year just
ended—the best return since the boom days, fifteen years
ago. At March, ’96, the return was £2 16s. per cent.; at
March, ’95, it was £2 14s. 6d. The splendid increase for
this year is another evidence that Maoriland is prospering
under the Seddon policy of State socialism. Also, it is a
solid proof that it is rich enough to build its new roads
and railways without foreign borrowing—if the Govern-
ment had rhe moral courage to face the situation. The
invariable Australasian way of looking at the matter is :
The country is prospering, therefore it can afford to pay
interest on another million. The other view is that the
country is prospering and can therefore afford to do
without borrowing another million. But nobody takes
much account of that other view.

Melb. Australasian scoffs at the Turner Govt’s
company legislation because there is nothing in it which
could prevent such elaborate bogus bill transactions
as were allegedly worked by the Ward Association in
collusion with the M.L. Colonial Bank. Its theory, ap-
parently, is that because the Companies Act can’t provide
for everything, therefore it shouldn’t provide for anything.
By the same logic the Australasian should be abolished
because there is nothing in it to make coffee beans grow
in Iceland. One great business of the press is to reform
grievances, and if it can’t reform the grievance caused by
the want of coffee beans in Iceland there shouldn’t be afiy
press.
* * * *
The full text of the A. J.S. Bank reconstruction
scheme is to hand, and it differs only in one or two par-
ticulars froni the cabled summary. The deposits are to
be divided into “ A ” deposits at 3 per cent, certain, pay-
able from 1898 to 1997, or earlier, at the bank’s option ;
“ B ” deposits at 3 per cent, certain, payable by two equal
instalments in 1912 and 1917, or earlier, at the bank’s
option ; and inscribed deposit stock at 3(4 per cent, re-
payable at the bank’s option, but not otherwise. The
way these amounts are distributed makes the probable
maximum saving—the saving to be effected if the bank is
only able to pay the 3 and 34 per cent, above specified—
about £69,500 per year, and as the last balance-sheet
showed a loss Of £48,200 for the half-year this certainly
doesn’t look enough to fill the gap. After the above in-
terest has been paid 5 per cent, of the surplus (if any) is
to go to reserve fund ; then the shareholders are to get a
dividend of £1 6s. Bd. per cent, per annum ; next, if the
surplus still holds out, the inscribed stock-holders are to
get an extra 1 percent., which will raise their rate of in-
terest to the original 4.J- per cent. If there is still any
surplus remaining after this operation, the “A” and
“B ” depositors are to receive an extra 1 per
cent., making their rate of interest 4 per cent.
Supposing there is still some surplus after all these things
have been attended to, the shareholders are to get an
additional 13s. 4d. per cent., making 2 per cent, in all;
hut they are not to go above this figure until the “ A ”
and “B” crowd are entirely paid off—except that, if any
new capital is called up, the dividend on it may go as
high as 3 per cent. Then, by a strange oversight, nothing
is said about what is to be done with the rest of the sur-
plus. Perhaps even the most sanguine committee couldn’t
imagine the A. J.S. Bank having anymore surplus after
all these things had happened to it.
The arrangement provides a fearful maze of
complicated ways and means whereby it is to he decided
which deposits are “ A ” and Which are “B,” but as the
rate of interest is the same in either case the “ A ” and
“B” complication is comparatively unimportant. The
mam features are : The A. J.S. can save nearly £70,000 a
year if necessary. The shareholders are not likely to re-
ceive more than £1 6s. Bd. per cent, for several years, and
not more than £2 per cent, for a good many years ; fur-
ther, it may be some time before even the dividend of £1
6s. Bd. gets into motion. Also the reserve fund isn’t
likely to boom to any great extent until things improve
materially. Another casual complication is that the re-
duction of interest on the inscribed deposit stock starts
from Ist January, 1897, while the reduction on the “A”
and “ B ” deposits only begins from the date when the
scheme is sanctioned by the N.S. W. Supreme Court. All
new business is to have a prior lien on the assets —barring
the fact that certain assets are to be kept separate as a
fund for payment of the “ B” deposits, which may thus,
under certain conditions, be paid before the “ A ” de-
posits. Likewise, £2,000,000 of the “B” 3 per cent, de-
posits may be converted into 34 per cent, inscribed stock.
In fact, the small complications and side-issues of the
A. J.S. scheme are about as hard to follow as the theo-
logical dissensions of an ant-hill.
* * * *
Ex-premier and director Morehead, orating at
the meeting of the Queensland National Bank: “It was
proposed by the commission’s scheme to wipe out the
shareholders entirely, and a certain section of the press
blamed the shareholders for the position the bank was
now placed in through their having kept the present
directors on the board. He failed to see the reason of
this argument, and also failed to see why the shareholders’
property should be taken from them. To his mind it was
a most unjust proposal.” The Q.N. bank directors have
put up a good many records for superhuman gall, but
this lays over everything up to date. Director Morehead
can’t “see why the shareholders’ property should be
taken from them.” By the bank’s own acknowledgement
the shareholders’ property in the bank is all lost—they
have no property at all. Also, they have lost an immense
amount of the depositors’ property, for which they are
liable so far as the uncalled capital will go. The
only sense in which the shareholders of the Q.N. Bank
own “ the property ” is that they owe it nearly £400,000.
The Q.N.B. is not like the other reconstructed institu-
tions which all have assets enough (on paper, at least) to
cover their liabilities. It hasn’t even paper assets to
meet its debts with ; it is admittedly a beggar howling to
its creditors for charity and saying, at the same time,
that it doesn’t want to pay even so far as it can ; and in
the light of these matters director Morehead’s attitude is
all the more interesting.

The municipal valuation of Newcastle (N.S.W.)
in 1891 showed an annual value of £374,400. This year’s
valuation shows £245,000—a drop of 34 per cent.
« * * *
The Premier Permanent Building Society,
whose downfall was the beginning of the great Melbourne
crash, has now been seven years in liquidation, and so far
has yielded no dividend to depositors. In the long struggle
it has paid away £751,000 to preference creditors, and
there is about £60,000 more to pay before the depositors
begin to have a look-in. The Premier Permanent pawned
nearly every security it possessed to banks and other
institutions before <it closed its ddhrs, and all this time
has been spent in struggling with these prior clairiis.
Liquidation ex.’s have absorbed £33,000, and £45,000 has
gone in keeping the society’s vast rows of jerry-built
tenements—most of them permanently empty—from fall-
ing about its ears. It now figures out that it has £92,000
worth of city property; 683 suburban houses, and 31
vacant allotments on which it doesn’t venture to place
any definite value; and about £35,000 worth of other
assets. The city property will probably he mostly needed,!
to cover the balance of prior claims, and £530,000 worth
of depositors are mainly dependent for dividends on the
683 houses and other sundries. Deposits have sold lately
as low as Is. in the £l. Many ’cute Hebrews and others
bought them at 14s. in the £ immediately after the stop-
page, and were denounced as “ cormorants.” Alas 1
where are those Hebrews now ?

The returns to the New York Insurance De-
partment for 1897 show that the Mutual Life Insurance
Co. of New York has 5857 loans in force on first mortgage.
These loans amount to a total of £14,690,745, and the
State Insurance Department experts report that each
piece of property-is worth more than double the amount
advanced upon it.

The story of a bill, as related the other day in a
Melbourne court, suggests that the financier’s existence
is rather,a squalid affair nowadays. According to the
narrative, No. 1 sold some shares for £5O, and was so
glad to do business that he took a bill at six months
in payment. He asked No. 2 to endorse it and
get it discounted. No. 2 apparently hadn’t £6O handy,
but he charged £5 for putting his name on the back, so
apparently the value of a name in Melbourfie now is 10
per cent, on the sum that its owner hasn’t got about him
at the moment. Then he went to business’’man No. 3,
who lent £5 on the bill. Afterwards the latter introduced
the holder of the bill (No. 2) to financier No. 4, who lent
£l5 on it. No. 3 got his £5 back out of this and charged
£2 for the introduction to No. 4, so that No. 2 got £8 net
out of the £l5. It is alleged that he claimed all this ; £5
for the use of his name, and £3 as commission for hawk-
ing the bill round to Nos. 3 and 4, So, according to the
plaintiff (No. l’s) story, it took four financiers several
weeks’hard struggle to melt 5, £5O bill with three names
on it, and the net result was £l5, all swallowed up in
commission—for, according to plaintiff’s tale, the little
cash he did get. out of the business had to be paid away
as commission or something tb a fifth person, who seems
to have looked on. Most of these people are now
suing each other for unpaid commission, or something of
the kind. Even gojng oti the land seems better than
being a modern Melbourne financier, when five carnivor-
ous birds get on top of one little p.n.
* * * *
Mr. James M. Finlay, manager and part-owner
of Mitchell’s Creek Freehold (N.S. W.) gold-mine, shot
himself in Sydney on Sunday last—for no known reason.
He was a man greatly liked and respected, and of most
steady habits. But he was of mercurial disposition, and
had of late suffered much from alternate excitement and
depression—and he had reached the fatal age of 37.
* * * *
Silver Hill Junction has its little milling-plant
almost ready, and now it is busy looking for ore. It has
some, of course, but the greater part of the bodies ex-
posed rs very patchy, and often mixed with much quartz.
Tarrawingee quarries continue to send in 2000
tons of flux a week.
The latest count of men on the Broken Hill
mines showed a total of 6030— probably the greatest on
record.
Nine vessels loading, or to load, sulphides were
lying at Port Pirie at one time recently. The aggregate
capacity was 15,000 tons. And Port Augusta and Port
Adelaide also do a good deal of this trade.
In order to profitably carry the large quantities
of sulphides from Broken Hill at the specially-reduced
rates both the Silverton and South Australian Govt, lines
are being re-graded and awkward curves removed.
The British runs its mill two shifts only, but
almost maintains its reduction-record. Very little car-
bonates are, however, being treated. In the last fort-
night of ’96, for instance, 1990 tons carbonates and 1698
tons sulphides were put through ; during the last-reported
fortnight 3315 tons sulphides and no carbonates. The
output of concentrates is now smaller—in the former case
it was 637 tons, averaging 670 per cent, lead and 16oz.
silver; last fortnight it wass37 tons, averaging 66 percent,
lead and 250 z. silver. The first grade concentrates, of which
last fortnight there were 482 tons of the gross total of 537,
is, however, better than formerly : the lead is 2 per Cent.,
and the silver soz. better, while the zinc has fallen from
8 per cent, to 6 per cent. But, though no carbonates are
going through the mill, additions are being made to the
big heap at surface at the rate of 300 t%ns a week.
What concentration means in losses of metal,
even when the latest and most improved machinery is
used, the North’s figures show. In a week 555 tons sul-
phides were milled ; the crude ore assayed 27 per cent,
lead, 7oz. silver, 12J- per cent, zinc—a total of 149 J tons
lead, 38850 z. silver, 69 tons zinc. The concentrates (from
which, of course, not all the metal can be recovered) were
shown on assay to contain 107 tons lead, 14400 z. silver,
and 114 tons zinc. Yet this is “ the solution of the sul-
phide problem.’’
A vugh or cavern in the lode, 85ft. long, 33ft.
wide in its widest part, and 30ft. high, has been broken into
in the 600 ft. level at The Central (Ashcroft’s Sulphide Cor-
poration) mine. This is said to be the biggest Broken
Hill vugh to date.
* * * *
Christmas Gift claim, Wyalong, has crushed
about 1000 tons averaging 20z., raised from the surface to
150 ft. deep. Aside-on claim, the Block, has
the reef at 240 ft., and is now raising a first crushing—not
counting a five-ton lot treated for 4oz. per ton when the
reef was first cut. At the bottom of the shaft the ore is
all sulphide ; assays have given 16oz. from the sulphide
ore. Although an east and west reef, the Gift has
managed to live down to 240 ft., so there is a reasonable
chance that it goes further.
The deepest shaft on Wyalong is in the Light-
house claim —360 ft., the last 200 ft. through hard granite.
The sinking is to get Klink’s reef where it runs out of that
claim on the underlie. The deepest workings in Kiink’s
are about 175 ft., and if the underlie had continued, the
Lighthouse should have got the reef at about 350 ft. The
Lighthouse people intend, with some Govt, assistance
now almost promised, to sink another 100 ft. The Prin-
cess., on same line, has been raising 4oz. stone from 230 ft.
As the stone in Klink’s has averaged about 6oz. from a
monthly output of about 50 tons, and as the Lighthouse
claim is side-on, the reef should be good in the latter if
cut.
Wyalong is agitating for a diamond-drill “on
easy terms.” The main reefs having been proved to
well on towards 300 ft.. there is a strong opinion in some
quarters that it would be better to give Govt, aid in sink-
ing the deep prospecting shafts. A man can go down a
shaft and look at the bottom and drive in any direction
and perhaps find something. A bore doesn’t tell much
worth knowing where reefs are small and gold-shoots
intermittent.
The first successful attempt to sell Bull a
Wyalong mine is reported. The Garryowen and Scotch-
man’s, comprising in all about 10a., is reported sold for
£5OOO and a quarter interest. Show fair and stone good,
hut reef small—like most on Wyalong.

Now there is no demand in London or elsewhere
for scrip in undeveloped Westralian mines, Block 45 of
the Hampton Plains Estate Co. has been thrown open to
prospectors on wonderfully easy terms compared with
those demanded at the birth of the boom. A reward of
£lO,OOO and a half interest are offered for the discovery
of the first payable gold-mine, and for eaeh of the next
four payable mines the finders will be entitled to a reward
of £2OOO and a three-quarter interest in the claims.
Leases to he granted subject to Govt, royalty and the
employment of one man to each 12 acres. To what ex-
tent the finds must be payable nothing is said. They
would need to be more permanent than nine-tenths of
the big finds of the country to warrant such rewards.
Some 2(4 years back a well-equipped prospecting party
found what seemed to be a good surface show ou this
gigantic estate, and enquired as to the terms of opening
it up. The leader told the writer that after some months
delay he was informed that, firstly, he would have to put
up a sum of money as evidence of good faith —that ope-
third of the gross results would go to the c-O. which held
the gold-rights of'this part of tiie estate, and another full
third to the owning eo. He asked what was going to
become of the other third and was told that that was fot
himself and party, less, of course, expenses of working,
&c. That show probably remains unworked. At one
time Bayle.v’s mine was supposed by this huge monopoly
to be on the estate. Also the co. had no doubt that the Lon-
donderry was. Neither turned out to be, nor anything
glse up to date. Now that Bull is no longer a milch cow

has already lost on them a great deal more money than

From Kalgoorlie to The Bulletin: “The
mines here are looking better than ever, but there is next
to no market in London. Within three months, there
will be a big increase in the yield, and there may be an-
other boom, but it will only be in shares in big mines.
At. time of writing, the Ivanhoe has shown nothing pay-
able on the 300 ft. At that levtel, the Boulder is looking
remarkably well. Boulder Perseverance, showed a falling
off last month, but the total crushed (3675 tons) gave an
average of 2oz. Last month’s yield for Hannan’s
district, 21,4430 z. If this is largely increased, arid no
demand takes place in London for shares, I wonder what
will happen. Kaufman has returned, hut admits that
London will not look at new ventures from W.A. The
Associated mines are doing well, and should turn out
bullion shortly. The group of mines around the Boulder
appears to be consistently good. Mt. Charlotte strike, I
am informed, does not amount to anything. Lake Lefroy
rush may turn out something. Not the usual white buck
quartz, but a Sort of porphyry with fine gold.”

By the crushing of nearly 4ewt. of specimens
in London, the Queen of the Earth mine, W.A., has paid
a div. of £75 a share. The chute from which they came
was found some twa years ago. It was literally slep't on
for some months. A little thin and short streak in a
huge body of white barren quartz. It had to be worked
because no one would buy at the price. The gold taken
out surprised everybody. How much is left is an unan-
swerable enigma.

SOCIETY
The question that is agitating Adelaide is :
shall an apartment in the Town Hall be reserved
as reception-room for the Mayoress ? Parties are
divided in the council and among the public, and,
a few days ago, the anti-Mayoress section euchred
the other group by snatching a vote which post-
poned the acceptance of tenders until after the
next municipal elections. The “ toney ” people
are all in favor of establishing a place where they
can eat “kyke” and drink tea supplied by the
female Hisworship. The other crowd, having no
hopes of agitating their molars, go all the other
way.
* * * *
It was a year on April 9 since the old
honorary medical and surgical staff of Adelaide
Hospital went out on strike, and no one has lost
by the change but themselves. There are now
no medical students in the hospital to experiment
on the patients or to philander with the nurses.
SLIGHTLY DIFFERENT.
MRS. SMITH (who has been making private enquiries): “I thought you told
me you left your last place on account of your religion, Bridget ? ”

atin’ mate on Fridays !”
The fledgling physicians have all flown to Mel-

* * • *
A S.A. poisoning-case turned out to be a
very ludicrous affair. Some,workmen were paint-
ing and coloring the inside of a house, and the
tenant asked them to clean the picture-frames.
They cunningly averred that whisky was a splen-
did thing to clean them with, and she immediately
gave them a small gin-bottle full. The frames
were cleaned with soap-and-water and the whisky
drunk. Shortly afterwards the painters were
attacked with violent pains in the stomach—so
severe that one had to be taken to the hospital,
while the others lay down and bellowed. Police
were called, and the workmen explained that they
had drunk the liquor and wanted to give the
woman in charge. On being appealed to, she
said she had given them the spirits to clean the
pictures and not to drink, as it was whisky she
had used for cleaning her dog with.
Citizens of Launceston, Tas., in public
meeting assembled, decided to mark the record
reign by founding a maternity hospital. Then
local sassiety commenced to think it over, and
resolved to “ put down ” the project “in the
interests of morality.” So the Mayor was requisi-
tioned to call another public meeting, at which a
screaming effort was made to rescind the former
decision. But the meeting re-affirmed the hos-
pital idea, and the alternative proposal (that a
certificated midwife should be dressed in royal
uniform and sent round to attend the poor in their
native slums) was indignantly scouted by an
unfeeling majority. Probable result: The town
will get neither the maternity hospital nor the
royal brass-bound nurse, and the population will
continue to arrive in the same way it arrived
before the record reign was thought of.
The Salvation Army is launching a Chinese
Mission in Little Bourke-street, Melb., in rivalry
to the Anglican and Wesleyan. John says
Allooyah gal no good—doubled up, too white, too
muchy starve. He can’t stand a kink in the back,
can John. Please give him nicee lit’ gel with
nothing else to do. No stitchers, or the like.

H. : “I have heard, from a white woman
married to a Chinese, a new version of the death
of the Misses Saunders, Australian missionaries,
in China. Now, I pledge you that this is the talk
among the Chinese, although I believe it untrue.
They say one of the Misses Saunders was trying
to arrange her marriage with an influential
Chinese man, who afterwards became one of her
murderers, and that she did this as a measure to
advance the cause. Mrs. Saunders and her third
daughter have just gone to the same mission-
field.”

Clutha Presbytery (M.L.) re Sunday ex-
cursion to open R. C. church :
The Presbytery unanimously resolved to protest against
the inauguration of such trains, and expressed regret that
this inauguration of Sunday excursion trains has been
connected with a professedly Christian church.
“ Professedly ”is good. The cautious Calvinist
doesn’t take any risks in the way of reckless en-
thusing about the rival sin-shifting establishment.

* * * *
The praying for rain business was badly
muddled, as usual. Some N.S. W. denominations
rain-prayed on Friday and some on Sunday, while
the Vic. churches propose to come in by them-
selves later on, instead of all making a simultaneous
effort. It is just the same as three men trying to
lift a 4cwt. bag of potatoes. If one gives a lift on
I riday and doesn’t shift it, and another does a
hoist by himself on Sunday, yet the potatoes don’t
budge, and the third hauls at the bag and finds it
immovable next week, the aggregate result is that
it remains just where it was at first. Yet, if they
all lifted together, the same amount of effort would
have done the business. The three separate com-
binations which have been struggling separately
to lift the drought should consider this parable.
Last Friday about 1 per cent, of the
N.S.W. male population prayed for rain, and the
other 99 per cent, or so drifted around holiday-
making and said “blanky.” The Bulletin, as
self-appointed returning-ofiicer without salary
reports that the blankies have it

A Darlinghurst gaol-war-
der, defending the use of the
triangles, makes public the state-
ment that there is at present in
Sydney a man who has received
a total of 600 lashes. As 50 are

militates against the deterrent powers attributed
to that form of punishment.

Last week, all the Sydney dailies came
out with a 30-line news-column par. about a social
function at a certain city undertakery. It began
with the cheerful heading, “A New Hearse.”
1 hen it went on to state that many mayors and
influential people met, by invitation, to inspect
the firm’s bran-new corpse-barrow, and how it was
of an improved early-ripening slipstone New-
foundland breed, with two masts and deep centre-
board, bound in half-calf, giving a beautiful clear
light with less than half the usual consumption of
gas, docile, non-forfeitable, free from spavin,
guaranteed to keep accurate time, and to
Pay 7h per cent, endowment bonus without
manure, and to lay eggs regularly at least
nine months in the year; also, that it was
a light-smoking variety with good aroma and
india-rubber frost-proof annuity, rifled in both
barrels. The crowd, it was further reported
greatly admired the receptacle, and had light re-
freshment among the wooden overcoats and the
embalming apparatus. Also a social ghoul pro-
posed a toast, “ Success to the firm,” and trusted
that it might have many affluent stiff s to carry to
the graveyard loam in the new vehicle, and much
embalming to do, and plenty of bones to rattle
over the stones with the red-nosed mute in attend-
ance. Likewise a visitor formally christened the
new vehicle, and the gathering had a real good
time like a, lot of early Christians on the razzle-
dazzle in the Catacombs. Thus does the under-
taker solemnly paste his advt. on the ghost of the
departed and leave his business-card in the bony
hand of Death with a view to future orders

Two Dunedin (M.L.) but-
chers were lately fined for color-
ing sausages with oxide, of iron
after admitting that the trick
was usual, as sausages were apt
to become black and unsaleable
within a day or two of manufac-

ing to believe that sausages, es .
pecially in a hot country, ought
to be prohibited by Act of Parlia-
ment.

Polynesian humor ! South
Sea navigator tells how a chief at
Santa Anna came aboard suffer-
ing from skin disease and seeking
a remedy. Received as remedy a
mixture of kerosene and sulphur
which he applied as prescribed-
only to be immediately set on fire
by a match judiciously held near
by a jocose countryman. The
dusky potentate was in among the
sharks in half a jiffy.

Brisbane (Q.) Telegraph, in a rabid
attack on the 0. of E. and the R.C. sects, declares
that “ the Nonconformists have no desire, or, if
any, very little,” for the money of the State. The
Nonconformists have, in common with every other
sect, grabbed as greedily as they could at State
grants of land. And wasn’t there a first-class true
story about South Brisbane Methodists evading
the municipal rates by holding service now and
again on a vacant allotment, or something with
only a ruined humpy upon it, and then asseverat-
ing that the land was “ set apart ” for religious
rites, and was exempt under the Act ? Also, is
not the Telegraph editor a Nonconformist par-
son or ex-parson ? And is not its ex-managing
director ditto ? And doesn’t the \v r riter remen,her
a wrathful editorial years ago from the former’s
pen, declaring that there are no such things as
Nonconformists in Australia, and there is no
State Church here ?
POOR MAN!
MRS. QUINN: “Good mornin', Mrs. Heeley, an
how arc ye the day?”
MRS. HEELEY : “ Good mornin’ to you, Mrs.
Quinn, an’ how’s yerself? ”
MRS. Q. : “ Troth, I’m well. I ain’t had the ache
or pain these two mont’s, plaze the saints .”
MRS. H.: “ Indade, thin, an’ how’s that same?”
MRS. Q. : “ Sure, it’s becaze me mahn have got a job
carpet-batin’?”
MRS. H.: “ Carpet-batin’! what have that to do
wid per aches, Mrs. Quinn?”
MRS. Q. : “ Tverythin’, Mrs. Heeley. Ye see, Dan
is so tired in the arrms wid batin' carpets
all the blissul day, he have no heart to be
batin’ me in the a renin’s, poor mahn!”
MRS. H. (sympathetically) : “ Poor mahn !”
KISS-AND BE DAMNED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I took the crimson flower of love
The devil proffered me,
And laid its petals at thy feet
And laughed in careless glee.
I took the kisses from thy lips
Like draughts of mellow wine,
That set my heart a-glow with love,
Quick-beating love for thine.
I took the notes of one glad song,
And sang them wild and well;
I gave my soul, and love, and lost,
And —this is woman’s Hell.
Yet would I give another life
For love that I have spent,
And live with thee, and die, and then
Be damned, and be content.
Waikato, M.L.
D.M.R.

WRITE FOR ILLUSTRATED LIST.
Many people who have read in Sydney
Daily Telegraph Mr. Colin M'Kay Smith’s
earnest denunciation of what, in painter’s slang,
are known as the “tripe” and “dingbats,” pur
chased for and hung in N.S.W. Art Gallery, more
or less agree with his outburst against “ The
Judgment of Paris,” by Greiffenhagen, and
several other expensive canvases. But will Mr.
M‘Kay Smith kindly tell people why he calls
Salle’s “ Anatomy Class”a “ disgusting ” picture ?
The Bulletin has up to now regarded it as a
model of figure-painting, and has never heard
artists speak of it but with admiring enthusiasm.
* * * •
The M.L. National Council of Women, at
its annual meeting, announced some important
discoveries. Mrs. Cunnington contended, as the
result of patient research and elaborate experi-
ments, that she had proved men to be “ quite as
kindly-hearted as women.” An exception as
against reporters was made amid loud applause,
and the thesis was taken as proved. Mrs.
Hendre, while reserving minor phases of the sub-
ject for further consideration, confidently an-
nounced that “Nobody loved men better than
women.” Many members seemed to regard the
induction as not fully justified by the investiga-
tions detailed, and the announcement in any case
as premature. It was eventually agreed that it
was desirable to make further observations and
experiments and report at a future meeting.
At a recent meeting of the Council of Women
aforesaid the members had at first rather a slow
time with their favorite Age of Consent. There
was no vim in the talk and no novelty in the pro-
posals. The trouble seemed to be that everybody
knew all that could be told about the consenting
side of the subject, while nobody had any fresh
first-hand information about the asking aspect of
the case. But Mrs. Cunnington was equal to the
occasion. She “ was strongly of opinion, seeing
the great importance of the matter, that the sub-
ject should be dealt with and discussed by a con-
ference of men and women.” Whereupon there
followed much vigorous applause and a hopeful
and expectant discussion. When that conference
comes off with the right men, and when everyone
talks right out ! ! !

Hobart coronial enquiries take place in a
stuffy room, lately described by an irate juryman
as a veritable chamber of horrors. In the fire-
place, for instance, are the loose boulders which a
suicide of some months ago had hung around his
neck as an anchor. The other day a skeleton was
the subject of “ crowner’s quest,” and a juryman
casually ventured to presume that the dry hones
would be indefinitely left on the mantel-shelf with
other gruesome mementoes; but the coroner
solemnly promised to have the remains, in this
instance, carted away somewhere. N.B. —The
juryman who so violently disturbed the usual
slumber of the court is curator of the local
museum. Otherwise —well, a rival show isn’t to
be tolerated.
* * * *
Some time ago a Geraldton (N.Q.) citizen
separated from his wife and migrated to Cool-
gardio. Here he dabbled in speculation, made
money, and married a second wife. Falling sick
he willed all his property to his “ wife,” thereby
meaning No. 2, who hastened, on hubby’s de-
mise, to get the will executed. An old
North Queenslander, resident in W.A., hearing
of the affair, informed wife No. 1, who speedily
set to work, claimed the estate, and, as the result
of a well-arranged compromise, came out at least
jeio,ooo to the good.
* * * *
The way of the transgressor is exceedingly
hard sometimes. At Melb., the other day, a man
got concussion of the brain from slipping off a
tram-car, and was removed to the hospital by a
policeman. On his way to the hospital the sufferer
threw away a couple of watches which proved to
have been just stolen from his employer. He had
intended to tram into town and pawn the goods.
In the words of the casualty reporter, ‘ ‘ should the
man recover —his recovery is very doubtful —he
will be prosecuted for the larceny of the watches.’
A notorious land-
shark and bone-
grinding boomer of
the deepest dye sat
in the d.c. of a Mel-
bourne theatre one
night recently, dri-
velling copiously and
noisily over the woes
of a “ pore, ” suffering
fellow-creature on
the stage. “ Have
you no heart ? ” a
widow asked a well-
known Melb. money-
lender. “Yes,
ma’am,” he replied,
“ but it would get
callous if I used it
in my business.”
Picnickers at
Coogee (Sydney) last
week were horrified
at the sight of a
woman and a naugh-
ty man bathing
openly on the beach
as the sun went
down. The lady
wore her robe de nuit
and an innocent ex-
pression, but a big
policeman thought
this insufficient, and
insisted on escorting her to the shelter of a clump
of trees and mounting guard until she was safely
into her clothes again.

* * * •
Five Chinamen charged at Sydney sessions
a couple of weeks ago with conducting a lottery
pleaded guilty. Being remanded for sentence till
the following day, they were taken into Darling-
hurst Gaol, where, as is usual under the circum-
stances, they were all searched. The Judge
changed his mind during the day, and in the
afternoon they were brought up for sentence, and
fined £5 each. One of them put his hand in his
pocket and, producing five presented
them in payment. Now, where were those fivers
hidden ?

A banquet will be held by “ the English-
men of Sydney ” to celebrate St. George’s Day,
April 28. Apart from the absurdity of paying
HE UNDERSTOOD THEM.
MRS. JUMPUP : “ This is not a smoking-carriage, sir !”
BARCOO BILL (down on a holiday) : “ You’re just like my ole woman, marin; she’d
keep on saying a thing till someone would contradict her, so that she
could argufy.”
reverence to St. George, who, if The Bulletin
mistakes not, was a fraudulent army-contractor,
noted for his villainously rancid bacon, the “Eng-
lishmen of Sydney ” have struck a somewhat
novel idea. Who ever before heard of the
Englishmen celebrating their alleged, national
anniversary in Australia, where, up to a
recent date, the English (as distinguished
from other British) nationality has been
dominant? A long time ago a certain Melb.
University professor was asked to stand
for Parliament. “Why should Ido so?” he
enquired in astonishment. “I am not a Scotch-
man, and so no Scotchman would vote for me;
nor am I Irish and entitled to expect the Irish
vote. lam only English, and, therefore, cannot
necessarily look for the English vote. In fact,
there is no English vote.” Is Bull in Australia
becoming clannish at last?
* * * *
The abortive discussion in the Adelaide
Convention re the proposed Easter recess incident-
ally illustrated the varieties of mind that go to
nation-building. Certain Christians desired Fri-
day to be a holiday for an Anti-Jewish celebration;
certain Jews wanted Saturday a holiday for an
Anti-Christian one ; both parties agreed to lie low
on Sunday, and both sent up a, lusty voice to
devote Monday to the Onkaparinga (Adelaide)
races.

A WOMAN’S LETTER .
r „ Sydney, April 20,1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
Women at Rand wick races on Saturday and
Monday were a sweltering crowd groaning beneath
too-previous winter garments and a much-too-
ardent sun. A few fortunate ones had furbished
up seasonable apparel from the wreck of last
season’s chiffons, and managed to look less boiled
than the majority, but the mass of fur and velvet
and heavy cloth gave a sensation of universal
smother. On Saturday, amid a thin stand, a
purple gown from Paris stood out conspicuously,
worked with pleated black silk and glittering
braid; a girl in another importation wore a bodice
showing quaint arrangement of flimsy and furry
bodice trimmings in stripes. Several tweed suits
were copied from the Tivoli sporting girls’ frocks,
and very heavy they looked. A bride from Mel-
bourne wore a tailor-made, and repeated it on
Monday. One woman wandered about holding
tight on to her silken skirts with both hands,
thereby showing mueh grass-lawn and lace frillery
beneath.
Cup-day went with a swing. The stand, includ-
ing the vice-regal box, was packed. The Gover-
nor’s party was clad, to a woman, in belated
summer frocks, and every A.D.C. looked exactly
like every other A.D.C., as these lofty youths
always do. Lord Kilmorey, who was here with
H.R.H. of Saxe Coburg many years ago, both
under different names—the nobility have usually
an alias or two in the family—surveyed the field
through elongated field-glasses with a bored ex-
pression. Mrs. Freeman Thomas, with hubby
well in sight, looked happy as usual. His Ex.
made the breasts of some of the Zenda Co. swell
with pride by conversing for quite a while with
the handsome mother of one of them, dressed in a
pink velvet bonnet and black and white gown
of pronounced design. The bridegroom-elect
looked white and wan, and wore Knowles-like
pants. Miss Ferrar had a lattice-work of black
velvet on a loose-cut jacket. Noney Seabrooke,
in a pretty white embroidered frock, made a
modest book and “ fell in ” over every race. A
marvellously slim boy, palpably corseted, looked
like a double of his own walking-stick. A
prominent Melb. lawyer was dividing his atten-
tions between a leading actress and a golden-haired
cafh divinity ; on Saturday it was 6 to 4 on the tea-
room girl; on Monday she receded slightly in the
betting. The brother of an Australian actress
now making a name at ’Ome escorted Mrs. M.
Morris, who was enwrapped in an ermine cape
rather too warm for a Borchgrevink’s polar search.
The A.J.C. secretary’s daughter wore a hat
with multitudes of white plumes like an in-
fant’s funeral. The wife of Australia’s only
Jewish Judge had a gown of a fashion
that few have caught up to yet—wide navy velvet
skirts and spreading cape of the same, much
jetted; black sleeves and low-cut neck-band. Some
courageous donners of the sac-back coat only suc-
ceeded in getting all-overish baggy effects, and
seemed too small for their clothes. At tea-time
there was a hurried scramble for a large cup of
strong, bitter fluid and thick, unappetising slabs
of cake. A daintily-served refreshment-room on
the tea-shop plan would be an immense additional
attraction to Rand wick.
On Easter Monday, at Rand wick, the leading
man of a visiting dramatic co. was taken in tow
by an influential society dame who introduced
him to the Nicest only, and had him to her after-
noon-tea ; whereby the feud between the red and
black Elphbergs is reproduced in real life with
added bitterness.
It is an oddly incongruous sight to see Puritan
girls arrayed in sad-colored garb and white bib
and tucker selling elaborately ornate clerical vest-
ments at the Cardinal’s Fair. Also, the worship
of the horse is blessed by the fact that the wife of
a prominent bookmaker is presiding at the Rand-
wick stall, which is arranged with a back-cloth
representing an alleged Spanish street—if a street-
scene is particularly picturesque and back-slummy
they always label it Spanish ; for which reason
Balmain would pass well as an ancient Iberian
sea-port. Mrs. Toohey’s Indian embroidery has
been bought up rapidly. I have seen some gowns
in the making for this week’s festivities, with
much wealth of Ind. worked up into the indis-
pensable “ boleerio,” as fashionable costume-
builders will persist in calling the short jacket
now the mode. For the rest, the Hindoo finery
at the Burwood stall resolves itself chiefly into
fans and gaudy brassware.
The fancy-dressed damsels at the Fair are doing
good business in more ways than one during the
progress of the carnival. Going about begging in-
discriminately for customers gives the most re-
tiring girl an insight into the methods of less-
restricted walks of life, such as barmaiding and
cafe-serving. And then they can speak to strangers
without introduction, and chaff the prevalent
straw-hatted youth and enjoy his brilliant return
sallies; also the bazaar belle can extract in one
evening more coin from his pockets than she can
screw out of him painfully in a month after she
marries him, which she sometimes does as a result
of bazaar-acquaintance.
In a back row of the d.o. a much-bediamonded
dame made a sensation in a shady place with her
glittering tiara and riviere, or, in plain English,
hairpins and necklace, supplemented by various
brooches, bangles, etc. Sensational dressing on
the stage was not forthcoming; many of the
Nicest people present had had practical experi-
ence of the length of a court-train, and those who
hadn’t wisely suppressed a damaging astonish-
ment at the regulation yards of superfluous rags
trailing on the floor. Princess Fla via’s last gown
gave a useful example of a sac-back coat worn
gracefully. Elliott Page, who stars as a beauty,
shared honors with Miss Ferrar. She is the
sassiety lady of the co., and, with J. Knight, in
the recent Melb. season was invited out exten-
sively to Toorak’s stuccoed palaces. On Good
Friday Miss P. and her mother entertained many
of the local Nicest at an afternoon tea-drinking at
their hotel.
A crowded house worked up to a frenzy of
deafening chatter greeted “The Prisoner of
Zenda,” at Her Majesty’s on Saturday night. A
dress-circle which seemed overcrowded, over-
heated, overdressed and overfed, was intensely in-
terested in the first appearance of the Court
dresses and the much-talked of photograph-belle,
Mrs. Maesmore Morris. At her entry m a spangly
black gown with bunch of red roses, her anxious
critics simmered down into disappointedly admit-
ting that she really is a beauty, and gave their
attention to trying to find out what the play is
about. An unexplanatory prologue confuses
people to begin with, but gives Julius Knight a
chance of wearing fascinating lace and ruffles in
last century style. In the play the leading man
has a lightning-change part, personating the Eng-
lish Rudolph in a vivid wig (apparently borrowed
from Bessie Rignold) and a replica of Capt.
Ferguson’s moustache ; as royal Ruritanian
Rudolph he retains the Elphberg beet-root-colored
hair, but peels off his lip-ornament. Miss Ferrar,
who as Princess Fla via can also lay claim lawfully
to ruddy tresses, exhibits a more becoming shade
of carrots. In the Court-scene the society beauties
who enthralled audiences in the South with their
intimate knowledge of how to gracefully kick
along a train, are not on show, and are
meagrely replaced by ordinary supers who grovel
at so much per week. A Melb. girl with Clytie-
like curves, dressed in gas-green and florally-
Eatterned appendage, shows the best shoulders m
er genuflections before the Napoleonic Knight.
Miss Elliot Page’s new notion of hair-dressing has
distinction ; success lies in the knack of fixing
aright the sparkling paste-combs.
Considered from a “ sassiety ” standpoint, the
Agricultural Show at Moore Park was rather dull,
though the Nicest, to a limited number, paid
reserve prices to see the arrival and going-away of
the Govt. House party. Vice-regal gowns, like
most others of last week, showed that their
owners were mindful of the sartorial demands of
Easter-week, and were consequently limp and
faded. On Thursday, some deluded wearers of
winter garments must have shaken the dust of
Moore Park off their skirts as a testimony against
road-authorities. Country visitors plodded through
the sand as cheerfully as hens taking a dust-bath.
One oppressed dame in a coppery-hued silk flamed
in the sun and radiated heat-rays around.
After the two merry Swiss maids in the dairy,
the most cheerful exhibit was the jumping,
especially the female steeplechasing, which made
many girls resolve to sell their bikes and return to
a real live horse —until the alarming sight of one
intrepid fence-taker hanging head-downwards
from her stirrup rather restored their conceit in
the jigger which won’t jump hurdles. When his
Ex. and cortege rolled away, the wife of an emin-
ent personage, anxious to keep in the procession,
stepped into her carriage, followed by her
daughter and a shiny-hatted male, and hastily
observed “Home”—not “ ’Ome,” mind you—to
the flunkey. The surrounding throng watching
the proceeding solemnly repeated the mono-
syllable, and murmured it to each other with awe-
struck tones.
The most gruesome exhibit at the Show was a
well-advertised hearse —inaugurated with speeches
and drink at an invitation scramble attended by
a lot of fat and pompous “ business men,” whose
names made about 30 lines in the S.M. Hebald.
But why should hearses be on view at shows?
And if hearses and coffins and other such hideous
gear are to be obtruded upon people’s notice
at what is ostensibly a place of entertainment,
why should not the confraternity of ghouls be
allowed to hold a public competitive exhibition of
embalmed corpses ? Let me urge this idea on the
up-to-date committee and their enterprising secre-
tary, who, so far, have not done anything by
halves.
A Govt. House circular has been issued calling
a meeting for next Wednesday afternoon to dis-
cuss a sensible project for celebrating the record-
reign by founding a hospital for consumptives.
The Thirlmere Home holds about three-dozen
beds, and is always full. It is suggested that a
shilling-subscription be started at a Town Hall
meeting, convened by the Mayoress, copying the
successful “ Queen’s Shilling ” movement in Vic-
toria. There are just as many tuberculous patients
in non-record years as in Jubilees, but if it is
necessary to tack on a royal title to the subscrip-
tion-list before the coin is forthcoming, “ loyal ”
feelings have a commercial value which can be
utilised.
The wife of one Australian Premier is particu-
larly keen on accompanying him on his trip to the
Jubilee rejoicings. She is so much interested in
all his doings that she won’t let him go without
her. The N.S.W. Prime Minister’s wife has
wisely decided to stay behind and look after her
babies. She could hardly leave the children at
home and sail away without them. Possibly there
is no nursery accommodation at the Hotel Cecil,
and if there is the squalling of infants might dis-
turb the assembled statesmen in their cogitations
over important matters of deep Imperial interest,
so Mrs. Reid and the babies don’t go.
The Bathurst Picnic Race Ball Committee is in
trouble over the annual dance, as someone outside
the circle of City of the Plains aristocracy has had
the mean audacity to engage the School of Arts
for the very night best suited to the P.R.B.C.
While the best set was discussing reluctantly the
possibility of enjoying itself in the second-
best hall in the place, that, too, was snapped up
by some of the common herd, and only a very
third-rate building remains available to the picnic
racers. At a late date there was an awful possi-
bility that the ball might not come off at all.
Westralia’s Six Families are now swamped in a
flood of t’other-siders, but the hauteur still remains
sufficiently to brand with a broad arrow anybody
outside the pale of the half-dozen. In Perth hos-
pital flannels and linen supplied to the unfortunate
patients are all marked with the convict sign.
One sensitive invalid nearly died of horror on dis-
covering, on regaining his senses after typhoid,
that his flannel-shirt was stamped all over with
the arrow, and he hastily left hospital long before
he was really recovered.
Prince Alfred Hospital report shows that patients
have been cheered by visits from members of dif-
ferent religious bodies. Protestant nuns are now
numerous and are called sisters, only to differen-
tiate them from the old original cowl-bearers the
Wesleyan organisation are only referred to as
“ sisters ” in quotes.
The Society of Artists has rented a room in
George-street, No. 384 a, for its exhibition of small
pictures. Gov. Hampden will pay the show a
visit on 3rd May—it opens on the Ist—and the
monotony of the invariable “musical afternoon”
will be varied by special afternoons got up by
different professions.
The male beauty-show has set the discussion
going again about women’s appreciation of beauty
in men, and, of course, the late lamented Wilkes
is dragged in. As he is the only instance of an
ugly fascinating man ever mentioned, by this time
he should be considered the exception to the rule
that beauty fetches a higher price matrimonially
than brains. An “amusing man ” has not a large
following of admirers. “We seldom give our love
to a humorist.” We don’t take him seriously—or
otherwise. The professional funny man at picnics
would be a terrible person to marry ; but fancy
being tied for life to the “ serious man who rarely
smiles ” ! In fact, it would be too deadly to know
exactly the kind of man you were about to marry ;
you would lose all the charm of unexpectedness.
As Sam Rogers said, “It doesn’t matter very
much whom you marry, for next morning you will
find you’ve married somebody else. ”
The Southern traveller, Borchgrevink, has been
more successful in his matrimonial voyage than in
Antarctic waters. He was lucky enough to have a
trip ’Ome subscribed for him, and when there he
won a moneyed bride with whom he is now visit-
ing Australia. She is always clad in exploring
get-up—alpine hat, “pilot ” coat and business-like
skirt.
Mr. Tigheßyan says that Sydney girls have big
feet. Goodness knows how he found it out, but
he and editor Fitchett take the joint responsibility
of advancing the proposition that while in Melb.
“stock sizes” in footwear-stores, as Americans
call boot-shops, are 3’s and 4’s, local daisy-trippers
are mostly encased in s’s and even 6’s. This try-
ing climate again ! It is a fact that visitors from
the South here, in replacing their down-at-heel
shoes, have often to take a size larger than before.
Ada Ward, now turned Hallelujah-lass, em-
ployed as chaperon, on one of her Australian tours,
a little bull-pup always on a very long string. It
was a very respectable dog. The animal was the
cause of breaking up the devotion of an admirer-
now one of the Federal Conventioners. Meeting
her adorer one day in Spring-street, Melbourne,
the actress, remarking that something had gone
wrong with her cloak, gave him the dog’s string
to hold and—well, she let him hold it all the way
down the Opera House side of Bourke-street.
When he had made his way, with Ada’s well-
known puppy trotting after him, through the
jeering and chaffing bookies outside Tattersall’s,
he turned over Toby to its owner, and thereafter
he and Ada met as strangers.
Miss Ward is thoroughly entering into the spirit
of her conversion : she is detailing to interviewers
interesting particulars of her past career, and not
sparing herself in the recital. A newspaper-
account stated that she was married at 16, and got
divorced vvithin a year. She immediately pointed
out that it was against herself that the divorce
was obtained. The “artistic” instinct of posing
gracefully before the public here asserts itself—
another form of Jane Shore’s candle-and-penance
sheet. An actress repenting prettily in public is
much more interesting than the cruelly-wronged
wife who has got rid of the wicked husband.
i( Attractions of shopping during one of the
monster sales ” were considerably increased one
day by the appearance in the shop of Ada Baker’s
clever little, daughter, Alina Grey. This pocket
copy of Alice Leamar was most gorgeously ap-
parelled in silk, with velvet trimmings, and her
brightly-tinted hair was carefully tousled. An
imitative infant like Alma is a proud possession
for any parents, and a profitable one—but it is
cruel to force a child’s mind into such unnatural
precocity as to make her sue for gallery applause
by singing alleged comic songs about bloomers. A
child assuming to be ashamed to show her little
legs is as revolting as the taste which thinks that
sort of thing funny. And the pretended innocence
yet real knowingness covering (what should
be) babyish ignorance in such a ditty as “I’ve
asked Johnny Jones and I know now,” works up
to a most bewildering and intricate effect.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“As far a,s Melbourne is concerned, female
sympathy with lady-doctoring* is being severely
tested jugt now. Women in all the city divisions
and suburbs are canvassing women for shilling sub-
scriptions towards ‘a woman’s hospital for treat-
ment of women by women.’ The male powers that
be (and their wives) have turned a cold shoulder to
the unpretentious scheme. It comes at the most
unpromising time possible, in the face of a fashion-
able rival fund, and with seldom a poor little
newspaper paragraph to help it along. It appeals
for the bulk of its support to the poorer people
among most of whom the idea of a doctor in petti-
coats is still regarded with ignorant ridicule. The
woman missionaries with their collection-cards
have to fight prejudice on one hand, and hard-
uppishness on the other, and if in the end they
can show a substantial sum for their cause the
local Argus will feel ill for a week after publishing
the grand total.

actress sets the fashion. Has she a peculiarity in

Block on Saturday morning, or on St. Kilda
esplanade Sunday afternoon. If the smile is
remarkable, that smile haunts you everywhere it
grins out of every ‘fashionable’ feminine face,

and guns at you in your sleep. Miss Frances

of nausea and maidenly meditation, has not yet
been deposed. If a bandy actress with a cackle

“ Mr. Theodore Napier, who, during a long
residence in Melb., masqueraded as a Highland
Chieftain of the last century, is just as striking
and picturesque a figure in the streets of Edin-
burgh at the present time. An Edinburgh paper
thus describes him : ‘ With his white moccasins
and the enormous cairngorm in his shoulder
brooch, his collar of fine Honiton laee, and his
cockade quite a foot long, he is a picturesque and
imposing personality. He is every inch the
picture of what a Highland chieftain'might have
been in the days when Black Roderick passed
round the fiery cross, and when the heather was
the home of an indomitable and warlike race.’
Mr. Napier’s startling costume, it appears, is
designed as a protest against Scotland’s ind’ivi-
duality being covered up by so one-sided and re-
strictive title as ‘ England ’ where the whole island
is meant.
“ A bona-fide lord is living a secluded life at a
fashionable St. Kilda hostelry. The noble hermit,
35 years of age, is in poor health, and attended by
a valet (who transacts all his business) and a
medical attache. He dines in his own rooms, and
is so exasperatingly exclusive that, last week, a
lady-boarder rigged up in cap and apron ’to
invade and reconnoitre the noble quarters. She
reports that the invalid’s surroundings are quite
commonplace, with the exception of travelling
appurtenances and toilet fittings—which are
aesthetic. Unfortunately, she forgot to bring
away a full description of his lordship’s tooth-
brush.
“ Matthew Davies was recently asked to deliver
a lecturette to the Try Excelsior boys at South
Yarra. The subject chosen for the discourse bore
the edifying title‘Honesty.” The discourse was
most convincing.
“ Lecturer Naylor, of Ormond College, Melb.
University, is one of the distingud beaux in Mel-
bourne, very much after the style of Chas. Ryley
in ‘dear Charley’s’ best days. The Prof.’s for-
get-me-not blue eyes, with luxuriant lashes, are a
religion among girl-graduates, and at Toorak
Sunday-high-teas they have been likened to twin
suns shining upon heaps of slain. One eye alone
suffices to kill at first shot. Also, Orpheus’ lute
was a tin-whistle compared to the magic of his
singing, and the figure of Adonis sinks into
nothingness beside his fine scholastic proportions.
It was this dilettante who ran the Greek play
Aristophanes’ ‘Birds,’ at Ormond, last year. And,’
ye gods ! he is a bachelor, and at large.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
A chapter from the history of the mad
New Guinea rush. From the log of a vessel just
arrived in Cooktown from Woodlark Island (an
alleged goldfield off the New Guinea coast)
Left Woodlark Island for Samarai on the 21 st March
with 40 passengers, most of whom crawled to the vessel
stricken with fever and dysentery. Just after leaving a
miner named Kennedy, hailing from Castlemaine (Vic )
died, and was buried at sea. On the following day, just
after the bunal of Kennedy, his mate named M‘Donald
hailing from same place, died. . . On the Ist April a
miner named Boyce, from Castlemaine, died and was
buried at, sea. . John Smith, an old Starcke miner,
died at 11 o clock on 6th instant. A miner named Rarw-
stone, late of Croydon, died on I.3th inst. While the
vessel was anchored at Cape Melville, Wm. H. Grant,
22, a well-known northern compositor, and an old man
named Dwyer died. . . One man named Dale went
mad on the voyage. . . Every two out of three men at
Woodlark are sick, and they are dying fast.
Ihe periodical accident has happened to
the peace of Europe, and at latest advices the
Greek and the Turk were fighting each other on
their mutual frontier, with all manner of possible
results ahead. The present state of things is a
cheerful satire on what is called the “ concert of
the Powers.” These six overwhelming nations
gave orders to the Turks for six years, off and on
to do certain reforms in Crete, and the reforms
never eventuated. When the latest Cretan in-
surrection began they gave orders to the com-
batants to cease butchering each other, and they
went right on and butchered just the same. The
Greek invasion followed, and the Powers gave
positive orders, at least a dozen times, that the
-Hellenes should take an immediate denar*
ture, notwithstanding which they still re-
main. And after many stern commands
that the Greeks and Turks should keep the peace
on trie border, these two nations have now started
a splendid free fight, while the Powers looks on
aghast. If Greece and Turkey are left to settle
things by themselves, the utter knock-out of the
former is quite certain ; numbers are heavily
against Greece, and the Ottomans, man for man,
can wipe the floor with the Athenians of the pre-
sent day. Possibly, however, an insurrection
in Macedonia, or a declaration of war from
bervia or Bulgaria or Rou mania or Montenegro
or all four at once, may take the Turks in the rear
and make a diversion. Also, there is always a
possibility that the Young Turkey party may rise
in Constantinople, and wipe out Abdul-Hamid
11., and put some fresh lunatic on the throne in his
place, and thus make a change of scene. Moreover
the usual end of troubles in Turkey is the swarm-
mg of a Russian army over the frontier, and if
Russia steps in the present appearance of things
is that the other Powers are too much flabber-
gasted to interfere. The “concert of the Powers”
seems to consist of five bewildered paralytics hold-
ing each other up and Russia looking on.

a high Vic. police official for a peccadillo before
the Flood.
Louis Becke’s next volume of S.S. life is
to be entitled “ Pacific Tales.” Fisher Unwin is
to publish it in May.

reminder to Kilmorey—who, by the way, calls
himself “Kilmurray.”

who edited a Sydney weekly a few years ago, is
now a travelling palmist in N.S.W. backblocks.
A Sydney Chow, who lately left Sydney
for the Flowery Land, built up a fortune of .£BOOO
in 10 years, from the humble beginning of a walnut-
pedlar.

vention, five have been either professional jour-
nalists or regular paid contributors to newspapers,
