and will leave Sydney for England per R.M.S.
Rome.
An ex-policeman, just returned from
Westralia, says that the first man he met on
arrival in Perth from the goldfields was a quon-
dam able metropolitan magistrate of “ genial ”
habits, who offered to carry hiß baggage for a fee
of sixpence.
The first man of Australasian birth to
occupy a seat in the British Commons was Chis-
holm Anstey, a Tasmanian and a barrister, elected
for Mallow, Ireland, in 1847, as a supporter of
Smith O’Brien.

Todd is outdone by Dr. William

Medicine, and Bachelor of Surgery,
and practises both at the bedside
and the bar. W.L.M. is the

shop and Block rendezvous.”
Horan, the Southern Cross
(W.A.) station-master who told
Gov. Jerrysmith to mind his own
business when the latter ventured
to interfere in a detail of railway
management, has been dismissed,
ostensibly for rudeness to another
passenger. At his valedictory ban-
quet at Southern Cross recently,
Horan ascribed his dismissal to
the Governor’s resentment, and
said that he would have still been
in the service but for his encounter
with vice-royalty. Nor was the
contention denied.
Ex - Commissioner Speight
nearly lost his life recently—an
incident unnoticed by the Wes-
tralian press. He had been com-
missioned to look over the timber
areas of one of the many timber
companies. With others he was
riding on a truck when the engine
bumped into another train on the
same line—quite a common occur-
rence in those parts. Speight was
thrown between two trucks and
picked up unconscious, his head
very badly cut. He was, at latest,
to be seen in the streets of Perth
with his face like a badly-damaged
paper-plan of bis own Victorian
railway-system
Memoriesof a famous divorce
case were revived, in Melbourne
the other day, by the announce-
ment that W. J. Malpas had died.
Malpas of yore was a cold, hard,
uncanny - looking land-owner,
whose wife had been a faithful
partner in their struggling days.
When prosperity came, the man
ceased to appreciate his jolly,
warm - blooded spouse, and she
lapsed into “indiscretion.” Malpas
got his divorce, but a sympathetic
Judge (Higinbotham) awarded the
wife substantial alimony. In after
years Malpas turned to share-
broking, and was the chief pro-
moter and the first manager of
Melb. Stock Exchange Club.
The lay staff of one Australian denomina-
tional paper is made to begin each day’s jour-
nalistic work with prayer.
W. C. Rodgerson, P.M. of Glen Innes, who
died last week, was some quarter of a century ago
an appreciated Sydney pressman. He devoted
himself later to the country press, but finally
settled down as Beak.

Boucaut (S.A.) is an improvement on the per-

N.S.W. has had seven Presidents of Leg.
Council and eight Speakers of Leg. Assembly. Of
the Presidents only Lackey, the sitting one, sur-

“Billy” Grimes, the Brisbane draper,
whose will was proved at over <£16,000 the other
day, is to have a monument to commemorate him
in an English church. He was the first Queens-
lander baptised into the Congregational Church.
Painter Arthur Streeton, writing from
Cairo to a Victorian artist-chum, enthuses over
Egyptian surroundings, and meditates making a
stay there. Streeton’s “at homeness ” in the
gorgeousness of Cairo color is easily understood, as
his best pictures display more Oriental than Aus-
tralian coloring.
Cecil Rayne, whom Chief-Justice Darley
had flogged last week at Tamworth, wore, it
seems, a black coat—and even spectacles. He was
a Sunday-school teacher and lay preacher, and his
offence was, of course, criminal assault on a young
girl. The first “genteel” person N.S. Wales
has ever excoriated!
Mrs. Goodwin, widow of the once well-
known Dr. John Goodwin, died last week at
Scone (N.S.W.), aged 87. Dr. Goodwin was one
of the first to take up residence at Scone, as far
back as 1837, and for many years performed the
combined duties of doctor, parson, politician,
alderman, and constable.

Henry Ayers, six times Premier of S.A.,
and, outside vice-regal domains, the only G.C.M.G.
in Australia, will be 76 on May 1. He was, for
37 consecutive years (1857 to 1894), a member of
the S.A Leg. Council, over which he was president
for 13 years. He came to S.A. 57 years ago and
made his pile out of Burra Burra copper.
James Fletcher and W. B. Dailey, whose
statues have been unveiled—one in Newcastle and
one in Sydney—within a few days of one another,
were cordially intimate for many years. One
speech written by Dailey for the use of a politician
whose lungs were sounder than his brains, passed
through Fletcher’s hands to the noble Stentor.
levers, of Melb. Corporation, informed
his fellow-councillors last week of how he was an
English soldier in Spain when the news arrived of
Victoria’s accession to the Throne, whereupon he
helped to raise “ a shout which rang over the
mountains of Valencia.” For sixty years he has
kept it bottled up, and now he is ready to shout
once more.
N. : “Re Way. Wrixon, after arguing in
Ah Toy v. Musgrove before the Privy Council,
expressed his surprise at the offhand way in which
its pleadings were conducted—no -wigs or gowns,
no oratory, mere conversation. All the legal
fuss and feathers are merely to hoodwink the
public. The highest. English Court is just an
American Court, in practice.”
The Vic. Cabinet stipulation, in recom-
mending the commutation of Phillips’ death-
sentence, that the full term of 20 years shall be
served, is not only contrary to usual practice, but
in itself practically futile. A to-day’s Cabinet
stipulation in a matter of a prisoner’s punishment
has as much effect on a Cabinet of 15 years hence
as would a stipulation on a tariff question.
Is military enthusiasm an indication of a
weak mind ? When Captain Pursy Chirnside for-
warded to the Vic. Government his cheque to cover
the exes, of an Easter encampment, he threw in a
“funny” stipulation that the Govt, astronomer
should be “ directed to arrange for fine weather.”
It is distressing to think That a humorist of this
description oan be born with the power to pay a
solid =£2ooo for his little joke.
Rather convincing testimony to the steady
business character of the Federal Conventioners,
as a body, was supplied by the boss of a leading
hotel in Adelaide, who said, “The blankers don’t
spend a shilling.” Boniface’s notion of a high-
class delegate was, seemingly, a man with plenty
of-public spirit in him— i.e., public-house spirit—
and lots of leisure time for exchanging political
opinions with the bar nymphs.
The most persistent litigants in N.S.W.
are the two Lucases, father and son, who allege a
long-standmg grievance against veteran Neeld in
connection with a Wyalong mining-claim. Their
case, in one form or other, has been heard of
almost weekly for three or four years past. The
two plaintiffs dispense with lawyers—and only
budge at Equity jurisdiction, where small techni-
calities have more than once tripped them up.

organisation, on selecting Hales
as president, had appointed its
officials for a short term only, as
some of them were unfit to be at
large for half the period. Like-
wise, Hales went to the Miner
office and the pair indulged in
hair-pulling and scratching until
the comps, pulled Hales off, and
he was subsequently fined in the
police-court, while each side
whooped about its victory in its
respective paper. But such mat-
ters are easily adjusted in Cool-
gardie.
A “ parcel ” of 50 Australian
landscape paintings, by Curtis,
destined for the Stanford Univer-
sity (Cal.), is pretty venomously
criticised by the fraternity of
Melbourne artists. Mr. Stanford,
of Melbourne, has for years past
had Curtis in constant commis-
sion to paint Australian bush
scenery from a matter-of-fact
point of view, and these 50 can-
vasses are intended to show the
natural characteristics of the
country to students at the Uni-
versity aforesaid. As educational
works of the class of art which
puts no strain on imagination Cur-
tis’s landscapes are more or less
admirable. Also, the fact that
they represent a big cheque for
the producer is likely to have a
chastening effect upon many
envious snorters whose pictures
please nobody at all. It may
encourage those hungry impres-
sionists to paint something that
pleases somebody, even if it be
only a signboard picture.
Defaulter Craddock, of the Katoomba,
distinguished himself at Hobart by attending the
Anglican Synod’s sessional meeting and delivering
addresses on the amount of good he was doing
among the crews of the fleet. His earnest manner
carried conviction to the hearts of many. Some
of his hearers were moved almost to tears, and the
grey-beards pointed to him as a worthy example
to all young men. There is little doubt that the
unfortunate officer utterly lost his mental balance
over his love-affair.
Gov. Buxton’s aide, Capt. Guise, is one of
the goodiest of young men. He takes parcels of
tracts with him on country journeys. On a re-
cent trip up the Murray in the Government
steamer, he distributed tracts at each stopping-
place. At one settlement he made kind enquiries
after the soul of a deaf bush man. “ Well, sir,”
said the man, who thought he was referring to the
farm, “ the drought and the rabbits have played
the very devil with it this year.”
“ Constant Reader,” to The Bulletin :
Having seen in your issue of 10th April the reproduc-
tions of ’Frisco prison portraits of Butler-Harwood-Ashe,
I am now thoroughly satisfied that he is the man who
was employed by the Coolgardie police as private detec-
tive in the hope of the speedy arrest of the perpetrator
of the Broad Arrow, or Bardook, murder. Without a
doubt it is the same man, and no doubt the portraits will
be at once recognised in the West.
This story does not much impress this paper,
which lets it go just for what it is worth.
Old George Throssel, the new Westralian
Lands Minister, is pretty deaf, but not always so
deaf as his friends think him. A young man was
introduced to him a day or two after he took
office, with the intimation that Throssel was as
“ deaf as h—.” The new Minister let the young
man roar and yell congratulations alternately, but
brought the y. m. and the introducer up with a
round turn by saying, in tones of Throsselian sar-
casm, “ Yell a bit louder, old chap—l’m as deaf
as h— ! ”
One convivial crowd in Adelaide shocked
Brother Brunker unspeakably. The good man who
never smoked, drank, or swore, told off to reply to
a toast at some sort of side-show, dropped into
Biblical language, and, in tones which would make
a nigger-comedian’s fortune, hoped that the founda-
tions of the Commonwealth would be “upon the
rock.” The crowd, a fairly merry one, thought
the old man was giving them burlesque, and
laughed till the speaker’s pained surprise put on
the brake.
The executors of the late Bishop Tyrrell,
who died in Newcastle (N.S.W.) 20 years ago, are
nourishing their diocesan fund in a very worldly-
wise way. Last year they bought 4000 acres of
land just over the Queensland border, and the year
before they bought 5000 acres. They intend to
increase these blocks till the whole reaches 30,000
or 40,000 acres. Meanwhile, they are stocking
heavily with sheep and cattle, and they reckon
that when their design is completed the Newcastle
Diocese will be as independent of church donations
as any lay enterprise.

hear thal; Rosebery, chagrined over his snub by
Princess Victoria of Wales, is trying to get a
Parliamentary Act to make him plain Mr. Prim-
rose, in order that he may tackle the Commons ? "

POLITICAL
POINTS
Port Augusta (S.A.) is the last place up to
date that announces itself as the only possible site
for a Federal capital.
One of the icicles around the Federal
Convention was the little crowd of defeated S.A.
candidates who wandered like lost souls about the
corridors. Their gloom gave visitors the shivers.
The latest aspect of sectarian fury. In
Brisbane Courier the query was recently pro-
giunded: —“ If a Presbyterian minister like
uchanan is to be appointed Immigration Lec-
turer, why not appoint also clerical representa-
tives of the Anglican and R.C. churches?”
Maoriland having a surplus this year,
Herald points out in a leading article that
the plain rule ”in such cases is to reduce taxa-
tion. A much plainer rule would be to do the
road-making out of revenue instead of with bor-
rowed money. When is that kind of rule coming
along ?
H- : The \ ic. militia needs exactly such
a reform as that wrought by Graham Berry,
while Premier, in the police, abolishing the cadet
style of officer—cadet is mostly the comparative
of cad—and allowing every private soldier to
carry the marshal s baton in his knapsack, as
Napoleon said.”
ic. Governor Latrobe, as far back as
1843, ukased for the right of the community to
unimproved land-values, when he ultimatumed
the pioneer Henty family at Portland : “ You
shall be allowed a pre-emption of such lands as
are actually under cultivation and fenced, at the
amount which it may be estimated that they would
realise without any improvements upon them.”
The Single-taxers at the S.A. Federal
Convention elections trailed right away at the
bottom of the poll, but they are having their
revenge. Under their auspices, every day a
sandwich-man ” parades up and down in front
of Parliament House, Adelaide (where the dele-
gates are sitting), between two boards, on which
are written : “Land values taxation is the true
basis of federation,”
A very nice point was raised over the invi-
tation of the Convention delegates to Broken Hill.
Though local people took the initiative, the S.A.
Ministry issued the invitations, with the result
that not a single N.S.W. man, except J. T.
Walker (who had cut himself off from the others),
went up. Most of the others who weren’t too
busy “couldn’t accept the S.A. Govt.’s invitation
to visit N.S.W. territory” !
Trenwith s defence of the maintenance of
the secrecy of the Convention Committees came
as a surprise. In effect he said that members
might feel compelled to argue in favor of a com-
promise in the committee which they dared not
support in public—surely a pretty severe reflection
upon the intelligence of the public. And a strong
argument, too, in favor of letting the public see
the mechanism behind the Cons’tooshun.
The elections for the S.A. Leg. Council,
and also the contests to fill the vacancies caused
by the death of George Ash and the insolvency of
A. L. Harrold, take place Sat., May 22. The re-
tiring Councillors are President Baker, John
Warren, W. Haslam and John Darling (Tory),
Guthrie and Kirkpatrick (Labor), Addison
(Liberal) and Dr. Magarey, a one-time Liberal
driven from grace by the hospital dispute.
Bananaland is perhaps the most resource-
ful of the Australian provinces. It has countless
acres of rich sugar land yet untouched ; a whole
El Dorado in the reefs of Carpentaria—and back
country of limitless resources. The other day
selectors took up about a million acres round
about Hughenden and Longreach, and nobody
made any particular fuss over the transaction.
It was only withdrawing a pannikin full from the
artesian bore.
J.T. Walker was very wroth at not being
chosen on the Federal finance committee, and he
refused to give evidence before it. He blames
Lyne for jockeying him out of the place. Walker
and Peacock were the only laymen on the judicial
committee, and Peacock said to the writer : “As
we shall be the only members who will not have
our eyes more or less on the fat billets we are
creating, it will be our mission to keep the salaries
and the perquisites from being fixed too high.”
Old Adye Douglas, of Tasmania, foaming
over his white beard in the Federal Convention :
“ The men who have been elected in Victoria all
represent one party. They don’t represent the
people. The candidates who represent the people
are those who were rejected. Tasmania is inde-
pendent. I have lived there 60 years. lam still
sound in mind and body. Victoria is going back-
wards. She is losing her population. She can’t
pay her debts as Tasmania can. They are a lot of
insolvents in Victoria. We don’t want any rene-
gades here.”
It looks as if “ the
Diamond Baubilee” would
express the situation.
Ex-Judge M‘Farland
proposes to stand for St.
Leonards at next N.S.W.
general election. Mr.
M‘Farland had better save
himself the trouble.
AUSTRALIA IM-
PERATRIX.
Speaking at the A.N.A. ban-
quet held at Melbourne re-
cently, Vic. Acting - Premier
Best informed the excited
Natives gathered around the
board that “their destiny was
in their own hands,” and that
“ they could help to huild up
the greatest power the world
had ever seen.” Whoop !
Away with your stories
of classical glories that
musty old Tories in col-
leges tell! —
Of Empires so splendid,
o’er earth that extended
—long busted andended!
They make me unwell.
Egyptian, Assyrian, Par-
thian, Tyrian and Graeco-
Illyrian—all are in-
terred.
Each was a world’s won-
der, and tore round like
thunder ; but all have
gone under —I give you
my word !
Though proud was old
Rome in her day, and
her women a terror to
foemen, North, South,
East and West,
A State shall rise greater
below the Equator—
you’ll get no tip straigh-
ter from young Bobby
Best.
Then here’s to Australia in
crown and regalia, and
paraphernalia of red,
white, and blue !
Though not Latin’s our
lingo we’ll show ’em, by
jingo, an Empire—yell
dingo, and jump kan
garoo !
Henry Dobson, the most frankly Con-
servative member of the Federal Convention
(likewise the most effective speaker among the
Tasmanians), established a reputation as the most
convivial of the fifty. Trenwith, the only Labor
man, established a counter reputation as about
the most terribly in earnest and mirth-despising
of the crowd. But, then, Trenwith is far from
well. _ His obvious astonishment at G. H. Reid’s
occasional misplaced levity and Gordon’s sparkling
flippancy was a thing to admire.
After all, N.S.W. Parliament is to sit
during Premier Reid’s absence. The Premier
isn’t all fat: he’s mostly elastic.
The church petitions about “the recogni-
tion of God ” evidently made a deep impression
on the Federal Convention. The drafting com-
mittee spent all Sunday (April 11) in drawing up
the new Bill.
FOOTBALL IS IN.
THE GOAL-SNEAK : “ Have you got me picked for next Saturday?”
THE LONG ’U N: “ No; you couldn’t get a goal while I hed my boot in
that bloke’s eye last Saturday.”
THE GOAL-SNEAK : Well, if I hadn’t been there to hold him while you
stood on him you wouldn’t have get on very well. So that’s
no reason.”
Mulhall : “In the matter of earnings
Australia comes first, United States second, Great
Britain third, Canada fourth, and France fifth.”
Figures are very delusive, for the Frenchman, who
comes fifth in this list, probably stands first in
material comfort. “Further,” says Mulhall, “if
occasion required, the colonies could probably pay
taxes to the extent of 20 millions, or double what
they pay now.” The which statement will pro-
bably fill the hearts of Australia’s borrowing poli-
ticians with joy unspeakable.

Ihe dreadfully smart railway men of
Maoriland some months ago sold a whole lot of
their ancient and decayed rolling-stock to the
placid fossil in charge of the W.A. lines at very
nearly its value when new, and the M.L. depart-
ment still wakes up in the night to laugh over the
deal Recently the bottom fell out of one of the
mouldered horse-trucks attached to the Coolgardie
express, and the train reached the next station
with a horrid mass of mangled horse-meat trailing
behind it. It might just as easily have been a
passenger-carriage, and the meat been a syndicate
that had come out to invest half-a-million of Bull’s
money in wild-cats.
Some patriot should insist that the £lO,OOO
a year which the Convention decrees as salary to
the Governor-General of the Federation should be
defined as covering salary, allowances, perquisites
and everything. Otherwise, the bill may mount
to another .£lO,OOO. Gov. Barkly (Vic.) drew
£lO,OOO salary, but ran up an extra .£5OOO as
allowances. N.S.W. Governors have long been
drawing .£7500 as salaries, but until the last
couple of years an average of .£2500 a year was
added for extras. The U.S. President runs his
fixed salary up to <£25,000 a year by a long string
of allowances and incidentals. Carington charged
for Ins toilet-soap and stove-blacking. The amount
of salary should be fixed and stuck to.
Ireas. Holder (S.A.) is of the half-hundred
Conventioners about the smallest federalist. He
it was who first suggested in Convention that the
post and telegraphs—local services, at any rate—
should not be handed over to the Federal Govt.
He pointed out that the S.A. department pays
and the other provinces’ don’t. In his reply Bar-
ton reserved his choicest invective for Holder.
.No wonder the S.A. department pays when it
charges 2d. on a town letter and 2s. to send a
wire to Sydney,” he exclaimed. Gordon and Dr.
Lock burn, who share a good many of Holder’s
views, at once came to the latter’s assistance ; and
Barton showed more warmth than at anv time
before or since in open convention.

This makes the sixth Victorian paper which has
risen, in the few weeks since a surplus be-
gan to look reasonably certain, to demand an
immediate reduction of taxation. Victoria has
2f millions of accumulated deficit to pay off
before it can honestly begin to reduce anything.
It has had six years of deficit. And at the first
sign of a £50,000 surplus a yell goes forth for the
immediate sacrifice of £1168,000 of revenue. The
blessed policy of self-reliance again.

At Poverty Point .
Maggie Moore’s letters from London wear a broad, an-
eouraging smile characteristic of the writer. All the ex-
Australian artists whom she saw on the stage, or who
visited her at the hotel, were looking “charming” and
getting on splendidly, and doing immense credit to their
native land. A Sydney girl has started business as a
dressmaker, or milliner, at Hanover-street, and she also
has “ lovely rooms, beautifully fitted up, and is doing
splendidly.” Maggie’s letters beam upon everything
Australian, and don’t seem to find much to admire in any-
thing else.
The advent of Phil Newbury and a few other high-grade
artists at the variety ’alls should lead to a spread of
ballad-singing and the enlistment of supporters for the
concert proper. The programme at a recent Sydney
Town Hall show by very ordinary persons did not include
a single English composition. Singularly enough, finished
artists, to whom the high-falutin’ arias are child-play, are
practically the only people who condescend to warble
simple ballads in a language which their audiences under-
stand.
A piquant Yankee variety artiste, now in Adelaide,
sings a song in which she selects two members of the
audience, an old and a young man, and warbles verses to
them, designating them “darlings,” “duckies,” and other
endearing things, generally poking amatory borak at
them. Some Conventionists, having heard the song, en-
ticed Premier Turner and Peacock to the show and
planted them in the front row. Turner’s horror at being
invited (in song) to take the young lady in short frocks
on to his knee and press her to his heart, was beyond ex-
pression ; but Peacock guffawed so riotously that the
singer broke down, and there was an interval for laugh-
ing. Turner wished he had stopped at home to draft
bills.
S.S. : “Dropped in to see the Caledonian amateurs, at
Melb. Alexandra, Saturday night week, and am now sure
that a steam-hammer could not get a joke into the aggre-
gate Scotch skull. ‘Rob Roy,’ as performed by these
amateurs, was a shrieking joke, and a large, canny Cale-
donian audience took it like pills, and between the acts
great, grey, prosperous Scotchmen shook hands enthusi-
astically at the top of the stairs, and said, ‘ Eh, mon, it’s
gran’ ! ’ as they went away in long links to feed their
patriotism with whisky. The amateur performing for
charity is liable to criticism, I take it. There are some
sins charity cannot cover. It is good for a critic to see a
performance of this sort, in order to appreciate the excel-
lencies of the worst possible pro. Davy Gaunson’s bitterest
enemy should have seen him covertly sneaking through
the part of Francis Osbaldistone, afflicted with a horrible
distrust of his clothes, and burdened with 700 pairs of
hands, and as many conflicting feet. The Rob Roy looked
freshly varnished, and fat, and red, like a bran-new whisky
advt., and remained so through all tribulations to the
sweet end. But most terrible of all was the corpse-like
gravity of the great audience, suggesting the performance
of a mad burlesque in a graveyard.”
The most refreshing item in the amateur performance
of “ Rob Roy,” at Melb. Alexandra, was provided on the
last night, when a privileged spectator, forced by the
unlicensed crowd behind the scenes, broke through the
scenery, and stood disclosed to the audience in the midst
of the boiling torrent, more unconcerned than ever a
Scotchman was before in so much water.
As to a South Sea opera, Mr. F. A. Packer, the com-
poser, writes :—“ This appeared in last Bulletin: ‘J. O.
Williamson is looking for a theme for next Xmas. There’s
an opera ready-made in any South Sea village, with the
pathetic minor strains and wild original dances of the
islanders set to an accompaniment of the roar of the surf
on the coral-reef and incessant rustling of the palms.’
Singularly enough, I had anticipated the suggestion,
having had a charmingly-written sketch of such an opera
sent me by an Australian ; and I have actually written
part of it, though interrupted in so doing by other com-
posing and an attack of illness. Speaking of the libretto
itself, I don’t think anything prettier could be put on the
stage ; but the question arises—how is a composer to get
paid for such a work ? One good song ‘ pays’ better than
a whole opera consisting of many numbers and much
thought.”
X.Z. : “Italian magician mind-reader Pagilani, who re-
cently stormed Paris, has been betrayed by a confederate.
Pagilani’s specialty was answering from the platform
questions conveyed in a whisper to an assistant moving
about among the audience. Crowds of scientists and
university professors and the like were utterly mystified.
Yet the explanation was a very simple application of the
telephone principle. Pagilani wore a thick wig flowing
down over his ears, and the reason of that flowing down
was that over each ear was a telephone-receiver connected
to a wire which passed under his clothes to the sole of his
boot. He always stood in a particular spot on the stage,
which meant that the wire-end in his boot made a circuit
with other wires passing under the floor of the hall, and
ending in contact points at certain places in the floor
known to his assistant. The assistant wore a scientific
white shirt (?) rigged up to act as the diaphragm of a tele-
phone transmitter, from which a wire passed down to his
boot-sole. Result : audience whispered at level of shirt-
front, sound conveyed by wire to contact point, thence by
wire under fi jor to Pagilani’s contact point, thence by his
wire to the receivers under his wig. If ever you want a
muddleheaded jury when conjuring, take a crowd of pro-
fessors and Supreme Court judges. They will be so
anxious to detect the obscure that they will miss the
obvious.”
A Hindoo yogi—some kind of priest—is now showing at
London Aquarium. This gentleman has bones as pliable
as indiarubber, and ties himself into knots so wonderful
that the Rontgen rays (which reveal no malformations)
are unable to explain the phenomenon. One of his favorite
exercises is to ioin his hands and play skipping-rope with
himself.
Far and away the best illusion ever thought out is the
Appearing Lady, now witching Europe under the wand
of Buatier de Kolta. There is a smallish table with four
legs. Kolta whisks a cloth on it, and leaves the edges of
the cloth off the floor, so that the audience can see under.
The cloth begins to bulge up, and a lady stands on the
table. No mirror in this trick. Scientific American
explains. Under the stage an assistant pulls the lady up
through a trap with a windlass. Kolta, arranging the
cloth, allows this to be done. She is kneeling in a kind
of cage, which crunches up as she leaves it. The windlass
wires are in the table-legs. The carpet and table-cover
patterns ingeniously fit the traps.
There is a legal process called writ of capias (ca. re)
on which a debtor can be detained in one province if
he shows a disposition to leave for another. The other
week a certain literary man drove both the capiater and
his attendant sheriff’s officer quite daft by appearing at
the Melbourne station barrier for six days in succession
(whilst the “ writters ” were on the platform watching the
departing Adelaide trains) and taunting them thus :
First day, “Grand day for travelling, boys, isn’t it?
Not going to-day. Sorry to have troubled you. Tra-la.”
Second day : “Ah, ah, you sly dogs! Enjoying your-
selves, I see, looking at the pretty girls 1 Eh ? See you
to-morrow, dear boys.” Third day : “ Well, well! you are
attentive : come again to-morrow, do, or I shall be so dis-
appointed.” And so on. As the literary man anticipated,
the bloodhounds “chucked it” the next week, and he
took train safely on the eighth day to Adelaide, whence
he considerately wired both of them announcing his arri-
val there and “‘thanking them from his innermost heart
for their enthusiastic appreciation of his society during
his most enjoyable stay in Melbourne.” Since which he
has paid up voluntarily.
John Gourlay lent a Melb. Rob Roy amateur the other
night the bonnet worn by Corbet Ryder, Gourlay’s
grandfather, the original Rob Roy. Sir Walter Scott
was at the first performance, which made more stir than
anything on record in Edinburgh. But even in a house
packed with Austral-Scotties, the bold outlaw won’t gee.
“ The second night was cold haggis,” says a contem-
porary.

Austin Brereton, sometime famed and defamed in
Australia for his childlike and “ Blande ” criticisms, is
sec of the dramatic section of the Victorian Era Exhibi-
tion, London, and chief of the press bureau. Brer Brere-
ton threatens magniloquently “ to trace, with painstaking
care and accuracy, the progress of the stage from its
actual and metaphorical darkness up to its present splen-
dor ” The present writer contemplates also a marine
store section to similarly trace the advance of that pro-
fession, from the time when Tubal Cain bought Naaman
and Nebuchadnezzar’s cast-off armor at scrap-iron rates
right up through the chiffonier and bottle-oh eras to the
present Henry V. Rignoldian tin-plate splendor.

" From Rev. Dr. Strong’s paper, the Australian Herald:
“ The Australian heroine and the Australian villain are
growing in popularity on the stage, and in fiction. The
premier British dramatist, Mr. Henry Arthur Jones,
makes the bewitchingly wicked temptress in his latest
play, 1 Michael and His Lost Angel,’ an Australian native.
Mrs. Audrie Leslie, who is rich, beautiful, and possessed
of more than her share of original sin, is the daughter of
an Australian millionaire. She sets herself to tempt a
high-church clergyman, and to bring about his downfall
from the pedestal of virtue on which he has set himself.
A few years back, a dramatist would have made such a
character a French woman. But fashion changes. The
French villainess gives place to the enterprising, attrac-
tive, clever, and bound-to-be-successful Australian native.
The dramatist knows his public, and, judge of character
as he is, he is aware that if he puts an Australian villainess
into his play, the audience will take her success as a
matter of course, since success and Australian nativity
are pretty well synonymous. Hence it is that this country
seems to have been appointed purveyor of frozen meat
and ehoice villainy to the British Empire.”

Kyrle Bellew, the actor, is related to Gov. Gormanston
of Tasmania. Gormariston has been married twice. His
first wife was a daughter of Patrick Bellew, an Irish peer
and baronet of County Louth. Bellew’s father’s name
was Higgins, but being left some property by one of his
kinspeople, the Bellews, he took their name.
Superfine critic William Archer, in his book, “About
the Theatre,” is rather rough on the veteran Paulton,
soon to be with us. He gives credit to the late H. B.
Farnie, authdr of some awfully-stupid burlesques, for a
talent that was worthy of better things, and then he goes
on to instance Paulton, the part-auihor of “Niobe,” as
one who has shown “no fitness for better things.” The
Bulletin, in its ignorance of the work which Farnie might
have done, but didn’t, is disposed to reckon Paulton
the smarter show-writer of the two. “ Niobe,” although
it may be only a burlesque of Gilbert’s “ Pygmalion and
Galatea,” is not less elegant, in its farcical style, than
the famous “fairy-comedy,” nor lessoriginal than Farnie’s
imitations of French opera-bouffe. “Niobe” is a well-
sustained joke. When the Greek heroine converts old
Peter Amos into Petramos, or fires off her innocent “Yes-
mum ! ” or greets the furious mother-in-law with “ Hail
to you ! ” one doesn’t ask for better things. The authors
of “Niobe” can claim to have shown what Jack Point
would call “a pretty, pretty wit.” Who made William
Archer an authority on humor, anyway ? Why, he can’t
even see the absurd side of Ibsen’s pretentious plays.
The Hero of Trafalgar is described as cutting a dull
figure in the new (and unsuccessful) play, “ Nelson’s
Enchantress.” Somebody says that Horatio, in his amour
with Lady Hamilton, is “ always repenting bitterly and
doing it again.”
In Catulle Mend6s’ one-act piece, “The Wife of Tabarin,”
a husband kills his wife on the open stage, and the blood
gushes forth. Then the remorse-stricken husband madly
kisses the wounds and pets his face boltered with blood,
and, all jrory, addresses his further rants to a delighted
N.Y. audience. And this is realism !
E.D. : “ One gets weary of the ‘ fallen ’ woman in fiction
and the drama who has repented and taken to the work
of ministering to the ‘ pore ’ as a sort of penance. Is
charity the last resort of the ruined female? We have
had her often, we get her again in ‘ A Woman’s Reason,’
where she spends two-thirds of her time in repentant
tears, and the other third in carrying soup and blankets
to the aged and afflicted. Is there no other means of
expiation open to the deceived heroine than rubbing
old Mrs. Blank’s ‘ rheumatiz,’ spoon-feeding the bed-
ridden Hodge, and reading elevating literature to deaf
paupers? When I write my celebrated drama, I shall
make the harmless, necessary fallen female a cheerful
creature who bears her sins bravely, or else set her to
scourge herself every act before breakfast, and have her
frequent fashionable haunts, and meet all the women she
knew in happier days, wearing big boots, a hat of two
seasons back, and a horribly ill-fitting, dowdy dress of
comparative antiquity. The she-sinner who soups the
poor is, I notice, always particular about the fit of her
dress and the date of her bonnet. My latter idea, as a
guarantee of good faith in the matter of repentance and
self-abnegation, is the only genuine. Besides, if things go
on as at present, it will soon be more than a woman’s
character is worth to be found ministering to the poor.”
Many of the London caf6s have theatrophones connect-
ing them with a dozen of the leading theatres and music-
halls. A dial and pointer indicate the particular show
on circuit, and by putting sixpence “ in the slot ” any
customer may hear the vocal or instrumental efforts of
his favorite comic-opera or hall artist on the stage a
mile away.
Re the “cartwheel” hat-nuisance in theatres—some-
body writes to The Stage pointing out that actresses who
attend matinees frequently make themselves conspicuous
by means of “ hats like juvenile umbrellas,” They, at
least, ought to have some regard for the feelings of the
public situated in the rear, but they haven’t. Lovely
woman is ever a selfish animal where her vanity is con-
cerned. If the man who had paid for the offending head-
gear were seated right behind her, she would refuse to
take it off to give him a view of the performance.
The theatre-fan is becoming as big a curse as the theatre-
hat. Sometimes, when it is a square yard of palm leaf
flopping about, and making a hideous row as it flicks
against a beaded bodice.it is a bit worse than the mil-
linery monstrosities.
The religious moral of “ Silver King ” is a vexed ques-
tion. Did the Lord, to whom Wilfred Denver appealed
for aid, specially ordain the destruction of the railway-
carriage in which the fugitive was travelling to Liverpool,
so that he might appear to have been burnt to ashes, or
did the Deity inspire Wilfred to travel by a train which
was about to get overtaken by a legitimate catastrophe,
and then further inspire the penitent to jump from his
doomed compartment before the catastrophe came off?
Probably the latter. Author Jones, being a serious-
minded person, wouldn’t represent Providence as a ruth-
less destroyer of half-a-dozen passengers for the sake of
giving one reformed “ drunk ” a chance to escape police
pursuit. Dear Wilfred prayed in effect: “ O Lord, show
me a way to get out of my present trouble,” and the way
was shown to him. The Lord didn't create the way
merely to oblige Mr. Denver. A Low Church parsonic
argument on this point would be deeply instructive.
Federici’s tombstone, in Melbourne cemetery, gives
no hint as to the tragic story of the actor’s death -
His stage name is quoted, within brackets, under his
proper name of Frederick Baker—born at Florence : died
at Melbourne—but the inscription doesn’t even state that
he died suddenly.
Henry Betty, son of the Infant Roscius, and a constant
benefactor to theatrical charities, died the other day in
London. This Betty inherited the fortune which his
father founded in 1803, so, as a capitalist, he was unique.
Probably there never was another man on earth who
could say that his own father had earned thousands a
year as an actor, or as anything else, 94 years ago. The
story of the original William Henry Betty, or “ Infant
Roscius,” reads like a romance. Before he got into his
teens he had “ starred ” in a round of Shakespearian
characters at Drury Lane and Covent Garden ; the town
went mad over the boy, and the great Sarah Siddons, at
50 years of age, found herself playing Lady Macbeth
to this nipper who had, for the time being, put her into
the shade. Master Betty reigned for about four years,
until he outgrew his boyhood. He then went to Cam-
bridge to complete his education, but as a young man he
reappeared upon the stage a soulless mummer, and the
public took no more interest in him. He was not a born
actor, he hadn’t even a naturally theatrical dr position ;
Betty was just a graceful, intelligent, teachable child,
possessing a good memory. Between the acts of “ Ham-
let ”he would play marbles at the wings with-a chum pro-
vided for the purpose, and at a word from the manager
slipped his marbles into the bag, and took the stage to
soliloquise or storm —just as he had been taught to
do. Fond mothers and medical men who talk about
the evils of “ straining” a child’s memory will regret to
hear that Master Betty lived to the age of 83, with all his
Shakespeare lines in his head. His son got as far as 77.

John R. Rogers, the Minnie Palmerite, intends to intro-
duce “The County Fair” to Britain, where that Mac-
Mahonic failure of Australian other days is still unknown.
John R., like the misguided Macs, before him, reckons
that)" The County Fair ” will knock them with its ridicu-
lous mechanical horse-race in the last act. He will dis-
cover his error when the time comes. The yokel-character
drawing and simple domestic - life episodes in “The
County Fair” are first-class —like those we got in Good-
win’s “In Mizzoura.” But the horse-race ! What’s the
good of attempting to boom such a hollow-sensation
device in the country which supplies us with Bland Holt’s
dramas ?

Carl Hertz is booming in Maoriland. At Wellington the
wizard had the loan of a N.Z. Times reporter, to whom
he told some stirring tales of his adventures, and next
day the local evening paper stated that a full set of false
teeth had dropped from a dress-circle lady’s mouth during
the Hertz performance, and was awaiting restoration to
the lawful owner. Agent Geach manages this branch of
the business.

Extremes meet. There is one word com-
pounded of Greek and Maori—the name Monowai,
given to the U.S.S. Co.’s greyhound. When a
Government survey was being made of the Otago
Lake district, a small lake was found with but one
creek running into it. The surveyor forthwith
christened it Monowai (Gr. monos, one; Maori
wai, water or stream).
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.*

" Trust in Providence : but keep your
powder dry ”
Oliver Cromwell’s advice to his army applies
to our every day life under almost all circum-
stances. To expect Providence to step in and
preserve us against carelessness, or total apathy, is
simply ignorance.
There comes the Express ; will Providence save
you if you remain on the metals? The fierce fire
rages in yon stairway; will Providence save you if
you refuse the proffered ladder? The huge, majes-
tic steamer is fast filling with water; will Pro-
vidence save you if you listlessly refuse the life-
boat? The epidemic of kidney disease is raging
round you; can you afford to simply trust in Pro-
vidence when there is a simple, proven, safe remedy
that thousands tell you cures—Warner’s “Safe ”
Cure? Do you think you will not be responsible
if you make no efforts to save yourself or friends?
Read the experience of the Rev. R. A. Caldwell,
Mt. Gainbier, S.A., who writes on the 22nd Dec.,
1896.—“1 have much pleasure in saying that I
have used several bottles of Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure,
for liver troubles, and derived benefit from it. I
have also heard others bear similar testimony to
the good effects reaped from the use of it. ”*
Warner’s Safe Cure is a Specific for Liver
and Kidney Disease.
F. C. : I saw a 3ft. Gippsland worm the
other day. They also grow some very fine cater-
pillars ranging from 3in. to 6in. long. But the most
extraordinary circumstance I have encountered
lately was one of these same caterpillars petrified
—or rather earbonified—with a small tree growing
out of it. They say locally that the animal some-
times dies with the seed of a tree undigested in its
stomach—if it has one—or in its mouth ; this,
springing up, grows into a tree, its roots spreading
into and filling up the worm’s body, thus making
a sort of petrified caterpillar.
Lectures to men only—Curtain ones.
Straw squeezed into solid bales in a baling
press at the rate of 25 bales per hour, is found by
Vic. farmers an excellent building material for
barns, stables, sheds, and the like. The bales do
not readily burn, are rapidly built up into firm,
solid, walls, will keep for years, and in stress of
drought may even be fed to farm stock.
“ A little girl named Florrie Norrie tell out of the train
carrying the Ballarat Orphan Asylum children on an ex-
cursion to Queenscliff this morning. An engine was
immediately dispatched by the station-master to look for
the girl, who was found under the care of some line-
repairers. She had escaped with a few bruises.” — Melb.
Paper. Florrie Norrie. The name sets one’s Muse
going.—
O begorry ! Florrie Norrie
Tumbled from a train.
No disaster—station-master
Fixed her up with sticking-plaster,
Made her “ right as rain.”
Florrie has no mother prayerful
To remind her—“ Now be careful 1 ”
Nor a father, who’d be sorry
If she died and went to Glorry ;
Hence our pleading incidental
Sung in accents fond parental—
“ O begorry ! Florrie Norrie,
Don’t fall out again!”
PROSPECTUS of the
LOCH FYNE CENTRAL (SOLD MINING COMPANY,
MATLOCK, GIPPSLAND.
CAPITAL : £15,000, in 30,000 Shares of 10s. each. (To be Registered as a No-Liability Co.)
10,000 Shares, paid up to 55., to be allotted to the Vendors ; also the sum of £5OO cash. 20,000 are offered to the public
at Is. on application and Is. on allotment. Over 0000 Contributing Shares have already been applied for by
the Vendors. Proceeds (less brokerage and flotation expenses) will be placed to the credit of the Company,
which may be registered when 10,000 Shares have been subscribed. Calls not to exceed Id. per Share per
month, power being reserved to make one call of fd. per share, if necessary, for machinery.
BANKERS : Bank of Australasia. BROKERS : Messrs. J. B. Were & Son, Trustees Buildings, 412 Collins-st., Melb.
LEGAL MANAGER (pro tern.): G. W. Smith, Baring Chambers, 64 Market-street.
rpuiS Company is being formed to purchase and develope an area of 37 acres 1 rood, held under Lease 3553, adjoining
the famous All Nations, and in close proximity to the New Loch Pyne, now one of the best mines in Victoria.
The All Nations lodes, which all the authorities agree dip into the Loch Fyne Central ground, yielded gold to the
value of £320,000, running from 930 z. per ton to 7 or 8 dwt., and paying dividends on the last-named return.
The Loch Fyne dyke is even more convincing evidence of the great richness of this locality, since, after yielding
handsome returns during the sixties, the present proprietors, New Loch Fyne Co., have won from it within the past
four years gold to the value of over £BO,OOO, of which £46,700 have been distributed in dividends, while the property,
with dyke proved 200 ft. wide, and carrying five distinct lodes, is valued in open market at £150,000.
Mr. D. B. Walker, who visited Matlock and reported on the district for the Victorian Government (ride “Our
Neglected Goldfields ”), states in a special report for this Company “ The richness of the New Loch Fyne dyke,
which traverses your ground from end to end, is now so well known as to need little notice. The All Nations claim,
from which gold to the value of a third of a million sterling was obtained close by your boundary, adjoins your lease,
and there can be little doubt their reef will be found in your ground at a reasonable depth.” “ If, as is probable, the
sandstone bar against which the All Nations reef was practically lost should prove to be the eastern wall of the Loch
Fyne dyke, the prospects of the Loch Fyne Central will be second to none among Victorian mines.” And further,
“ I cannot but think that careful, steady work will be rewarded by the discovery, at an early date, in your claim of
the rich groups of reefs known as the All Nations and Loch Fyne lodes.”
Mr. R. Blythman, J.P., describes the Loch Fyne dyke as clearly traced from east to west through this lease, and
its course well defined. He also says that “ good facilities exist for proving your dyke to a great depth at compara-
tively small outlay.” And further, “ Your tunnel is 500 ft. long, and I roughly estimate the 5 chains extension would
reach the dyke, and give 300 ft. of backs. It could then be extended on dyke course, when there is every reason to
believe that valuable shoots and reefs would be found.”
Mr. P. Fleming says “ There is a diorite dyke traversing your lease diagonally. This is the continuation of the
rich Loch Fyne, at present yielding such rich stone. The dyke enters your ground from the adjacent Loch Fyne at
the S.E. corner, where it has been prospected on the surface ; also on the eastern slope there is an ironstone and
quartz lode 28ft. wide, and carrying nice gold in the casing. Further along your eastern boundary are the workings
of the old All Nations Company, in which above 80,000 oz. of gold were won from lodes tending to and dipping into
your ground. The last stone was got by a tributing party, and gave 14300 z. from 1600 tons. Ithinklyou have here a
valuable property, splendidly situated, as it is between two such famous mines as are the All Nations and the present
dividend-paying New Loch Fyne.”
Unsolicited Praise .
GRATITUDE OF A NEWTOWN
INHABITANT.
It is an old saying that one volunteer is worth
ten pressed men, and when we receive unsolicited
testimony as to the value or efficacy of any par-
ticular article we are inclined to place double value
on such voluntary statements, more especially
when they hail from old-established and respected
men of business. It was in view of the foregoing
paragraph that a reporter interviewed Mr. Charles
Green, Provision Dealer, of 4 Alice-street, New-
town, who, for the last four years has been a
martyr to indigestion, accompanied by a dry,
hacking cough.
Our reporter, on making himself known to Mr.
Green at his pleasant home in Newtown, asked,
“ Is it true that you have been suffering forever
four years from indigestion, Mr. Green ? ”
“ It is, indeed, and it has been so bad at times
that I have often felt tempted to go up to a
stranger in the street and get him to punch me in
the back. I have often had friends to do it, and,
simple as it was, it gave me relief for a time.”
“ Did you have any other ailment at the time,
Mr. Green ? ”
“ Yes, I also suffered from a nasty cough in the
summer time ; it was also on me on and off for
about the same as the indigestion. As an example,
I would cough and immediately a lot of thick
black phlegm would fly up into my mouth., I
presume it came from my chest, on account of the
ticklish feeling I would feel there. For that I
have taken numerous concoctions, including cod
liver oil, which did me no good, but now I am
thankful to say I feel grand, and hope the cough
will not return. In one sense I miss it, as I have
been acquainted with it so long, but it is a very
welcome miss. ”
“ To what do you attribute this marvellously
happy alteration in your health, Mr. Green ? ”
“To one medicine alone, and that is Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, for from nearly
the first pill I felt relief and an improvement in
my appetite. I went steadily on taking them,
until I got as I am now, and there doesn’t seem
much the matter with me now, does there ? ”
_‘ ‘ Indeed, no, Mr. Green, you look the very
picture of health and strength.”
“ I feel so, and I assure you that I have such
unbounded faith in their restorative properties
that I have recommended them to plenty of my
customers, numbers of whom are taking them,
observing the wonderful improvement in my
health and condition. Why, I have actually
gained 141bs in weight since taking them, and I
continue to put on weight daily. I learnt of them
first through an ad vertisement in one of the papers,
and I can truthfully state that they have done
all and more than I expected them to do.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills create new blood,
build up the nerves, and thus drive disease from
the system. In hundreds of cases they have cured
after all other medicines have failed, thus estab-
lishing the claim that they are a marvel among
the triumphs of modern medical science. The
genuine Pink Pills are sold only in boxes, bearing
the full trade mark, “Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People. ” Protect yourself from im-
position by refusing any pill that does not bear
the registered trade mark around the box.
They are now obtainable of all chemists, and
from Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company, Queen’s
Place, Sydney, at 2s 9d a box, or six for 14s 9d ;
but are genuine only with full name, Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.*

irly application for same (together with a
remittance of £3 35.) should be made to
Messrs. C. D. SMITH & CO.,
Fink’s Buildings,

The Diamond Jubilee .
There can be no doubt that the English-
speaking peoples are determined to make
the forthcoming Diamond Jubilee the
memorable anniversary of the Queen’s
reign, and it seems very fitting and proper
that it should be so. Contemporary with
these six past decades, the progress and
expansion of British trade has been so
phenomenal tha
thoughtful people
naturally look for
a cause. They find
it in the fact that
the prudent, judi-
cious government
of the British Em-
pire has enabled
them to push their
enterprises far and
near in peace and
security. The pro-
tection of the Flag
has ever been
cherished as an
unique protection,
even in the re-
motest lands and
waters. Hence, no
doubt, the enthusi-
asm which the
forthcoming cele-
bration has evoked.
* * *
In Melbourne
lavish preparations
are being made to
keep up the festi-
val on a large scale.
Illuminations and
fireworks, the
founding of hos-
pitals, and special
benefactions to the
charitable institu-
tions are all in-
eluded in the programme. And the enter-
prise of a local firm (Messrs. C. 1). Smith
and Co.) is also providing a permanent
souvenir of the occasion—not only available
for Melbourne and Victoria, but for the
whole of Australasia, and one which there
can be no doubt Her Majesty would wish
her faithful subjects to possess. In fact, it
is by the special permission of the Queen
only that it could be offered, and it is the
Official Memorial of the Longest Reign.
• * •
The souvenir is a proof copy of the Jubilee
celebration in Westminster Abbey, from the
celebrated painting by W. E. Lockhart,
R.S.A., which was painted by the royal
command. The original picture, of which
the copyright represents a capital value of
THE JUBILEE CELEBRATION IN WESTMINSTER ABBEY.
£lO,OOO, is in Windsor Castle. It is the
Queen’s favorite picture, and Her Majesty
has purchased over 50 additional proofs.
The picture—which measures about 50in.
by 40in.—contains nearly 300 portraits
of historic value. These were painted
from individual sittings. The memor-
able scene in the ancient abbey is vividly
depicted.
* * * *
The life-like portraits of the greatest men
and most beautiful and distinguished women
of the age are recognised, and even minor
details of attire stand out distinctly. The
following English press notices speak for
themselves :
The Times, describing the soene in the Abbey, on
the occasion said: —“A noble pioture it is,
one that has never been equalled in this
country. . . . Grand, majestic, thrilling
to tears, and in every sense a worthy memorial
of the Golden Wedding of the Queen and the
Nation.”
The Daily Telegraph.— “ A very excellent finish
as well 4 as a refinement of execution distin-
guishes the engraving issued by Messrs. Doio,
from W. E. Lockhart’s painting. . . .
Most skilfully is the distance managed.
. . . The light streams down upon the
prominent place occupied by Her Majesty.
. . . The picture indeed commemorates an
event of which every native of the sister Isles
should be proud, also the dwellers East, South,
West and North, who are content to be safe-
guarded by the flag which belongs to the
* Queen of the Seas. ’ ”
The Scotsman.—“lt is a picture certain to be
popular.”
The Glasgow Herald.—" Of the enormous labour
involved in the painting of the Jubilee pic-
ture, few people can form any adequate idea.”
The Weekly Scotsman.—“ Mr. W. E. Lock-
hart, R.S.A., may be proud of his Royal
Jubilee Memorial Picture, so, too, may the
Royal Scottish Academy, that it possesses a
member capable of producing such an excel-
lent work. . . . The picture is truly ad-
mirable. There are no fewer than 277 figures
in it. . . . It is appalling to think of it.”
Sussex Daily News.—" It is certainly the most
remarkable work of the kind that the public
has ever seen.”
Yorkshire Post.—“ It is really a magnificent
work, thoroughly worthy of the greatness of
the occasion. ”
Liverpool Mercury.—" It fulfills the highest
expectations that had been formed of it.”
Manchester Examiner.—“ The picture far sur-
passes anything ‘ painted by Royal command ’
that I have ever seen.”
The Civil Service Guardian.—“ The picture is
a success from whatever point of view we re-
gard it.”
Society.— “ In every sense an honour to the
occasion, and a lasting Memorial of the
Queen’s Jubilee.”
Figaro.—“An achievement no other artist can
boast.”
Lady’s Pictorial. —“A valuable and unique
record of the most remarkable event in the
Victorian era . . . This really superb
historical picture. ”
Illustrated London News.- “We can honestly
congratulate the artist on the result.”
The Sportsman. —“Splendidly effective.”
Morning Post. — “ A fine pictorial record of an
ever memorable event.”
The Daily News. “In itself a gallery of por-
traits.”
New York Herald. —“ It is full of faces that
will at once be recognised as amongst the
greatest and most beautiful of living men and
women.”
The World. —“ A faithful record of one of the
most memorable and picturesque historical
occurrences in the history of the 19th
century.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Sydney Cup this year was perhaps the greatest
" boil-over ”in the history o( the race. Almost any price
could be got about the winner, Tricolor, who is by that
sterling stayer, Trident, whose stock of late have been
running very well. Kaliatina nearly brought off a well-
planned coup, though 25 to 1 was always on offer against
her. Loch Leigh, the third horse, was perhaps the most
unfanoied animal in the race, and yet he beat the well-
backed candidates—Tom, Battalion, Coil, Vanitas, etc.—
into a “ cocked hat.” Ikev and Co. completely skinned
the lamb over Doncaster and Cup.
Monday’s starting prices at Rand wick were:
4—l, 3 —l, I—3, 40—1, 3—l, 3—l, whilst, on same day, at
Onkaparinga (S.A.) the “tote” dividends panned out:
£4 155., £l2 45., £6, £3O 135., £8 195., £lO 15s. Thus,
despite the victory of outsider Tricolor in Sydney Cup,
the machine easily comes out on top.
• • • *
The sensationrl gelding, Hopscotch, added to
his unsavory notoriety by showing miraculous improve-
ment on his Hawkesbury form and winning the Autumn
Stakes in a common canter, downing, with the greatest of
ease, Battalion, Coil, True Line, and Vivian. The groans
and yells that greeted the gelding’s return to weigh in
were loud and hearty enough to almost reach the ears of
owner Long in the London hank parlor of the A. J.S. ; but
the stewards met and decided that the hooting was un-
reasonable.
The demonstration against Hopscotch, when he
got home for A.J.C. Autumn Stakes, was second only to
a previous experience of the Chipping Norton stable
when the crowd yelled, and Mr. “ Bill ’ Lons stood at the
and smiled sweetly. And, by the way, Mr. Long’s
trip ’Ome has not been quite a happy outing. He has
suffered severely from influenza, and writing to a Sydney
friend, says he would rather live in a hovel in sunny Aus-
tralia than in a palace in foggy England.
As in Epsom, so in its counterpart, the Don-
caster, the country division with its outsider, Superb,
has come to the rescue of Ikey Mo and Co., whose bags
are now fat with the shekels of the unfortunate backers
of Vanitas, Reka, Adoration, Cremorne, La Carabine, &e.
The honors of the race are Cremorne’s; he carried his
thumping impost of 9st. 91b. into second place, though,
had little Cical b ought Adoration a bit sooner, this hand-
some filly would probably have been returned winner.
The mysterious tactic i adopted by the Vanitas division
availed nothing, as that much-bepuffed animal finished
“down the course” both in’Caster and Cup. The La
Carabine people had good reasons for backing their filly,
for, although too small to successfully compete in a big
field, yet she made mincemeat of her opponents in the
First Nursery on Monday.
That marvellous nugget, Aurum, easily carried
his penalty successfully in the Champagne Stakes ; his
meeting Newhaven to-day (Thursday) will be the event of
the meeting. Newhaven will probably have to strike his
colors to the Trenton flyer if the latter is jumped off and
kept hard at it, but in a slow-run or muddling race
Newhaven will prevail.
Resolute’s “ poor feet ” have troubled him so
much that backers may have to wait some time —perhaps
till next Caulfield and Melb. Cups— to see him once more
exhibit those good racing capabilities which he is well-
known to possess. The Officer is another of this season’s
crack three-year-olds who may be heard of about Cup-
time.
Though the Leger Reserve at Randwick is
largely advertised as being under the direct control of
the A. J.C., yet flagrant cases of rampingand ticket-snatch-
ing are but too prevalent. On Doncaster day one punter
from Morpeth had his winning ticket stolen and then had
the pleasure of seeing the thief draw the money. With
no means of ascertaining- jockeys’ names, and with but a
very badly-managed scratching board, Leger patrons have
plenty to grumble at. On Cup-day there were plenty of
“ balancers ” about, and several backers of La Carabine
were thankful enough to get their own money back, with-
out any winnings.
Tricolor, the Cup-winner, with his nearest
attendants, Kaliatina and Loch Leigh, never once did a
really good Cup gallop, and yet they managed to beat the
much-fancied, over-trained moderates that opposed them.
Properly trained and ridden, True Blue and Coil could
give the winner any amount of weight and then lose him
over two miles.
In the Second Hurdle Race, Irish Stew and C.P.
appear to have good winning chances, whilst Ellengerah
and Pilatus may be expected to run well in the Welter
Mile. In the absence of Aurum, The Hypnotist or Am-
berite should secure the Easter Stakes: and the City
Handicap of a mile and a half may be better suited to
the capabilities of Vanitas than his last two trials, though
Wahine and Tom also possess winning chances.
If, as was done in same case with Marvel and
Carbine, Newhaven be pulled out in Cumberland Stakes
to day (Thursday), he should win, but, in his probable
absence, a good race should be seen among Coil, Bat-
talion and True Blue—leaving the mysterious and unre-
liable Hopscotch out of the question.
Much enthusiasm was shown at Sydney Agri-
cultural Show over the trotters and the trotting contests,
but, as usual, the trotting section was woefully misman-
aged. The Bulletin last year pointed out that Tuxedo,
winner of the stallion prize, was a pacer, and this j ear
the committee very properly barred him from competing
against the trotters. So far so good, but in the very next
item of judging the judges stultified themselves by giving
the prizes for trotting mares to Hattie S. and Charmer,
both pacers, who were merely led into the ring and never
showed their paces; and, to make matters worse, Charmer
has a hip very badly knocked down. Violetta Junior,
that beautiful mare by Childe Harold from Violetta
(imported), was passed over altogether, and the writer
ventures to say that if a vote were taken among N.S.W.
trotting men as to the best local trotting mare Violetta
Junior would easily head the poll.
Trotting stallions were poorly represented, bar-
ring that handsome little horse Prince Harold, bred,
owned, and driven by Mr. Frank Morris, who was
awarded the champion ribbon. In the contests for speed,
however, he had to cry ago to Mr. Harry Knight’s rapid
Van Cleeve mare, Siren 11., who fairly distanced all com-
petitors in harness and saddle, driven and ridden by
Knight, senior and junior respectively. As often here
pointed out, the ring is not large enough for speed con-
tests, but Siren showed a phenomenal gait round the
little track and took the crowd by storm. However,
Prince Harold is just from a season at the stud, and Is
not in racing trim.
The entries for blood mares comprised but three,
Mr. Sam Hordern taking first prize with imported
Meridian. Second was awarded to a shapely little mare,
standing about 13.3, pedigree doubtful, with foal at foot.
To award a prize to a pony mare of doubtful origin, as a
representative of the class of thoroughbreds now bred in
Australia, is playing the game very low down.
The high jump, at Sydney Show, was a very in-
teresting item. Only Rainbow, Receipt, and Frisco were
left in. Rainbow failed, also Receipt; and Frisco knocked
the bar down twice, but at third time of asking he flew it
like a bird, amidst the delighted shouts of the onlookers.
* • • *
At a recent Show a lady rider took a fractious
horse over his jumps without a mistake, and then gave
nourishment to her eight-day-old infant whilst awaiting
her next turn at the fences.

Ed. Bulletin, —Have you noticed how fre-
quently of late years the letter “a ” after the capital
letter has been successful in principal racing events in
Victoria?—Caulfield Cup: Paris, Sainfoin, Paris, Water-
fall. Newmarket: Laundress, Maluma, Carlton. Mel-
bourne Cup : Carbine, Malvolio, Tarcoola, Patron. Derby:
Camoola, Carnage, The Harvester, Wallace.

“ There are two Nortons and two Erl Kings, all
jumpers more or ’ess, running here, thanks to lax racing-
regulations,'’ writes The Bulletin's London corres-
pondent, “and the reputation of the illustrious Austra-
lian first-named makes many ignorant punters back his
English namesake, a fourth-rater, who started the other
day at tens and won. Little is heard of Daimio, and the
B.P's opinion is that Norton can smother him on the
present form of both.”

Dreadnought will be remembered as a 3-year-old
that might have won Melb. Cup if he hadn’t been care-
fully omitted from the entries. The late James White
threw away two chances of winning the Cup in ’B9, firstly
when he didn’t enter Dreadnought, secondly when he
scratched the penalised Abercorn, who at the V.R.C.
Spring meeting proved himself superior to Carbine.
• • * •
An event not carefully and fully chronicled in
the Melb. dailies was the recent abortive attempt of a
much-paragraphed detective to raid a big Collingwood
tote-shop. This time the tote-man was too cute, and the
moment the massive d’s hove in sight the doors were
closed and barricaded. For the next ten minutes a
wondrous sight was seen ; four strong detectives in their
agony lashing into big red gum gateposts with axes, but
by the time the obstruction was demolished and the place
rushed, the whole of the crowd inside had escaped by the
secret exits, and had gathered in the road, chaffing
the crude axemanship of the sleuthhounds.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—Your London correspondent,
who says “ Most billiard-bailers are aweary of the spot-
barred, 1 push ’-playing champion who will have his own
rules and stipulations,” speaks for a very small section of
the billiard world —the section that has little practical
knowledge of its subject, and can’t appreciate Roberts’
cannon practice which has brought English billiards into
touch with the Franco-’Murkan game. Hear what the
Referee says about the Roberts-Peall controversy :
“ There may be forty championships set up, with as many
different holders, and Roberts’s name missing from the
lists. This will leave John Roberts what he is—in short,
John Roberts, a class by himself. He can, till he loses his
power of execution, command the highest terms of any
professional, and you can’t find a sounder test of excel-
lence than that.” Roberts traded on his pre-eminence as
a spot-stroker until he found other fellows, also capable
of putting down the red hundreds of times in succession,
equal to himself—for show purposes. Then he took to
the all-round game and developed the push-stroke. The
other fellows have tried to come near him at this— and
failed. — Melbourne.

Frank Farnell, M.P., not satisfied with the
sheaf of honors already won, threw out a sculling chal-
lenge at Drummoyne, which was at once accepted by
Edward Terry, an old Parramatta magnate. Both men
have gone into training. There’ll be money on the row.

» » * »
Norwood Club —Lyons’s team—has won S.A.
cricket premiership. The Norwoods have been top of
the tree oftener than any other club in Australia ; in the
old days they carried everything before them with the
Giffens at the head, and now are top once more without
them. But there is not much in it considering the pre-
sent state of S.A. cricket.

Dear Bulletin,—" Colonial blow ” appears to
be more vigorous and more provincial than ever. Last
week Vic. Chief Justice Madden, re the matter of sending
a rowing team to England, blew as follows—“ Anybody
who chose to stroll along the Yarra bank could see a
body of grenadiers that would have gladdened the heart
of Frederick the Great. It mattered not whether the
Australian eight won or not; it would show the class of
men we had here.” This from a Chief Justice. Why
didn’t he propose to send ’Ome full reports of all legal
proceedings appertaining to bank failures and private
bankruptcies in Melbourne—to show the class of men we
have here ? About the same time that Madden was twirl-
ing his waxy moustache at Melbourne Town Hall the
Maoriland Premier was enjoying a “ blow” in Wellington.
The cable tells us : “ Mr. Seddon said that in his recent
visit to Australia he saw the country at its best, and the
more he saw of it the more he thought of Maoriland
and its people.” Seddon, however, is probably biassed.
Let Maoriland send eight oarsmen ’Ome for comparison
with the Australian eight, and let the voice of Europe
decide which of these two Southern countries has the
finest population in the world.—Yours, Chkapsidb.

London Olympia indoor track is nine laps to
the mile, with two straights and two turns, banked almost
to perpendicularity. Yet. tandems and even triplets can
spin round the corners at a two-min ute bat.
* * * •
Bilmartin intends having a month’s rest in
Queensland, and then making for Italy. “ I’ll put in a
season there,” says Bill, “ an’ then I guess I’ll come back
an’ settle down. The two years I raced in Italy and
France brought me in nearly £-2000—and I only meant
stopping four months in Australia when I came. I think
I’m about done, too ; your winds here plav up kinder
badly with a man on a high gear. There’s no winds to
speak of in Europe—only sorter thin airs, and a bicycle
cuts through them as slick as lightning. I reckon the
best ridin’ I’ve ever done was about the end of last year ”
* * * *
While Vic. Justice Hood smashes himself up
with a bike within three months of buying it, his brother
Williams, J., rides continuously for 15 years without a
fall, and at last fractures his leg mountaineering. By the
way, the Judge's sister, Miss Jessie Williams, was killed
through falling over a precipice in Orbe, Switzerland, 22
years ago, when travelling with Mrs. Bladen Neill,’ of
silkworm celebrity. The news of his daughter’s death
wag conveyed to old Judge “ Bomba ” Williams while he
was in court, and the shock was so great that he fainted
on the bench.

Within the last week in Australia one man aged
73 and another aged 67 have been seriously injured while
riding bicycles.
* * * *
A recent careful investigation of the Vic. Cus-
toms’ returns shows that the invoiced price of the dif-
ferent bicycles imported varies from t'3 to £l2 10s. This
is, of course, less saddles and tires, but even adding the
cost of these accessories at retail price the topmost grade
machine is brought up to only £l7—and the public pays
£33.
* * * *
Bevan, the red headed heir of the wily, woolly,
Melb. doctor of divinity of that ilk, whose “ lepping” at
Oxford has been extensively cabled around the world, is
a very tin-pot long-jumper at the best. At the amateur
sports at St. Kilda, last Nov., Bevan ran a very bad third
to Roseingrave. C. H. Watson, perhaps the best all-
round amateur athlete in Australia, was only a few inches
behind Roseingrave on that occasion, and he and a couple
of other Melb. lads could make a hack of Bevan any day
of the week.

One riderof the successful triplet in the multicycle
race at a recent big meeting was fairly “ dotty ” for 24
hours after the race. Another record-breaker between
two Australian capitals gave his pacers on the home
stretch a heap of bother. He would take long, round-
about routes, for fear of dead trees, which he swore
shudderingly were alive, and would frequently swerve off
a good road into natural earth when a passing breeze dis-
turbed the shadows of the branches overhead. Arriving
at a township at nightfall, he refused to enter, saying
that he would be eaten, and rode round and round' the
cluster of houses until a fall sobered him up and enabled
bitn to be dragged in to rest. Long-distance cycling and

A notion that the cracks would be all there in
the final of Melb. Eight Hours Anniversary Wheel Race was
shattered by the result of the first heat. Porta and
Martin, after catching their field, never recovered from
the effects of the stern chase. The plugger, finishing
fourth, objected to the winner for having bored Porta
against him, but this little misfortune, which was only
one of the chances of the game, didn’t seem to make
much difference to the tired rider, and the protest was
dismissed. Waning William’s real grievance lay in the
fact that he couldn’t go faster than the others when the
pinch came. All ex-champions suffer from the same
complaint.
Megson, assisted by a ssec. slower pace, won
the next heat brilliantly. Then came a masterful per-
formance by Walne and Relph, who soon got on terms
with the sluggish leaders, and were able to work the finish
between them. In the fourth heat, Green and Kellow
were right in the thick of it after going a mile at top
speed, and the scratch man looked to be a certainty for a
place. However, he hadn’t a sprint to bless himself with
at the business end of the journey. The multitude, after
discovering wondrous virtue in the Simpson chain whilst
it was levering Green to the fore, went back to the opinion
that it handicapped the advertiser. Green, who looks fit
and well, must be feeling sorry that he didn’t return
’Ome with his mate, Edwardes. Their expedition had
been chiefly an advt. of biking “form” in this savage
country, and up to now there is no reason why the links
in the chain that bind us to the dear old Mufferland should
be manufactured by Simpson.
\et Britisher Relph, as it happened, was the
only back-mark man to achieve a place in the final. The
first of the semi-goes (won by Body) brought Megson,
W alne and Relph together, and the new-chum alone sur-
vived. Every now and then Relph rides a great race, and
this was one of his happy moments. In the final he
was cut off from humanity, a gap of 35yds. lying
between him and A. A. Middleton, who (going* strictly
on his own) was 30yds. behind the nearest of the
others. By the time the field had got bunched
Relph and Middy, were fairly pumped, and Body
took care that the man from England shouldn’t
give him another severe shaking. The winner started
from 80yds., a decrease of 30yds. upon his handicap in the
A.>.A. event. Barker, who got third money, had to go
100yds. further than on Natives’ Day, when he also
finished third. What a soft thing was’ the League Cud
for Barker aforesaid 1 e
And what a fine show the scratch men were get-
ting at the beginning of the year ! On January 26 Teb-
butt and Barker met Martin and co. upon 170 and 100yds.
better terms than nowadays. Same distance, same track,
Matthews (from the 240yds.) won his heat in the
E.H.A.W.R. by the simple policy of making the pace hot
all the way. Time, 4min. 29 2-ssec. In the semi-final he
got collared and beaten—time, lOsec. slower than before.
A long start is wasted upon these bad judges of pace.
Body was reckoned among the very probable
ones on account of his sturdy pacing in the 50-miles
championship, and the public were glad to see the 200sovs.
captured by a rider who had previously given them some
lively sport. Since the occasion when a bad fall made
him a bruised and sore Body he has displayed an
admirable weakness for working into the front rank and
stopping there as long as possible, out of harm’s way.
One of the heats in the mile foot-race last Satur-
day was amusing at the finish. Three men completed
their journey in very slow time, and then the winner
started to spnnt under the delusion that he had another
lap to go.

the hind wheel will sometimes jerk a piece of road-metal
with much force. A rather serious accident happened in
this .way at South Melb. recently : a stone, flipped bv a
passing bike, struck above the eye a child sitting on the
kerb, nearly destroying the eye, and inflicting a nasty
wound. The writer has already broken one window i£

* • * •
Horse-sense from a Yankee sheet : “ The only
lesson to be learned from the several recent local cycle
accidents is that the rider who, while a-wheel, gives any-
body credit for any intelligence whatever, runs the risk
of an accident every time he does it. The only safe rule
is for the rider to regard all persons except himself, other
riders especially included, as being absolutely incapable
of taking care of themselves, and to look out for them
accordingly.”

Included in a recent contract for fencing on
Coongoola (Q.) is a clause stipulating that a bicycle-track
be cleared along the fence to allow of boundary-riding on
wheels.
* • * *
A trial spin showed the N.S.W. interprovincial
eight-oar crew to be anything but a bad lot, and with a
bit more coaching and rowing together they should prove
really hard nuts to crack. Victoria, seeing that N.S.W.
has picked and put its crew into work, is showing its
hand, and now states that each crew must row under its
own province’s definition, so that, after all, it is not
absolutely certain that the N.S.W. crew will start. J.
Bannister was chosen to represent N.S.W. in the wager-
boat race, but as Victoria has picked upon a cash cyclist
to represent it, he has decided not to compete. The
Gumsuckers have been informed that N.S.W. will only
compete under conference definition of an amateur, and
have been asked for a definite reply.

JACK BROWN, Coorangoora.
• * *
Those enthusiasts who introduced pike into
Maoriland waters some time ago are not quite so elated
over their acclimatisation as formerly. As a fresh water
shark the pike is an excellent institution. He will eat
rata, swans, etc., and attack the heads of horses when
drinking, and the feet or hands of bathers when swim-
ming.
• * « *
A writer in English Field sizes up the ideal
polo-pony as an animal “not over 14.2, combining the
substance of a hunter, the speed of a racehorse over a
short distance, and the docility of the Arab horse.” The
same writer has a high opinion of the few Australian
ponies he has seen, styling them “ superlatively good.”
Apropos of polo ponies, Vincent Dowling, up
near Mudgee, has in his imported Arab “ Snow,” the
champion Indian pony, and “ Arab Chief the prize-
winner of his class at last Sydney show—a pair of polo
pony sires unsurpassed in N.SiW.

Favorite dissipation in N.Q. backblock towns
just now is a female tug-of-war. Of an evening the female
helps of various rival hotels grip hold of a cable and pull
for the honor of their respective establishments. Male
backers are there in abundance, and when one tbam falls
backwards and shows its legs, the mobs bellow iirq'oy un
speakable.
The ice-bicycle has one wheel and a skate.

Archdeacon David (C. of E.) is an active mem-
ber of the tj. Bowing Club. When he turned up at a
special meeting of the association, the other evening, after
a severe illness, he was welcomed with enthusiasm. The
meeting was held at a hotel, too.

A brother of Frank Allan — the bowler of a
century—-is located as bank-manager at Maffra (Gipps-
land). He trundles for the local cricket-club, and the
other day got five wickets for six runs against Stratford.

The select little "‘school” used to gather o’
nights and play devil's pool. There wasn’t a straight ’un
among them, and they all knew it. After an hours play
j "If" w u° hadn’t two marbles at least was a chump,
and though this game was worked very openly there is
but one occasion on record when objection was taken.
One night there were four in the pool, including the
marker. After playing an hour or so the marker
starred and went to the basket for a shake: but
instead of a dozen marbles rattling there were tiro-the
o\\ est, of course. The marker turned an admiring gaze
?" the others, and ejaculated, “By ! you are ahot
• Gome on now, tip em up.” And he went round
collecting the marbles—two from one of them, threefrom
another, and four from the third. Then he produced
three from his own pocket, and a couple from “ cobbers-’
on the sofa, after which there was another shake-up and
the game of ‘ skill ” was resumed.
