The Adlake, a real, good wheel, is sold by
Mr. A. L. Baird, one of the best-known of Aus-
tralian sporting men—known, not because of his
sporting proclivities alone, but because of his well-
recognised mechanical knowledge—Baird’s judging
machine being one of the sporting inventions of
the day. Mr. Baird made a special trip to

N.S.W. played a very poor team, in a represen-
tafcive sense, against Queensland at Brisbane, yet won
easily by an innings and 101 runs. Queensland’s first
innings totalled 180; the second only 73; while the
N.S.W. men piled up 354 with no apparent difficulty. Q.
fielding was very slip-slop, and Pye gave five chances for
his 166. Coningham, Howell, and Newell bowled for the
visitors, getting second innings averages of 3 for 8. 4 for
23, and 3 for 33 respectively.

Cardinal Moran ata recent church function:
“ Of all people I would mistrust it would be a

Edmund Barton was, on the whole, not in
his best mood in Adelaide. Trenwith scored
heaviest off him. Barton had rebuked Carruthers
for suggesting that members, of the Convention
could be guilty of a subterfuge; there were, he
said, 49 members as incapable of such a thing as
Carruthers himself—which was probably about as
far as any man could safely go as to such a matter
in a body of politicians. A week afterwards
Barton opposed the admission of the reporters to
the committees on the ground that members would
talk to the galleries. Using precisely the same
words as Barton had suppressed Carruthers with
Trenwith rebuked the leader for the insult. The
incident was underlined by Barton himself : while
Trenwith still had him under the whip, he turned
his back upon the speaker perhaps because
ashamed of the hollowness of his own rebuke.
“ I was present,” writes Louis Becke, “ at
the great meeting in Hyde Park yesterday (Sun-
day, March 7) to protest against the upholding of
the Turkish bond-holding interests against the
rights of human flesh and blood. It was a curious
and impressive sight. No noise, no confusion,
though there had been but 36 hours’ preparation
for the demonstration which had been forbidden
the use of Trafalgar Square. There was cheering
at the mention 'of Greece and Crete, ominous
silence when the name of Lord Salisbury was
spoken, and then Havelock Wilson, M.P., touched
the soul of the huge gathering by a few words.
‘And Lord Salisbury,’ he concluded, ‘and his
minority get their policy made for them in Ger-
many bv that magnificent young man who thinks
he has been appointed by God Himself to rule the
world—the Emperor Will Before he could
finish the name there burst forth an indescribable
roar of contempt and hatred. The English work-
ing man is no German lover, anyway.”

Many Australian rivers at present are
such only in name. The Lachlan has ceased flow-
ing at Condobolin, the Macquarie has stopped
some distance above Warren, the Namoi has six
inches in it as it rolls by Gunnedah, and the
Castlereagh is as dry as when bushrangers Angel
and Thurston ran along its bed after, shooting
Constable Mitchell and escaping from Coonamble
gaol.
The Argus gushes about Nansen’s daring,
his dash for the Pole, his noble sufferings, his
heroic perseverance, and concludes
This is a story which, as a mere exhibition of human
courage, stirs the British imagination like a vignette from
the great breach at Badajos, or an echo from the thunder
of Trafalgar, or the musketry of Wellington’s stubborn
squares at Waterloo.

he was bom in “ Londing.”
The various Sydney dailies variously inter-
pret the cables. Last week one paper announced
that the Gulf of Martaban, with the N.S.W.
Mounted Rifles, had arrived at Suez. Another
placed her at London on same date. A third was
delightfully vague as to her position, whilst the
fourth located her at Gibraltar.

porary, says the Tobacco Tbade Review, caps
the jokes about things “made in Germany” by

Dear Bulletin, —Somebody tells you
For years past the British navy has declined to follow
deserters. The motto is, that the man who “ jumps ship ”
is not worth keeping. Before that any policeman of civilian
who captured a deserter received ±T, known as “ blood
money. ” If Craddock’s only offence had heen desertion
his name would simply have been taken off the roll. What,
then, was the use of warrant No. 2 ?
This is mostly wrong. Navy deserters are fol-
lowed as rigorously as ever, and every runaway,
from A.B. down to admiral, is worth £3 to the
man who arrests him. The use of warrant No. 2
was simply this, it is a law of extradition that
the extradited person can only be punished for
the offence upon which extradition is granted.
Thus, if Craddock got off on the charge of
embezzlement, and had not been made liable
for desertion, he could not have been made subject
to penalty for the latter offence. Geake’s case
was somewhat similar. He was extradited from
Uruguay on a charge of fraud only, but is now
also serving a sentence for absconding from bail.
Possibly he is smart enough lawyer to discover
u S so ’ ke appeals, the chances are
that the illegal period of imprisonment will have
to be remitted. Similarly, if Butler were acquitted
for the Lee Weller and Preston murders, he could
not be tried for any other crime of which he is
suspected. One thing is true, and to the credit of
the police force, in connection with
deserters—no decent policeman will arrest them—
in spite of the £3 blood-money.—Lex.

A W.A. goldfields pub. Two thirsty men
sitting in front of bar. Big, bulkv six-footer
comes in partly drunk, takes a drink “on his
own,” and sits down between the two stone-
brokers. “11l bet you two drinks,” says one of
the pair, “that he goes forty-five inches round the
chest." Takes his belt off and starts to measure.
The other : “ 111 bet two drinks he goes seventeen
round the calf.” Pulls up his trouser-leg and
starts to measure. The man of means and bulk
shoves the two of them aside and says, “ 111 bet
you two drinks that I don’t shout.” And he
doesn’t. There are many ways of scraping ac-
quaintance with a man who looks good for a drink
but very few of them end in liquid refreshment ’

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfalme Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
6ome seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Ilerbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out' of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders ar—’ve
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

We have just received the following:—“Marra
Creek, Brewarrina, N.S.W., 24th March, 1897.
To the Tasmanian Eucalyptus Oil Co., Hobart.
Dear Sirs, —Enclosed please find a postal note for
3s Will you kindly forward me a bottle
of your ‘ Platypus ’ Eucalyptus Oil. It will,
doubtless, seem curious to you that I should send
over 1150 miles for this, but, as far as my observa-
tion goes, the art of making this commodity pro-
perly has not reached this colony. The stuff which
I have used and met with here, and which goes by
the name of eucalyptus, I can only describe by
the one word, ‘ rubbish.’ Having frequently used
yours and knowing its value is my reason for send-
ing this order to you. Yours truly, .”
“ Platypus ” Eucalyptus Oil, which our customer
refers to, is to be had in Sydney of all chemists,
but purchasers should only buy original bottles
with our trade-mark (a platypus) on the labels.
Messrs. Elliott Bros., Ltd., of O’Connell-street,
Sydney, are the wholesale agents. The Tasmanian
Eucalyptus Oil Company, Limited, Hobart and
London.*

Public Notices.
DUBLIO SERVICE BOARD.
-*• 60 Young-street, Sydney,
9th April, 1897.
The Public Service Board hereby notify that the next
COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION for JUNIOR CLERK-
SHIPS in the PUBLIC SERVICE of the colony will be
held on the 28th, 29th, and 30th days of JUNE' next, at
various centres, which will be fixed to suit the conveni-
ence of candidates. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made during the year is 50. Candidates must
be over sixteen and under twenty-one years of age on the
first day of examination, be of good moral character, in
sound health, and have resided in the colony for at least
three years at the date of application.
Every person who desires to submit himself to examin-
ation must forward to the Secretary to the Board, to
reach him not later than the 7th June, 1897, a statement,
in his own handwriting, giving his full name and address,
and his age, and notifying in which of the principal towns
of the colony he wishes to be examined. He must also
submit, with his application, an extract from the register
in which his birth is recorded, or other satisfactory evi-
dence of his age ; a certificate to the effect that he is of
good moral character, signed by his teacher, if he is at-
tending school, or has ceased to attend school for a shorter
period than six months ; by a clergyman or Justice of the
Peace or other responsible person if it is more than six
months since he left school; and a certificate from a repu-
table person to the effect that the applicant has resided
in the colony for at least three years.
A list of registered candidates will be advertised, with
the times and places of examination ; and, in addition,
every candidate registered will be personally notified of the
time and place when and where he must present himself.
Every candidate whose name is not registered by the
Board will be so informed, with the reason why registra-
tion is refused.
The subjects in which candidates will be examined are
—Handwriting, English (dictation, grammar, and com-
position), Arithmetic (full course), Geometry (Euclid,
First Book), Outlines of Physical and Political Geography,
and the Geography of the Australasian Colonies in
moderate detail, English History from the middle of the
Eighteenth Century to the present time, especially with
reference to the colonies and dependencies. Candidates
will also be allowed to take any two, but not more than
two, of the following subjects .-—Mathematics, English—a
prescribed author—(the same as that prescribed for
the Junior Examination of the current year), Latin,
French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and Elementary
Geology. Every candidate who wishes to be examined in
the extra subjects must give notice of his desire in his
application.
The fee for examination will be 10s., which must be paid
to a Clerk of Petty Sessions or into the Treasury, and the
receipt lodged with the Secretary of the Board 10 days
before the examination. Every successful candidate must
produce a medical certificate to the effect that he is in
sound health, and free from physical defects, before he will
be allowed to enter upon his duties.
The regulations governing the examinations appeared
in a supplement to the Government Gazette dated Ist
April, 1897.
By order of the Board,
J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

The officers of a Union Co.’s steamer got a
shock while between Lyttelton and Wellington
recently. When on the look-out for Kaikoura
Peninsula, they suddenly descried a light to star-
board—where there should have been open sea.
Soon innumerable other lights appeared, and it
seemed as if the ship was running into a good-
sized town. Under the circumstances the amazed
officers decided to go full speed astern, but by
some occult agency they did not get away from
the lights—the town was apparently chasing them.
Some of the passengers hinted at an “ optical
delusion ” or “ sea phantasy ” and the like, until it
suddenly dawned on the skipper that a squadron
of warships was the cause of the eerie and uncer-
tain sight. So it proved. Admiral Bridge and
co. were on their way to a northern coastal town.
Coincident advts. from English Clarion :
ME. FLURSCHEIM’S LECTURES on “ Co-opera-
tive Exchange Bank Notes.” Conditions : Mr.
Flurscheim pays his own railway and hotel expenses ;
society engaging his services pays for advertising
meeting and hire of hall. Application for dates to be
made to A. Brittlebank, Organising Sec., 40 Hounds
Gate, Nottingham.
T ECTURES on COSME COLONY by W. LANE, in
London or South-East England, between March
13th and 25th (week nights), may be arranged.—A.
Tozer, 220 Goswell-road London, E.C.
Brittlebank for Flurscheim and Tozer (not Horace)
for Lane. Thus the two men who first astonished
the Guaranis by introducing British fisticuffs to
the Paraguayan backblocks meet again in
the (advertising) columns of a Socialist paper.
Brittlebank (the writer presumes) is the
N.S. Wales single-taxer who, four years ago,
went to Paraguay, and didn’t like it. In a
truly communal spirit, he plugged young Tozer.
Tozer, a New Australian who has never been
in Old Australia, retaliated with one
on the Single-taxer’s countenance, and, in a spirit
of true mateship, levelled an acre or two of
“ monte ” scrub with the belligerent brother’s car-
case. Then Britt, went to England and wrote
violent anti-Lane letters to the papers, and re-
marked that socialism, of the Tozer brand, any-
way, wasn’t what it was cracked up to be. Tozer
helped Lane to found the Cosme settlement, and
now figures in England as It. S. Smythe for Don
William. And the first man he sees glaring at
him over a metal-rule, is his ancient antagonist,
Brittlebank! So whirls the whirligig. Writer
suggests a conference between the rival showmen
in the interests of Liberty, Equality and Fra-
ternity, and to prevent the undue warping of
Blatchford’s metal-rule.

The other day a young man, acting as
third assistant to the cook in a Sandringham
(Vic.) coffee house, was sent a scrap of newspaper
by one of the waitresses, who had found it
wrapped around the ham or something. The
paper contained an advt., intimating that certain
lawyers desired to meet with a person of the same
name as the cook’s assistant. A lump sum was
waiting the pleasure of that cook’s assistant in
Sydney, and now he is off ’Ome to take possession
of £17,000 a year and a palatial residence in
London. Which shows that the devil is indeed
good to cooks.

A couple of Chinese cabinet makers were
fined for evasions of the Factories Act in Melb.
the other day (working after hours), and the
Argus headed its report “Work or Starvation,”
and pointed out that the poor pagan had to toil at
all possible hours or perish of hunger; therefore,
the Factories Act is a tyrannous and inhuman
piece of legislation. It is useless pointing out to
the Argus that if the Chow is allowed to work at
all hours, his white rivals must perish of starva-
tion, or take to burglary, or garroting, or some
other industry in which they are not likely to suffer
from Chinese competition. Argus’s charity for
the Chow is the charity that dissembles a multi-
tude of conservative sins.

There has been trouble amongst the
soldiery of Ballarat, where the native man of
blood and iron has fierce and flaring tastes in the
matter of clothes. It became known at head-
quarters that unless the bold militiaman at this
town was given something more startling than
khakee wherewith to drape his figure, he would
retire from the army in disgust. There were
pleadings, and protests, and negotiations, but the
soldier triumphed, and Government is having a
large quantity of scarlet cloth, as flaring as measles,
manufactured, and presently the garden city will
be painted red by a fresh outbreak of local soldiery
clad in the color of a smoky sunset, and picked
out with slabs and splashes of glittering gilt. A
horrible fatality amongst nursemaids will speedily
follow. When one thinks of the tremendous
expense to which a Government is put to enable a
vainglorious young cobbler to mash the boss-eyed
girl at the store round the corner, one can fully
appreciate the honest efforts of the Peace Society.
Extract from document written by Sydney
Cingalese who lately tired of scraping the dirty
edge off existence: —
“ Notic to every poor people. How the Goverment do
to poors; also how to pass missrable life. Dear Sir.
Since the year our lord one thousand eigt hundred and 82
I was left my nativ home of Ceylon and came to Australia
to earn my living without robbing or frauding, etc., only
by working, hut I find it was failur up to to-day; I have
been earn by working a few years back and start on busi-
ness. That was failure as well, and went broke. There I
had to go on the track again to work. There I made a
bit, also start a farm on Tweed River in N.S.W. I was
there until 1893 with another person name . When
1 was doing farming I got an attack of rhumatic because
I was working ‘ wet, and dry to earn my daily bread.
Through that been laid up had go to hospital at Brisbane.
There was for some time and pain releaf no of. Then
return home, and as I colled the farm. After I was
arrived to farm I was told by mates one day our landlord
came to us and asked us to give our crop of sugar-cane to
crush by him, because there was rent due to him ”
And much more to the same effect. This is the
history of Australian land-development. Fail in
business, “ go on the land ” without much know-
ledge of it, incur rheumatics or something equiva-
lent, get back with the rent, and then it is either
Hell or the Bogan scrub. That is the common
way of helping to build the Great Australian
Damnation.

Boys of 11 or 12 years go round with the
dust-carts in several Sydney suburbs. As the
rubbish is tipped into the carts they sort it out
and throw articles of possible value into a separate
heap. The law, which is so beneficent to tender-
aged stage children, might also have regard for
those youngsters whose bodies must make com-
fortable nests for the multifarious germs that
haunt suburban dirt-boxes.
“The medical brotherhood of Britain
is a close guild,” writes The Bulletin’s Lon-
don correspondent. “No foreigner (Australian
included therein) need apply for any position, no
matter how high his credentials. Yet Playfair,
the King’s Hospital gynaecologist and discoverer
of Dorothea Baird ‘ Trilby ’ —he whose wife’s confi-
dential whispers were prescribed for in a famous
libel-case of late; the prescription was a .£lOOO
draft, but it was not taken or offered—plainly
avows he got his appointment as a specialist in
obstetrics when he was absolutely ignorant of that
wide subject. To-day, certainly, few are his
superiors, but the hospital and asylum appoint-
ments are now cut and dried before advertising
in accordance with the regulations. In one par-
ticular case, recently, an Australian was refused
because his degrees and testimonials were ‘ too
good,’ and the local butcher’s son with a low-class
degree got the job. The scalpel and the cleaver.”
The hominy ’bus—Black Maria.
Viator : “ What a blather about the diffi-
culty of managing the Australian railways in
block. I class it much about the same as the job
of the Atchison, Topeka and Santa Be, or the
English Midland.”

In the Grey of the Morning .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The glamor the dull present throws over the dis-
tant past, in which eyes were brighter, smiles
sweeter, and lips more dewy ! Here, in the small
hours, when a long white glove lies forgotten on
the floor and a broken fan lies among faded
flowers and soiled glasses ; when the heat of the
wine has vanished, leaving regrets and memory
for company ; a line of bygone faces file silently
round the table more real than the noisy, reckless
flesh and blood so shortly preceding them. How
charming they were, those girls one did not
marry ; how dainty their ways, how neat their
waists and their ankles, how warm their kisses !
What good fellows were those chums of the old
days ! How sweet was love, how true was friend-
ship ! The shy look that set one’s blood tingling,
the hearty hand-grip ! To youth the gods are
lavish.
There was a day, dear love, that we spent by the
sea, with the south breeze stirring your hair ; and
your hand in mine, when the summer moon came
up, round and full, and met the evening star in the
still southern ocean, making a wide shimmering
pathway to the west, where, under a line of rosy
clouds, a white-sailed boat drifted slowly. There
was the scent of creamy ti-tree flowers, the kiss of
tiny waves on the sand below. The flash of swift
gulls, and the twitter of birds in their nests near
by. For the rest, you were there, giving, for that
one day at least, the meaning of the earth and sea
and sky, and interpreting the sweetest love lyric
in your shy eyes.
O, days that were ours ! dreams that were ours .
Ambitions dead or realised, what matter ? Who
climbs a high peak but sees from thence a higher ?
The mile stones of life are marked failure and
folly, and he who passes one marked success may-
be has the more furrowed brow, the more lagging
step, and he reads the letters with dim, listless
eyes.
The young cry: “See what the gods have
given ! ” and they point to the great grey piles of
Dead Sea apples, seeing neither the greyness nor
the ashes —only ripe, red fruit sufficient for all
desire.
All will so soon be over and done with, and one
returns to the cool, old earth, to lie unconscious of
the pulsing life above ; knowing neither love nor
sorrow nor sin. To lie where, by stretching out
one’s hands to either side, one could touch the
brave men and sweet women who went before and
come after.
Will it be a quick, fierce death, life clinging for one
cm»l instant to the warm body she has nourished
--cl—gUig like a bride to her groom, crying to the
hot pulse, the strong muscle and sinew ; and then
torn away to perish in the parting ? However it
be matters little, it is so soon over.
The ghostly company have flitted from my
table ; there is nothing but the empty glasses, the
disorder, and the flickering candles. The glove
lies where it fell. Let’s to bed, to wake again pre-
sently to the workaday world, in which To-day is
of paramount importance.
S.O.S.

An Idyll of the Ring.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The combatants were known, and Victoria Hall
was uneasy with the impatience of a Saturday
night audience. In a dressing-room under the
stage ; Monty Hammond was being rubbed down
by his seconds. Monty was anxious ; at every
volley of heel-stamps and shrill whistles he en-
quired : “ Hasn’t that bloomin’ Moonlight turned
up yet? ” The answer was invariably in the nega-
tive. Presently, a tiny man, possessing the
pressed-down nose peculiar to pugilists, came in,
announcing an injury to Moonlight’s hand, and
said there was “ no ’ope of ’im turnin’ up.”
“ There’ll be no fight ! ” growled a second.
Monty Hammond's face grew black.
“ Carn’t yer move round and get some bloke to
take me on instead ? ” he suggested.
The second went to move round. He returned
shortly: “ There ain’t no blokes in the’all as is
willin’ an’ yer weight. ’Arry Thompson ses ’e
don’t mind fillin’ in, but ’e’s three stun ’eavierthan
you an’ knows more.”
Asked Monty : “ But will ’e split the ’ouse ? ”
“No; ’e wants 75 per cent, to go to the winner,
cos ’e knows ’e can beat yer ; so it ain’t no good
yer takin’ on a bloomin’ ’iding fur a couple o’quid.
I’ll tell ’im you ain’t taking any.”
“ Don’t tell ’im that: tell ’im I’ll ’ave a cut,”
said Monty, doggedly.
“ But ”
“ Go on, carn’t yer—tell ’im ! ”
Half an-hour afterwards, Monty was helped
back, having received great punishment and a
“ knock out ”in six rounds. A few sympathising
friends accompanied him, as well as his garrulous
second, who eased his soul with talk beginning:
“ Didn’t I tell yer yer ’ad no ’ope ” and ending in
curses for a fool. Monty was sphinx-like in his
silence until the entrance of the “gentleman” who
had attended his interest at the “ gate.”
“ ’Ow much in the ’ouse ? ”
“ Twelve sixteen, after exs.”
“ Wot’s coinin’ to me ? ”
“ Three pun four.”
“ There, I told yer so,” interposed the second,
disgustedly. “ A bloomin’ idin’ an’ a smack agin
yer rep. for 64 blanky bob. Gawd, yer was
balmy ! ”
“ Stow yer lip,” said Monty, savagely, “ ain’t I
got a bloomin’ missus an’ a kid at ’ome ! Wot
else could a blanky fam’ly man do?”
H. A. CONANT.
The Labor Question —“Any show for a
job, boss ? ”
A London illustrated paper, just to hand,
drags out half-a-dozen instruments of torture used
to-day in the Anglican Church, a hair shirt being
the mildest.
“ She said somedings about somedings, but
vat dat somedings vas or vat she vas say aboudt
it I vas not know.” It took 25 minutes to extract
this evidence from a small, fat, bewildered Ger-
man one day recently in Masterton (M.L.) magis-
trate’s court, and the bench was in such a bad
temper for the rest of the day that it was con-
stantly on the verge of giving the whole town six
months without the option.
The Crow and the Crane: an
Aboriginal Legend.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Crane was a great fisherman. He used to
hunt the fish out with his feet from underneath
the logs in the creek, and so catch numbers.
One day, when he had a great many on the bank
of the river, a Crow, who was white then, came up.
He asked the Crane to give him some fish. “Wait
a while,” said the Crane, “ until they are cooked.”
But the Crow was hungry and impatient; he would
not cease bothering the Crane, who kept saying
“ wait, wait.” Presently the Crane turned his
back ; the Crow sneaked up and was just going to
steal one. The Crane turned round, saw him,
seized a fish, and hit the Crow right across the
eyes with it.
The Crow felt blinded for a few minutes. He
fell on the burnt black grass round the fire, and
rolled over and over in his pain. When he got up
to go away his eyes were white and the rest of
him black, as the crows have been ever since.
The Crow was determined to pay out the Crane
for having given him white eyes and a black skin.
He watched his chance. One day he saw the
Crane fast asleep. He crept quietly up to him
holding a fish-bone. This he stuck right across
the root of the Crane’s tongue. Then he went off
as quietly as he had come, careful, for once, not to
make a sound.
The Crane woke up at last, and when he opened
his mouth to yawn he felt choking. He tried to
get the obstruction out of his throat; in the effort
he made a queer scraping noise, which was all he
could give utterance to. The bone stuck fast.
And to this day the only noise a crane can make
is gah-rah-gah, gay-rah-gah, thus giving the name
by which he is known to the blacks.
K. LANGLOH PARKER.
The S.A. bootmakers’ strike is mainly
owing to starvation wages following on the
machinery system and cut-throat trade competi-
tion. Some of the piece-workers have been
averaging under 10s. a week for months past.
A resident of Greendale (Vic.), has a
grievance against the trustees of the local ceme-
tery. He bought a small allotment in Green-
dale bone-yard, on the supposition that he was
securing a last resting-place in a truly Christian
district, where he might sleep the sleep of the
righteous undisturbed by doubts concerning his
neighbors’ respectability. He had hoped that
when the Last Trump awoke him he would be
quite safe in formally introducing himself to the
occupants of the adjoining graves, with a view to
getting included among their little “ set,” so that
he could have the pleasure of passing them into
the Kingdom as personal friends, after giving
their names to the angel at the gate. And now
it seems that the tenant of the ground next
door to his property is an alleged suicide, in
whose company the great man would appear to
great disadvantage in St. Peter’s eagle eye.
Therefore, he desires the authorities to eject
this disreputable dead ’un, otherwise he will be
compelled to forfeit his freehold and buy another
plot in a more select corner of the cemetery.
Dogma has its discomforts.
To Sydney’s Champion Newspaper
Sore.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My dear Mr. Knapp, you make a mistake
If you really are pleased to suppose
That the country’s alight with the letters you
write—
We could all do the same if we chose.
From Solomon down we may read as we run
Of the ways of a man and a maid,
There is nothing that’s new to us under the sun,
And certainly not in the shade.
The Jingo remarks that you fiddle aloud
Are as vulgar as coin from the Mint;
And you merely distinguish yourself from the
crowd
By the fact that you get them in print.
You’re a ’prentice, my boy, in the primitive stage
And you itch, like a boy, to confess ;
When you know a bit more of the arts of the age
You will probably write a lot less.
For your wearisome clap-trap we don’t care a fig,
It is this that we deeply deplore ;
You were cast for a common or usual pig,
But you play the invincible “ bore.”

From London Labor Chronicle :
A London stockbroker of our acquaintance, talking
over these matters a few weeks ago, expressed surprise
that Labor leaders had not made use of the Stock Ex-
change in fighting employers. Why should not a Trade
Union, contemplating a strike, sell the shares of the com-
pany before announcing the strike ? The fall in price as
soon as the news is spread abroad would enable the Trade
Union to buy at a profit, which would place them in funds
for strike-pay for many months. It was reckoned that
the fall in North-Westerns amounted to four millions. A
slice of this would have been a welcome addition to the
£lBO,OOO which the Amalgamated Society of Railway Ser-
vants was said to have had at its back. There is, of
course, a difficulty in the way of Trade Unions dealing in
stocks and shares with their funds, but there is nothing
to hinder the striking of a bargain with some enterprising
stock-jobber. The Union might supply exclusive infor-
mation of their intention for a lump sum cash down.
Dear Bulletin, —Splendid fellow as San-
dow is, I think he opens his mouth too wide in
calling himself the strongest man in the world.
Dr. Winship, of Boston, may be dead now, but he
was certainly very much alive when he lifted
30001b.—7601b. over the ton. Done with straps and
careful weight-distribution, certainly, but still an
astounding feat for an ordinary-sized man. Also
Whistler, the American wrestler, carried drunk
—a 13cwt. iron girder upon his shoulder for 100
yards. Also Harrison —many years ago “ muscle-
model ” at the Royal Academy, and rather a small
man—could twine two heavy horse-shoes round
each other. These two latter men are dead, but
it’s hardly to be doubted that others like them are
in the flesh. The world may know nothing of its
strongest men—as well as of its greatest.—Del-
toid.

A Pensioner’s Plight.
CHRONIC DIARRH(EA, RHEUMATISM, NEURALGIA
DYSPEPSIA, CURVATURE OF THE SPINE.
WHAT PINK PILLS DID IN THE WAY OF CURE.
(From the “Times,” Kansas City, Mo.)
Robert H. Hunter is an old veteran of Arlington, in the terri-
tory of Oklahoma, who, after 33 years of helpless suffering, is now
so far rejuvenated that he has taken up a quarter section of land
“to grow up with the country,” as he says.
In 1863 Mr. Hunter was serving at the front in Co. G., Ist
Mo. Cavalry, and while on a long forced march was ten days and
nights in the saddle during very rainy weather. He was, shortly
/
; />
after getting into camp,
from the exposure he
had gone through, taken
with such severe cramp-
ings and swellings of
the limbs that it was at
first surmised that he
had been bitten by a
rattler or copperhead.
The regimental sur-
geon was not of that
opinion, but diagnosed
it as a case of saddle
cramp and acute rheu-
matism of a very severe
type. Here is the old
soldier’s own story:
“The regimental sur-
geon informed my cap-
tain that I would not be able for some time, if ever, to ride my
horse, so I was appointed commissary sergeant of the company, in
which capacity I served until September, 1864, when from lack of
exercise, owing to my crippled condition, I was taken with
chronic diarrhoea. Neuralgia, dyspepsia, and curvature of the
spine soon added their presence to my weight of woe, and at my
discharge I was a pretty badly used-up man. Of course, I was
obliged to undergo medical treatment, and had numerous physi-
cians who attempted to give me relief without success, and as the
years rolled on hope grew dimmer until I saw nothing that would
assuage my suffering but the grave.
“ Thus I dragged on a miserable existence until 1890, when, to
add to my misery, I caught influenza, and when that left me all
my diseases were emphasised, and my lungs became affected. I
was not able to walk any distance, and when I came to Oklahoma
in 1893 was too ill and weak and helpless to look at the quarter
section I had taken up.
“ In August, 1893, I read in a newspaper that Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People had cured the effects of la grippe in a
great many cases, so I thought it could do me no harm to try
them, and I did.
“ Before I had finished the first box my cough was cured, and
all pulmonary symptoms had disappeared, and my general health
became better, especially my appetite and digestion. My heart,
which had been irregular, missing about every third beat, resumed
normal action, and my liver and kidneys began once more to do
the labors for which they were intended. I continued to take the
pills, and soon my right arm, which had grown useless, and my
hand so nerveless that I could not grasp a pen, became much
better, and eventually well. I can now use my right arm, hand,
and shoulder as well as ever. I can chop wood, and often walk 10
or 20 miles, and can plough a whole day.”
‘' e looks altogether different to me, thanks to Dr. Williams’
Dink Pills, and with my pension I hope to be able from my labors
to lay up a little money. As I had entirely given up all hope of
health, it may be imagined how happy I am, and how changed
the world looks. I have offered to pay for Pink Pills for others
who cannot afford to buy them, as a debt of gratitude that I can
thus partially pay, and I try to let everybody know what the
remedy has done for me.
t i j r m npt able express my changed feelings, nor could lif
, l , had a far higher command of language than I now have. I hope
that this testimonial may be the means of others knowing that
there is a relief for them in Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People.
(Signed) “ ROBERT H. HUNTER.
Counterfeiters at Work.
HEALTH AND LIFE ENDANGERED BY UNSCRUPU-
LOUS DEALERS, WHO PERSUADE UNSUSPECTING
PEOPLE TO TAKE IMITATIONS—SOME POINTERS
WORTH REMEMBERING.
No medicine that is not of more than ordinary merit suffers
from imitations or substitutes. The fact that an imitation is
offered is one of the strongest proofs of the excellent qualities of
the genuine article. The Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. is con-
y im P ress upon the public the fact that Dr.
Williams Pink Pills are sold in Australia only in wooden boxes
the wrapper around which is printed in red ink and bears the
registered trade mark “ Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
Notwithstanding this constant warning, there are unscrupulous
dealers here and there who defraud the public by selling an imita-
tion pill (also colored pink) either by the dozen, hundred, or bv
the ounce, alleging that they are “just as good,” or “just the
same as the genuine Dr. Williams’Pink Pills. This is a false-
■l the unscru P u l° us dealer who offers the imitation knows
it, but is more concerned for the extra profit he makes on the
im \irii lo1 L ™ an or th® health of his unfortunate victim.
Will the public, in their own interest, bear in mind the following
facts;—Dr. Williams Pink Pills are “never’’sold by the dozen
hundred, or ounce. If any dealer offers you a pill in this form (no
should bLavofde'd ° red Pmk ° r n heis trying to cheat you > and
The formula of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills is a secret, and is
known only to the company. Therefore, if some dealer tells you

The Master.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In sea and air, in leaf and stone,
Where’er Truth’s magic words are writ,
Where thousands throng, or rapt and lone,
Life was his book. He pondered it.
Through page of earth and sun and star
He heard the swift sweep of the Song
Of Law and Motion, streaming far,
Which seemed to sing, “ The world is wrong. ”
Men passed him by. They brushed aside
The dreamer in his dreams of law.
They laughed and left him, open-eyed
Fixed on the point that no man saw.
“ Life is to scheme, to love, to play.
Strike out for plunder in the fight !
Our fathers did so,” answered they,
“And they have said, ‘The world is right. ”•
Men took the baubles at the call,
Men rose to wealth and power and state,
But he —the mightiest of them all—
Remained, because his heart was great.
The fool cried : “ Life is but a jest,”
The schemer : “ Earth is for the strong,”
The thoughtless : “ Why think for the rest ? ”
But he cried : “ Nay. The world is wrong.”
And friends declined, and fortune frowne.d,
And hope grew dim with health’s decay,
The thorns of hardship hedged him round,
But still he toiled from day to day—
From clue to clue, from year to year,
From law to law, from light to light,
Till came the triumph flashing clear—
“ The world is wrong and I am right 1 ”
“ The Laws of Life, eternal, true,
Swerve not by prayer for that or this.
A Power hath given the world to you
And ye have made it what it is.
Why see worth perish, want accrue ?
Why see greed flourish day by day ?
A Power hath given the world to you,
And ye can make it what ye may.”
No mourners wept beside his bier,
Scant homage to his grave was brought.
He left no wealth behind him here,
Only the splendor of his thought.
And as the brown earth, heaping slow,
Shut the rude coffin from the sight,
One who had known him long ago
Cried: “ After all, the world was right! ”
But Thought is king no clowns can bind,
And Genius, in its crowning hour,
Sows deep the seed that, for Mankind,
Springs, centuries hence, to splendid flower,
When, by that lonely stone of white,
With heads uncovered, men shall say :
“ The world was wrong and He was right
Who died for what we reap to-day ! ”
GEO. ESSEX. EVANS.

Counting up to Thirty.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One evening about two years ago I was knocking
the balls about an American pool-table in the
“Solid Silver ” saloon, Denver city, and chatting
at the same time to Sheriff—generally known as
J udge—Benham.
“ Yes,” he said in answer to a question of mine,
“ there’s still a bit of shooting done here at times ;
though I’m thankful to say that it’s getting rare.
If you stop here long and frequent this sort of
place you may drop across an incident in that
line ; and if you’ll take my advice, whenever you
see it coming on, just light out for all you’re
worth, for the contending parties are seldom shot,
it is generally an outsider that pays for the fun.”
We were separated from the bar by a screen,
but could distinctly hear anything that was going
on there, and soon after the Judge finished speak-
ing a rasping Eastern voice full of twang de-
manded a whisky cocktail—“and make it up
smart, my man.” The Judge nudged me and
whispered, “ He’s into the establishment for a
long score, and has done two or three ruffianly
things here, and the bar-tender has promised to
pitch him out. Let’s see.”
So we sauntered from behind the screen, the
Judge handing me a match for my cigar and in-
viting me to drink. We breasted the bar as the
tender thereof handed to him who was expecting
an addition to his many free drinks a long bill
with the words, “ I’ll see the color of your money
and hear you speak civilly, you loafing dog, before
you drink here any more.”
The whisky-desirer opened his mouth and
dropped a few choice Yankee swears, which
seemed to hang in tha*smoke-charged atmosphere
and befoul the place. Then he was looking down
the barrel of the bar-tender’s “ long Colt.” “ Get
out of this, !” etc., etc., hissed the bar-tender,
who, it appeared, could swear too.
The loafer had time to leave behind him the
memory of a hideous look of fear and baffled rage ;
then he was out of the door like a hunted cat.
The bar-tender replaced the Colt on a handy shelf
behind him, served our drinks, and went about
his business.
* * * *
“I opine,” said the Judge, “that the curtain
will ring up for Act 11. of that little play before
long. “You and I will sit right here, play a
game of dominoes, and wait.” The Judge, it
seemed, had the common failing of advice-givers.
I knew him for a man who dispensed justice not
in name only, but in very truth ; and wondered
what scheme he had in his eye.
One by one the habitues dropped in, chatted,
and partook. We were seated at a small, round
table close to the bar, playing with the dominoes.
When there were eight men in the room, and the
bar-tender was busy concocting a drink, the door
swung back with a bang, as a kicked door does,
and, with an overcoat on and his hands in the side
pockets, the Easterner appeared on the scene. All
who were sitting stood up. The man behind the
bar went any color, and half-turned to his shelf.
‘ ‘ Better not, I guess ; or you’re as dead as yar
look, yar sneak,” snarled the man who now held
the situation ; and out flashed a pistol in each
hand.
“ Now, gentlemen,” he began, but without ever
taking his eyes off his victim, who, in his turn,
was watching him for a second’s wavering, “ I’m
going” (he drawled horribly) “ to shoot that, etc.,
etc., but if anybody interferes I’ll drop him first.”
He spoke as if about to try a conjuring trick, and
he were just asking for a handkerchief or a watch
from one of the audience. “ Guess we’ll play the
fish a bit,” he said ; his face expressing a devilish
delight such as I have never seen equalled by a
melodramatic villain ; and he walked right up to
and faced the bartender. “Now you, etc., etc.,
you cahn’t see the clock because it’s behind with
your shooter, but you’ve got just 30 seconds to
breathe, so that if you’ve got anything to say,
you’d better talk, I guess.”
* * * *
The man of drinks had pulled himself together,
and was as game as a pebble. He never opened
his mouth, nor did it tremble, and his eyes never
wavered from those of his enemy. I knew as
well as if he had said it out loud that he was
counting up to 30 himself, and that he would have
some sort of a “go ” for his life in the last five. I
was counting, and I believe every man in the room
also. I stared and counted ; then counted and
stared. In a hideous sort of way it was fascinating
me, while I tried to think what I should do. I
wasn’t going to stand by and see an innocent
man murdered without making an effort to save
him; yet if I moved I should most certainly be
“dropped” before I could do any good. Of
course, my “dropping” might give the bar-
tender a chance ; but was there any reason why I
should offer myself up as a leaded sacrifice for a
man to whom I owed nothing—not even a score
for drinks ? So I went on staring and counting—
perfectly resolved to do something before the half-
minute was up, but, for the life of me, I couldn’t
think what.
I had clean forgotten all about the Judge, who
was standing kind of half-behind me and to the
right rear of the brute with the pistols, distant
from him about 12 feet. “ Twenty ! ” drawled
the Easterner, “ nothing to tell us, you etc., etc.,
eh?” The Judge crouched down; the murderer
mistook the movement, thinking he was going to
sit, and took off the corner of his eye that had
noted the motion. . . .
It was a splendid standing jump for a heavy
man in no training, and I could not suppress
something between a cheer and a relieved sob as
13 stone of athletic judgeship hurtled through the
air and literally fell upon the man, bringing him
with a crash to the ground. They rose as one
man, chiefly Judge, the arms of the shooting
scoundrel pinned tight to his sides.
» * » *
Act 111. followed naturally enough. The bar-
tender wheeled to his shelf, and his hand, un-
steadied by the awful seconds through which he
had just passed, made it excessively uncertain
whether he would pot his enemy or his preserver,
for he evidently meant business. “ Don’t shoot,
George ! don’t shoot, man ! ” cried the Judge, with
a clear ring of command that seemed to come
natural to him now that he had taken the floor in
earnest. “Just leave him to me and he’ll be no
more bother here,” and he picked up the defeated
shootist, pistols and all, and carried him out into
the street, telling us not to follow him.
Nobody did follow him, and what species of
justice it was that the Western Judge dealt out to
the Eastern loafer has ever since been shrouded in
mystery. All that we inside heard was a double
clang as the two pistols dropped on the sidewalk,
followed in two or three minutes by that sort of
squelching thud made by a powerfully-urged boot
helping humanity on its way.
J. JINGLE.
Dear Bulletin, —The Chinese must go 1 I
am in a solicitor’s office. A week or two ago a
Chinaman came in to see my boss. He wanted to
go through the Bankruptcy Court. The boss
explained that it was necessary to find some
money for that purpose. “Ah!” said John
“ can’t me go through court in form paupery ? ”
The boss fainted.— R. R.
Recent exposures in Vic. Postal Depart-
ment were all engineered by “Dick” Hain, son of
the late election-agent, Hain, of Ballarat. Hain
had a two-year-old charge brought against himself,
involving a technical alleged offence regarding
private moneys officially in his keeping. The
Board of Inquiry very willingly punished a man
who sheeted home serious charges against officials
in high places. Hain resigned and is now attached
to the Age staff.
A novel form of University life is being
seriously talked about in Queensland educational
circles. For a quarter of a century Queensland
has been thinking of establishing a University
but till the present has got no further than the
extension lectures provided by Sydney. The new
proposal comes to this : Young men who go in for
these extension lectures will, on their own
account, rent a house or houses in Brisbane where
they will “ batch,” or live in common. The idea
is feasible, and will have the advantage of keeping
the students together. At present they are scat-
tered all over the town.
X.Z. : “Noted that the next big shekel'
attractor in London is to be a huge model of the
earth, showing mountains, rivers, lakes, oceans,
everything, on a scale of one half-millionth of a
mile to the mile. On this scale London would
show as a little bigger than a penny, Melbourne
and Sydney about the size of a sixpence ; all the
other Australian capitals would range from the size
of the head of a tin-tack to that of the head of a
pin. After such an illustration, the sudden pain-
ful diminution in the size of the swelled head of
the Little Boy at Manly and the extensiveness of
the j awfulness and general dough-rise in Jawbone
Neild’s head are left to conjecture.”
Dear Bulletin,—l have lost all faith in
the sincerity of females who shudder at the sight
of a black beetle. The other morning I stood
around in the operating-room of Oldfield’s Dental
College-hospital, whilst a calm, unmoved woman-
doctor was administering chloroform to a woman-
patient. When the figure on the table was “nicely
off,” a young damsel, one of the pupils at the col-
lege, took part with the principal in a cheerful
extraction of 20 stumps from the patient’s gums,
whilst a natty nurse mopped up the flowing gore
with a sponge. The lady pupil, smiling breezily
every time she captured a stump, was very sug-
gestive of Little Jack Horner pulling plums from
a pie. The nurse, also, revelled in her portion of
the business, whereas the lady-doctor seemed only
moderately happy, she not being able to dabble in
the berlud. Timid little creatures are women !
Melbourne.
